Dialectic -- Jadal

The Objection: Attaching to a Different Word
Author defines and illustrates the objection type where each party attaches to a different word of the evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when an objector and arguer are each attaching to different words of the same evidence in a jadal exchange.

Core Idea
Each side picks a different word; the dispute is over which word governs the meaning.

Disputants often appeal to the same text but focus on different parts. This lesson breaks down how to spot when each party is clinging to a specific word, and why that matters for resolving the debate. We will use the classic Hanafi-Maliki example on whether a Muslim can be killed in retribution for a disbeliever.

Guided Path
1
Identify the evidence and the two parties
Find the single textual proof (the hadith) and the two disputants (Malikite arguer, Hanafite objector). Note which party is presenting the evidence and which is objecting.
Here the Malikite introduces the hadith 'A Muslim is not killed for a disbeliever.'
2
Locate the word each party attaches to
The Malikite attaches to the word 'disbeliever' (كافر) in the first part; the Hanafite attaches to 'covenant-holder' (ذو عهد) in the second part. Mark these words in the text.
The objector uses a different part of the same hadith to challenge the arguer's interpretation.
3
Understand how the objector uses his word to restrict the arguer's word
The Hanafite argues that 'covenant-holder' shows the intended 'disbeliever' is only the combatant, because the covenant-holder is excluded from being killed by a combatant. This restricts the scope of the first part.
The objector claims consistency: the end of the hadith (exception) must match the beginning.
4
Distinguish this objection from other objections to evidence
Compare to other types: here both sides accept the same text but disagree on which part carries the ruling. It is not a challenge to authenticity or relevance, but to interpretation based on wording.
This type is called 'ta'alluq bi-lafz' – clinging to a word.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أ - 2 - أن يتعلق كل منهما بلفظ من الدليل غير اللفظ الذي ذهب إليه الآخر : ومثاله : أن يستدل المالكي على أن المسلم لا يقتل بالكافر قصاصا، بما روي عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أنه قال : (لا يقتل مسلم بكافر) (1) ، وهذا نص في موضع الخلاف، فيقول الحنفي معترضا : المراد بالكافر في الحديث : الحربي، بدليل قوله في آخر الحديث ولا ذو عهد في عهده، وقد أجمعنا على أن الذمي يقتل بالذمي، فثبت أنه أراد به، ولا يقتل ذو عهد في عهده بحربي، وإذا ثبت ذلك وجب أن أن يحمل قوله ( لا يقتل مسلم بكافر على الكافر الحربي، ليصح عطف آخر الكلام على أوله، والجواب عن ذلك، أن ذلك، أن يبين المستدل أنه ليس المراد بقوله (ولا ذو عهد في عهده ما ذهب إليه المعترض، ليسلم له التعلق بأول الحديث، وذلك أن يقول (إلا ذو عهد في عهده، لم يرد به أنه لا يقتل بالحربي، ولو أراد ذلك لم يكن لتخصيصه بوقت العهد فائدة، لأنه لا يقتل أيضًا في غير عهده بقتل الحربي، وإنما يقتل بكفره ومعنى الخبر : لا يقتل ذو عهد في عهده بكفره، فتظهر حينئذ فائدة تعليق هذا الحكم بوقت العهد، فيكون معنى الخبر : لا يقتل مؤمن بكافر قصاصا، ولا ذو عهد في عهده بكفره
The second type: that each of them attaches to a word of the evidence other than the word that the other adopted. Its example: that the Malikite argues that a Muslim is not killed in retaliation for a disbeliever, based on what was narrated from the Prophet, peace be upon him, that he said: 'A Muslim is not killed for a disbeliever.' This is a clear text in the locus of dispute. The Hanafite objects saying: 'The intended meaning of 'disbeliever' in the hadith is the combatant, as evidenced by his statement at the end of the hadith: 'nor a covenant-holder in his covenant.' And we all agree that a dhimmi is killed for a dhimmi. So it is established that he intended by it, 'and a covenant-holder in his covenant is not killed for a combatant.' And when that is established, it becomes necessary that his statement 'A Muslim is not killed for a disbeliever' be interpreted as referring to the combatant disbeliever, so that the conjunction of the end of the speech with its beginning is correct. The answer to that: that the arguer explains that what is intended by his statement 'nor a covenant-holder in his covenant' is not what the objector adopted, so that his attachment to the beginning of the hadith is preserved. He says: 'Except a covenant-holder in his covenant, he did not intend by it that he is not killed for a combatant. For if he had intended that, there would be no benefit in specifying it with the time of the covenant, because he would also not be killed outside his covenant for killing a combatant; rather, he is killed for his disbelief.' The meaning of the report becomes: 'A believer is not killed for a disbeliever in retaliation, nor is a covenant-holder in his covenant killed for his disbelief.'
Source pages p. 382

Commentary
This passage defines and illustrates the second type of objection under the first category of objections to evidence (al-tamassuk bi'l-dalil). In this type, the objector and the arguer each attach themselves to different words of the same evidence, leading to different interpretations. The example given is from the hadith 'A Muslim is not killed for a disbeliever.' The Malikite (arguer) uses this as a clear text that a Muslim cannot be executed in retaliation for killing a disbeliever. The Hanafite (objector) points to another part of the same hadith: 'nor a covenant-holder in his covenant,' arguing that 'disbeliever' in the first part must mean 'combatant' because the exception of the covenant-holder implies that a combatant disbeliever is killed for a combatant disbeliever, not a Muslim. The Malikite's answer is to reinterpret the exception clause: the covenant-holder is not killed for his disbelief only during his covenant, meaning he is killed for his disbelief outside the covenant. Thus the first part remains general: a Muslim is not killed for any disbeliever, and the exception clause is about a different ruling (killing for disbelief, not retaliation). The lesson shows how careful analysis of wording resolves objections in jadal.

Key Terms
تعلق
لفظ
دليل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example from the lesson, which word does the Hanafite objector attach to?
A) مسلم (Muslim)
B) كافر (disbeliever)
C) ذو عهد (covenant-holder)
D) قصاص (retaliation)

Key Takeaway
In jadal, an objector can challenge an arguer's use of a text by focusing on a different word within the same evidence, forcing a reinterpretation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Find a similar example in Fiqh where two scholars cite different parts of the same Quranic verse (e.g., al-Ma'ida 5:38 on cutting the hand of a thief, where one focuses on the hand and another on the context of order). Write a short jadal exchange following the pattern: arguer cites part A, objector cites part B.
?
In the hadith 'Actions are but by intentions,' one scholar attaches to 'actions' to argue for external deeds, another attaches to 'intentions' to argue for inner sincerity. Identify which words each party clings to and explain how the objection works.

Responding to the Objection: Reinterpreting the Other Word
Author provides the Malikite's answer to the objection, reinterpreting the exception clause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a response that reinterprets the objector's chosen word to preserve your original attachment to the evidence.

Core Idea
Reinterpret the objector's word to show it does not conflict with your own attachment.

When an objector clings to a different word to restrict your evidence, you must show that their word means something else. This lesson walks through the Malikite's response to the Hanafite objection, teaching how to salvage your original argument by reinterpreting the seemingly contradictory part.

Guided Path
1
Restate the objector's claim based on the other word
Identify the objector's word ('covenant-holder') and how they use it to limit your word ('disbeliever'). They claim consistency requires 'disbeliever' to mean only combatant.
The objector assumes the two parts of the hadith are inseparable.
2
Propose an alternative interpretation of the objector's word
The Malikite reinterprets 'nor a covenant-holder in his covenant' as referring to a different ruling: being killed for disbelief, not for retaliation. This separates the two parts.
The key is to show that the objector's word does not actually constrain your word.
3
Provide evidence for the new interpretation
The Malikite argues that if it meant what the objector said, there would be no benefit in specifying 'in his covenant' – because a covenant-holder would not be killed for a combatant even outside the covenant. The specification only makes sense if it means something else.
This uses a logical argument about the purpose of the restriction.
4
Show that the original attachment remains sound
After the reinterpretation, the first part 'A Muslim is not killed for a disbeliever' stands for all disbelievers, and the second part now says something different: a covenant-holder is not killed for his disbelief during his covenant. Thus the Malikite's original position is preserved.
The two parts now address different issues, so no conflict.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أ - 2 - أن يتعلق كل منهما بلفظ من الدليل غير اللفظ الذي ذهب إليه الآخر : ومثاله : أن يستدل المالكي على أن المسلم لا يقتل بالكافر قصاصا، بما روي عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أنه قال : (لا يقتل مسلم بكافر) (1) ، وهذا نص في موضع الخلاف، فيقول الحنفي معترضا : المراد بالكافر في الحديث : الحربي، بدليل قوله في آخر الحديث ولا ذو عهد في عهده، وقد أجمعنا على أن الذمي يقتل بالذمي، فثبت أنه أراد به، ولا يقتل ذو عهد في عهده بحربي، وإذا ثبت ذلك وجب أن أن يحمل قوله ( لا يقتل مسلم بكافر على الكافر الحربي، ليصح عطف آخر الكلام على أوله، والجواب عن ذلك، أن ذلك، أن يبين المستدل أنه ليس المراد بقوله (ولا ذو عهد في عهده ما ذهب إليه المعترض، ليسلم له التعلق بأول الحديث، وذلك أن يقول (إلا ذو عهد في عهده، لم يرد به أنه لا يقتل بالحربي، ولو أراد ذلك لم يكن لتخصيصه بوقت العهد فائدة، لأنه لا يقتل أيضًا في غير عهده بقتل الحربي، وإنما يقتل بكفره ومعنى الخبر : لا يقتل ذو عهد في عهده بكفره، فتظهر حينئذ فائدة تعليق هذا الحكم بوقت العهد، فيكون معنى الخبر : لا يقتل مؤمن بكافر قصاصا، ولا ذو عهد في عهده بكفره
The second type: that each of them attaches to a word of the evidence other than the word that the other adopted. Its example: that the Malikite argues that a Muslim is not killed in retaliation for a disbeliever, based on what was narrated from the Prophet, peace be upon him, that he said: 'A Muslim is not killed for a disbeliever.' This is a clear text in the locus of dispute. The Hanafite objects saying: 'The intended meaning of 'disbeliever' in the hadith is the combatant, as evidenced by his statement at the end of the hadith: 'nor a covenant-holder in his covenant.' And we all agree that a dhimmi is killed for a dhimmi. So it is established that he intended by it, 'and a covenant-holder in his covenant is not killed for a combatant.' And when that is established, it becomes necessary that his statement 'A Muslim is not killed for a disbeliever' be interpreted as referring to the combatant disbeliever, so that the conjunction of the end of the speech with its beginning is correct. The answer to that: that the arguer explains that what is intended by his statement 'nor a covenant-holder in his covenant' is not what the objector adopted, so that his attachment to the beginning of the hadith is preserved. He says: 'Except a covenant-holder in his covenant, he did not intend by it that he is not killed for a combatant. For if he had intended that, there would be no benefit in specifying it with the time of the covenant, because he would also not be killed outside his covenant for killing a combatant; rather, he is killed for his disbelief.' The meaning of the report becomes: 'A believer is not killed for a disbeliever in retaliation, nor is a covenant-holder in his covenant killed for his disbelief.'

Commentary
This passage defines and illustrates the second type of objection under the first category of objections to evidence (al-tamassuk bi'l-dalil). In this type, the objector and the arguer each attach themselves to different words of the same evidence, leading to different interpretations. The example given is from the hadith 'A Muslim is not killed for a disbeliever.' The Malikite (arguer) uses this as a clear text that a Muslim cannot be executed in retaliation for killing a disbeliever. The Hanafite (objector) points to another part of the same hadith: 'nor a covenant-holder in his covenant,' arguing that 'disbeliever' in the first part must mean 'combatant' because the exception of the covenant-holder implies that a combatant disbeliever is killed for a combatant disbeliever, not a Muslim. The Malikite's answer is to reinterpret the exception clause: the covenant-holder is not killed for his disbelief only during his covenant, meaning he is killed for his disbelief outside the covenant. Thus the first part remains general: a Muslim is not killed for any disbeliever, and the exception clause is about a different ruling (killing for disbelief, not retaliation). The lesson shows how careful analysis of wording resolves objections in jadal.

When an objector clings to a different word, you can respond by reinterpreting that word so that it no longer conflicts with your original evidence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the Quranic verse: 'The thief, male and female, cut off their hands' (5:38). A Shafi'i arguer uses this to mandate amputation of the hand. A Hanafi objector points to the same verse's context (repentance in 5:39) to argue the verse is not applicable in all cases. Draft a response that reinterprets the objector's word to preserve the original ruling.
?

Construct a response that reinterprets 'innovates' to mean only harmful innovation.

Attachment to General Evidence in Jadal
Author defines and illustrates the objection type where each party appeals to the general scope of the evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the 'attachment to generality' objection in a jadal exchange and evaluate which party's application is more appropriate based on the specific circumstances.

Core Idea
When both sides invoke the same general evidence, the debate turns on which party's application fits the specific context better.

In jadal, a common move is for opponents to appeal to the same general textual evidence. The key is not to deny the generality, but to argue whose specific application is more appropriate. This passage from a jadal manual illustrates how the Malikite refines the application of the hadith on consent to prevail over the Hanafite's objection.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general evidence
Locate the hadith that both parties cite. In this example, it is 'A person's wealth is not lawful except with his consent.' Both sides accept this as a general principle.
The evidence is shared (مشترك الدليل), not disputed.
2
Determine each party's application
The Malikite applies the hadith to the seller: the seller cannot take the buyer's money (the price) without the buyer's consent. The Hanafite applies it to the buyer: the buyer cannot keep the seller's money (the price) without the seller's consent.
Each side claims the generality supports their own position.
3
Analyze the Malikite's response
The Malikite specifies that the seller's wealth is the fruit, not the price. Since the fruit was destroyed by blight, the buyer never received it, so the seller's consent to the buyer taking the fruit was not realized. The price, however, was taken by the seller with his own consent.
The response involves specifying which 'wealth' is at stake for each party.
4
Evaluate appropriateness (أولوية)
The Malikite argues that his inference is more appropriate because the condition of consent applies differently to the seller and buyer given the specific facts. The seller consented to sell the fruit for a price, but the buyer's consent to pay for undelivered fruit is absent.
The debate is resolved by showing that the generality does not apply symmetrically due to context.
5
Recognize the jadal structure
This is an objection type where both parties 'attach to the evidence from the perspective of generality' (التعلق بالدليل من جهة العموم). The answer involves بيان الأولوية (demonstrating priority) or تخصيص ضمني (implicit specification).
The arguer (المستدل) must show that his application is أولى (more appropriate) than the objector's.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ب - أن يتعلق كل واحد منهما بالدليل من جهة العموم: ومثاله : أن يستدل المالكي في وضع الجائحة، بما روي عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أنه قال : لا يطيب مال امرئ إلا بطيب نفس منه) (3) ، فلا يجوز أن يأخذ البائع من المشتري ماله إلا بطيب نفس منه .. فيعترضه الحنفي قائلا : هذا مشترك الدليل، فإنه لا يطيب للمشتري أيضًا إمساك مال البائع إلا بطيب نفس منه، والجواب عنه أن يبين المالكي، وهو المستدل أن استدلاله بالحديث أولى من استدلال المعترض، ويرد اعتراض السائل بأن يقول : إن مال البائع إنما كانت الثمرة، وتلك لا تطيب للمشتري إلا بطيب نفس من البائع، فإذا أصابتها الجائحة فلم تحصل للمشتري، فلم يعتبر طيب نفس البائع بذلك، وليس كذلك الثمن، فإن البائع أخذه بطيب نفس، فوجب ألا يحل له
B) That each of them attaches to the evidence from the perspective of generality. Its example: that the Malikite argues concerning the cancellation of a fruit sale due to blight, based on what was narrated from the Prophet, peace be upon him, that he said: 'A person's wealth is not lawful except with his consent.' So the seller cannot take the buyer's wealth except with his consent. The Hanafite objects saying: 'This evidence is shared, for it is also not lawful for the buyer to retain the seller's wealth except with his consent.' The answer to that: that the Malikite, who is the arguer, explains that his inference from the hadith is more appropriate than the objector's inference. He rejects the objector's objection by saying: 'The seller's wealth was the fruit, and that is not lawful for the buyer except with the seller's consent. So when the blight afflicts it and the buyer does not receive it, the seller's consent to that is not considered. The price is not like that, for the seller took it with consent, so it should not be lawful for him (to keep it without delivering the fruit).'
Source pages p. 382

Commentary
This passage defines and illustrates the type of objection where both the arguer and the objector invoke the same evidence but from the perspective of its generality, each applying it to a different party. The example is from the hadith 'A person's wealth is not lawful except with his consent.' The Malikite uses it to argue that if a fruit sale is struck by blight (jaiha), the buyer must get a refund because the seller took the price without the buyer's consent (since the fruit was not delivered). The Hanafite objects that the same hadith applies to the buyer: it is not lawful for the buyer to hold the seller's money (the price) without the seller's consent. The Malikite's answer is to specify that the seller's wealth here is the fruit; the seller does not consent to the buyer taking the fruit without payment, but when blight destroys the fruit, the buyer never received it, so the seller's consent to the price is not tied to delivery. The price itself was taken voluntarily by the seller, so the seller should not keep it when the fruit is lost. This shows that the arguer's application of the generality is more appropriate given the specific circumstances.

Key Terms
عموم
جائحة
طيب نفس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How does the Malikite argue that his inference from the hadith is more appropriate than the Hanafite's?
A) The hadith is specific to sellers only.
B) The seller's wealth (fruit) requires the buyer's consent, but the price does not require the seller's consent in the same way.
C) The hadith is abrogated by another text.
D) The Hanafite's argument is based on a weak transmission.

Key Takeaway
The 'attachment to generality' objection is resolved by showing which party's application is more appropriate given the specific context, not by denying the evidence's generality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify the two applications of the hadith 'لا يطيب مال امرئ إلا بطيب نفس منه' in the jaiha debate. Then, write a brief defense of the Hanafite position using the same method of specifying which party's wealth is at stake, and show why the Hanafite might argue his application is more appropriate.
?
Consider the hadith 'The burden of proof is on the claimant, and the oath is on the one who denies.' Both a claimant and a defendant might invoke this generality. Construct a short jadal exchange where each side applies it, and then provide a response similar to the Malikite's that shows one side's inference is أولى.

Objection by Substituting a Word in Narration
Author defines the objection type by substituting a word in the narration and gives an example with answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify an objection by word substitution in a hadith-based argument and evaluate the response using jadal rules.

Core Idea
A single different wording can undermine a proof—but reconciliation is the tool of the master debater.

In dialectic, an opponent may try to derail your hadith-based argument by citing a variant narration with a different word. This lesson teaches you to recognize this move and how to counter it by combining both versions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the arguer's hadith and its key term
The Shafi'i/Hanbali uses the hadith 'kill' (قتلوا) to argue that heirs have a choice without the killer's consent. The key term is 'kill.'
2
Spot the objector's substitution
The Hanafite cites a variant with 'ransom' (فادوا) instead of 'kill,' changing the meaning to mutual consent.
The substitution is effective because 'ransom' implies a reciprocal agreement.
3
Recognize the objector's claim
The Hanafite argues that the variant is also a khabar wahid, so we must suspend judgment until the original is known.
This tactic aims to block the inference by creating doubt about the wording.
4
Apply the jadal response: combine both versions
The defender responds that both versions are authentic and should be treated as two independent reports. They are combined: the choice is allowed both with mutual consent (ransom) and without (kill).
This preserves the arguer's position while accommodating the variant.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المطلب الرابع: الاعتراض على الاستدلال بالسنة بدعوى اختلاف الرواية وهو نوعان : فأهله أ - الاعتراض بإبدال لفظ مكان لفظ : وهو على ضربين : أ - 1 - أن يورد المعترض الحديث على وجه يمنع من استدلال المستدل به : مثاله : أن يستدل الشافعي أو الحنبلي في تخيير أولياء دم المقتول بين القود والدية من غير مراعاة رضا الجاني، بقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام في قتيل خزاعة : (وأنتم يا خزاعة، فقد قتلتم هذا القتيل وأنا والله عاقله، فمن قتل بعد ذلك قتيلا بين خيرتين إن أحبوا ،قتلوا وإن أحبوا أخذوا العقل) (2) . فيقول الحنفي معترضا : قد روي : (إن أحبوا فادوا) (3)، والمفاداة مفاعلة، فلا يكون إلا صلحًا بالتراضي والخبر خبر واحد، فوجب أن يُتوقف فيه حتى يُعلم أصل الحديث . والجواب : أنه قد روي الجميع، والظاهر منهما الصحة، فيصير كالخبرين، فتجمع بينهما فنقول : يجوز بالتراضي وبغير التراضي
The fourth section: Objection to inference by the Sunna by claiming variance in narration. It is of two types: Its first: A) Objection by substituting a word for a word. This has two subtypes: A-1) That the objector presents the hadith in a way that prevents the arguer from inferring with it. Its example: that the Shafi'i or Hanbali argues for giving the heirs of the slain the choice between retaliation and blood-money without considering the consent of the perpetrator, based on his statement, peace be upon him, regarding the slain of Khuza'a: 'And you, O Khuza'a, have killed this slain one, and I am by Allah his 'aqila. So whoever kills a slain one after this will have two choices: if they wish they may kill, and if they wish they may take the 'aql.' The Hanafite objects saying: 'It has been narrated [with the wording] "If they wish, they may ransom" and ransom is a mutual exchange, so it is only a settlement by mutual consent. The report is a single narration, so it must be suspended until the original of the hadith is known.' The answer: that all versions have been narrated, and both appear sound, so they become like two reports, so we combine them by saying: it is permissible with mutual consent and without mutual consent.
Source pages p. 383

Commentary
This passage introduces the fourth category of objection to inference by the Sunna: claiming variance in narration. It focuses on the first type: objection by substituting a word. The subtype here is where the objector presents a different wording of the same hadith that undermines the arguer's use of the original wording. The example involves a hadith about the slain of Khuza'a, which says the heirs have two choices: kill or take blood-money. The Hanafite objector claims that another narration says 'ransom' (fadu) instead of 'kill,' which implies mutual consent (since 'ransom' is a reciprocal form). Because the hadith is a single narration (khabar wahid), the objector argues that we must suspend judgment until the original is known. The answer is that both versions are widespread and both are considered authentic, so they are treated like two independent reports. We can combine them: the choice is allowed both with mutual consent (according to the 'ransom' version) and without (according to the 'kill' version). This shows that when variant readings exist, one does not automatically reject the arguer's version; rather, both can be reconciled if possible.

Key Terms
إبدال لفظ
رواية
خبر واحد

8 min
Objection by Substituting a Word in a Hadith
Author classifies objections to hadith-based arguments by variant wording and gives two examples with refutations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify an objection by word substitution in a hadith and evaluate the proponent's response by reconciling variant narrations.

Core Idea
A single word change can shift a legal ruling; the dialectician must harmonize both wordings.

In jadal, opponents often attack a proof by citing a different hadith wording. This lesson examines one such objection type: substituting a word to block the inference. The proponent's response involves reconciling both narrations or preferring the more specific wording. Through two examples, you will learn how to handle such objections in Islamic legal debate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proponent's original hadith and legal claim
Find the hadith citation and the ruling the proponent intends to derive from it. For instance, the Shafi'i uses the hadith about the slain of Khaza'a to support giving heirs a choice between qisas and blood money without the killer's consent.
The claim must be clearly linked to the specific wording.
2
Identify the objector's variant wording and its legal implication
Note the word substituted by the objector and how it changes the meaning. In the first example, the Hanafi replaces 'take the blood money' with 'ransom', implying mutual consent is required.
The shift alters the legal consequence.
3
Analyze the proponent's response
Determine whether the response reconciles both wordings (as in the first example: both narrations are sound, so the ruling applies with and without consent) or prefers one based on a principle (as in the second example: the more specific wording is preferred because narrators report what is certain).
The response uses hadith criticism principles, not just assertion.
4
Evaluate the strength of the response
Assess whether the response effectively preserves the proponent's proof while acknowledging the variant. Does it convincingly show that both wordings can be true or that one is more reliable?
In jadal, the goal is to maintain the proof, not necessarily to win the debate outright.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المطلب الرابع: الاعتراض على الاستدلال بالسنة بدعوى اختلاف الرواية وهو نوعان : أ - الاعتراض بإبدال لفظ مكان لفظ : وهو على ضربين : أ - 1 - أن يورد المعترض الحديث على وجه يمنع من استدلال المستدل به : مثاله : أن يستدل الشافعي أو الحنبلي في تخيير أولياء دم المقتول بين القود والدية من غير مراعاة رضا الجاني، بقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام في قتيل خزاعة : (وأنتم يا خزاعة، فقد قتلتم هذا القتيل وأنا والله عاقله، فمن قتل بعد ذلك قتيلا بين خيرتين إن أحبوا قتلوا وإن أحبوا أخذوا العقل) . فيقول الحنفي معترضا : قد روي : (إن أحبوا فادوا)، والمفاداة مفاعلة، فلا يكون إلا صلحًا بالتراضي والخبر خبر واحد، فوجب أن يُتوقف فيه حتى يُعلم أصل الحديث . والجواب : أنه قد روي الجميع، والظاهر منهما الصحة، فيصير كالخبرين، فتجمع بينهما فنقول : يجوز بالتراضي وبغير التراضي . ومثاله : أن يستدل المالكي في ثبوت خيار الشفعة للورثة، بما روي عن النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام أنه قال : (من ترك حقا فلورثته )، والشفعة حق فوجب أن يكون للورثة، فيقول الحنفي معترضا قد روي من ترك مالا فلورثته)، ولا يمنع أن يثبت المال للورثة والطريق للمالكي في الجواب عن هذا الاعتراض أن يقول : إنه إذا روي هذا وروي هذا وجب استعمال الروايتين جميعا، وأيضًا فإنه لو لم يكن بد من استعمال إحداهما لكان الحديث الذي استدللنا به أولى، لأن الراوي إذا شك في الخبر رواه على أخص أخباره لأنه يقين عنده، ولا يجوز أن يشك في الأخص فيرويه على الأعم لأن ذلك يكون كذبًا .
The fourth topic: The objection to reasoning with the Sunnah by claiming difference in narration, and it is of two types: A - The objection by substituting a word for a word: and it is of two kinds: A-1 - That the objector presents the hadith in a manner that prevents the proponent's reasoning from it. Example: The Shafi'i or Hanbali argues for giving the guardians of a slain person a choice between qisas and blood money without considering the killer's consent, by the Prophet's saying about the slain of Khaza'a: 'And you, O Khaza'a, indeed you killed this slain person, and I am by Allah his 'aqila. So whoever kills after this a slain person is between two choices: if they wish, they may kill, and if they wish, they may take the blood money.' The Hanafi objects: It has been narrated: 'If they wish, they may ransom,' and ransoming is a mutual dealing, so it cannot be except a settlement by mutual consent, and the report is a single report, so it must be suspended until the original of the hadith is known. The answer: That both have been narrated, and apparently both are sound, so it becomes like two reports, and we reconcile them by saying: It is permissible with mutual consent and without mutual consent. And another example: The Maliki argues for the existence of the right of preemption for heirs, by what is narrated from the Prophet that he said: 'Whoever leaves a right, it is for his heirs,' and preemption is a right, so it must be for the heirs. The Hanafi objects: It has been narrated 'Whoever leaves property, it is for his heirs,' and it does not prevent that property is established for the heirs. And the way for the Maliki in answering this objection is to say: When this has been narrated and that has been narrated, it is necessary to use both narrations. Also, if there is no way but to use one of them, the hadith we have used is more worthy because if the narrator doubts the report, he narrates it with its most specific version because it is certain for him, and it is not permissible that he doubts the specific and narrates it as the general because that would be a lie.
Source pages p. 383

Commentary
This passage is a classification and illustration of a particular type of dialectical objection (اعتراض) in Islamic legal reasoning (jadal). The author defines the fourth type of objection to arguments based on the Sunnah: objecting by claiming variant narrations (اختلاف الرواية). Within this, the first subtype (أ) is the objection by substituting one word for another (إبدال لفظ مكان لفظ), which itself has two forms. The first form (أ-1) occurs when the objector presents the hadith in a wording that prevents the proponent from drawing his intended inference. Two examples are given. In the first, the Shafi'i or Hanbali uses a hadith that gives the choice of qisas or blood money to the heirs of a victim, implying that the killer's consent is not required. The Hanafi objects by citing a variant narration saying 'ransom' (فادوا) instead of 'take blood money' (أخذوا العقل), arguing that ransom implies mutual consent, so the hadith cannot be used to support the without-consent ruling. The answer is that both narrations are sound, so they should be reconciled: the ruling is allowed both with and without consent. In the second example, the Maliki argues that the right of preemption (شفعة) passes to heirs because the Prophet said 'whoever leaves a right, it is for his heirs.' The Hanafi objects by citing the more well-known narration 'whoever leaves property, it is for his heirs,' which does not mention 'right.' The Maliki's answer is that both narrations should be used together, and if one must be preferred, the proponent's narration is more specific, and narrators when uncertain go with the more specific wording to avoid lying. The argument relies on the principle that narrators report what is certain, and the specific is more certain than the general. This passage demonstrates the dialectical skill of harmonizing apparent contradictions between hadith texts and using principles of narration criticism to defend one's proof.

Key Terms
الاعتراض
رواية
تخيير
إبدال لفظ

Variant Hadith Wording as a Dialectical Tool
Second subtype: objector's version becomes his proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and analyze the dialectical technique of citing a variant hadith wording to flip its evidentiary value, and evaluate the proponent's responses.

Core Idea
In jadal, the objector can turn the proponent's own hadith into a proof for himself by citing a variant wording.

In dialectical debates over legal rulings, both sides often rely on hadith texts. A sophisticated objector does not simply reject the proponent's hadith; he may present a variant wording that actually supports his own position. This lesson examines that technique through the classic Maliki-Shafi'i debate on tayammum, and explores the rebuttal options available to the proponent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proponent's claim and evidence
The Maliki argues that tayammum is permissible with any earth substance (including stones), citing the hadith: 'The earth has been made for me a mosque and a purifier.' Note how the proponent uses the general term 'earth' to support his broad ruling.
The proponent's interpretation depends on the scope of the word 'earth' in the hadith.
2
Recognize the objector's move
The Shafi'i objector does not deny the hadith. Instead, he cites a variant: 'The earth has been made a mosque and its soil a purifier.' The addition of 'its soil' restricts the purifier to soil, thus supporting the Shafi'i position that tayammum is only with soil.
This is a word-substitution objection (al-i'tirad bi-lafz). The objector turns the hadith into his own proof.
3
Analyze the flip in evidentiary value
The variant wording changes the meaning: 'earth' is general, but 'its soil' specifies a particular substance. The objector argues that the specification (takhsis) indicates the ruling is limited to soil, making the hadith a proof for him.
The objector now claims the hadith as his own evidence, not just a refutation of the proponent.
4
Evaluate the proponent's rebuttal options
The author presents two rebuttals: (1) Show that the proponent's narration is more famous (ashhar) and more apparent (azhar) in meaning. (2) Argue that if forced to choose, the proponent's narration should be taken as the original (asl) because the objector's narrator may be unreliable.
These rebuttals shift the discussion from meaning to the authenticity and priority of the narrated versions.
5
Assess the strength of each response
The first response relies on hadith criticism and clarity of text; the second appeals to a principle of preferring one's own narrator. Both are standard tools in jadal to defend one's evidence when variant texts exist.
The outcome depends on which narration is judged more reliable or apparent in the specific scholarly context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أ - 2 - أن يروي الحديث على وجه يصير به حجة له : وذلك مثل أن يستدل المالكي على جواز التيمم بالحجارة، بما روي عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أنه قال : (جعلت لي الأرض مسجدا وطهورا) ، فيجب بمقتض هذا الحديث أن يجوز التيمم بكل ما يقع عليه اسم الأرض، فيقول الشافعي معترضا: قد روي: جعلت الأرض مسجدا وترابها طهور) ، فيصير الحديث حجة لنا، لأن عدوله في التيمم عن جميع الأرض إلى التراب يدل على تخصيص التيمم به، والجواب عن هذا الاعتراض أن يبين المستدل أن روايته أشهر وأظهر إن تمكن من ذلك، أو يقول : إن حديثنا يجب أن يكون الأصل إن لم يكن بد من الأخذ بأحدهما، لأن راويكم .
A-2 - That he narrates the hadith in a manner that makes it become a proof for him. Example: The Maliki argues for the permissibility of tayammum with stones, by what is narrated from the Prophet that he said: 'The earth has been made for me a mosque and a purifier.' So by this hadith it must be permissible to perform tayammum with everything that falls under the name of earth. The Shafi'i objects: It has been narrated: 'The earth has been made a mosque and its soil a purifier.' So the hadith becomes a proof for us, because his specification in tayammum from all earth to soil indicates the restriction of tayammum to it. And the answer to this objection is that the proponent should show that his narration is more famous and more apparent if he can, or say: Our hadith must be taken as the original if there is no way but to accept one of them, because your narrator...
Source pages p. 383

Commentary
This passage presents the second subtype of word-substitution objection (أ-2). Here, the objector does not merely block the proponent's inference but cites a version of the hadith that actually supports his own position. The example involves the Maliki argument that tayammum is permissible with any stone or earth, based on the hadith 'the earth has been made for me a mosque and a purifier' (implying the earth itself is purifying). The Shafi'i objects by citing a variant: 'the earth has been made a mosque and its soil a purifier,' which restricts the purifying element to soil (تراب), not any stone. Thus, the objector turns the hadith into a proof for his own view that tayammum is only with pure soil. The proponent (Maliki) has two possible answers: either show that his own narration is more famous (أشهر) and more apparent (أظهر), or argue that if one must choose, his narration should be taken as the original basis because the opponent's narrator (i.e., the one who transmitted the restrictive version) might have been uncertain or unreliable. The text cuts off but the idea is to prioritize one's own narration on grounds of authenticity or preference. This passage illustrates how variant wordings can flip the evidentiary value of a hadith in dialectical exchange, and the skills required to defend one's proof.

Key Terms
التيمم (al-tayammum): The dry ablution performed when water is unavailable or harmful; the debate here is whether it is valid with any earth substance or only with soil. [Note: No significant shift; the term is stable in fiqh and jadal.]
طهور (ṭahūr): A purifier; in the hadith, the earth is described as a purifier for tayammum. [Note: Standard usage in hadith and fiqh.]
أشهر وأظهر (ashhar wa aẓhar): More famous (in narration) and more apparent (in meaning); criteria for preferring one version of a hadith over another. [Note: These are standard terms in hadith criticism and usul al-fiqh for weighing conflicting evidence.]

Objecting with a Variant Hadith Narration
Dialectical exchange over tayammum using variant hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the dialectical move of objecting with a variant narration and evaluate which party has the stronger position using jadal principles.

Core Idea
A variant narration can flip a proof: the objector cites it to restrict the claimant's generalization.

In dialectical argumentation, a common tactic is to cite a variant narration that changes the meaning of the evidence. This lesson examines a classic exchange between a Maliki and a Shafi'i over tayammum. The Maliki uses the hadith 'the earth was made for me a mosque and a purification.' The Shafi'i objects with a variant that adds 'and its soil a purification.' We will analyze how the objection works and how the Maliki can respond using principles of narrator certainty and fame.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claimant's original evidence
The Maliki argues for tayammum with stones based on the hadith: 'The earth was made for me a mosque and a purification.' The key term is 'earth' (الأرض), which is general. Understand that the Maliki takes this as a proof that any earth-like substance suffices.
2
Understand the objector's variant
The Shafi'i objects by citing a version: 'The earth was made a mosque and its soil a purification.' The difference is the addition of 'its soil' (وترابها). This restricts purification to soil alone, not all earth. The objector claims this variant is the correct wording, so the evidence now supports him.
3
Analyze the legal implication of the variant
If the variant is accepted, the generalization collapses: tayammum is limited to soil. The Shafi'i uses the change to show that the Maliki's hadith is incomplete or inaccurate. The move is to 'turn the evidence' (صار حجة له).
4
Reconstruct the Maliki's possible responses
The text offers two responses: (1) argue that the Maliki's narration is more famous (أشهر) and apparent (أظهر); (2) argue that the Maliki's narrator spoke with certainty, while the Shafi'i's narrator may have hesitated (شك) and chosen 'soil' out of caution, but adding a specification without certainty is a lie. Evaluate the strength of each response.
5
Determine who prevails by jadal rules
In jadal, the burden is on the objector to disturb the claimant's evidence. If the claimant can show his narration is more reliable or certain, he retains the proof. The debate hinges on the epistemological status of the narration, not merely on textual variants.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أ - 2 - أن يروي الحديث على وجه يصير به حجة له : وذلك مثل أن يستدل المالكي على جواز التيمم بالحجارة، بما روي عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أنه قال : (جعلت لي الأرض مسجدا وطهورا) ، فيجب بمقتض هذا الحديث أن يجوز التيمم بكل ما يقع عليه اسم الأرض، فيقول الشافعي معترضا: قد روي: جعلت الأرض مسجدا وترابها طهور) ، فيصير الحديث حجة لنا، لأن عدوله في التيمم عن جميع الأرض إلى التراب يدل على تخصيص التيمم به، والجواب عن هذا الاعتراض أن يبين المستدل أن روايته أشهر وأظهر إن تمكن من ذلك، أو يقول : إن حديثنا يجب أن يكون الأصل إن لم يكن بد من الأخذ بأحدهما، لأن راويكم يجوز أن يشك فيعلق التيمم بالتراب لأنه متيقن ولا يجوز أن يشك راوي حديثنا فيعلق التيمم على الأرض لأن ذلك كذب
A - 2 - That he narrates the hadith in a way that makes it evidence for him: such as when the Maliki argues for the permissibility of tayammum with stones, based on what was narrated from the Prophet ﷺ that he said: 'The earth was made for me a mosque and a purification.' So according to this hadith, tayammum should be permissible with everything to which the name 'earth' applies. Then the Shafi'i objects: 'It has been narrated: "The earth was made a mosque and its soil a purification,"' so the hadith becomes evidence for us, because his deviation in tayammum from all the earth to soil indicates the specification of tayammum to it. The answer to this objection is that the arguer should show that his narration is more famous and apparent if he can, or he says: 'Our hadith should be the original as long as there is no alternative but to adopt one of the two, because your narrator may doubt and thereby attribute tayammum to soil because it is certain, but it is not permissible for the narrator of our hadith to doubt and attribute tayammum to the earth because that would be a lie.'
Source pages p. 384

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exchange from a manual of jadal. It illustrates the objection called 'narrating a hadith in a way that makes it evidence for the objector.' The Maliki argues that tayammum is permissible with anything called 'earth' based on the hadith 'the earth was made for me a mosque and a purification.' The Shafi'i objects with a variant narration: 'the earth was made a mosque and its soil a purification,' implying that only soil is specified. The answer given is twofold: either the Maliki shows that his narration is more famous and apparent, or he argues that his narration must be taken as the original because the Shafi'i's narrator could have hesitated and specified 'soil' out of certainty, while the Maliki's narrator would not have risked lying by attributing tayammum to all earth. The response relies on the principle of certainty in narration, a key concept in jadal.

Key Takeaway

Objection by Changing a Vowel: The Case of the Child's Lineage
Author gives an example of objecting by vowel change in a hadith about lineage, with response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a jadal objection based on a variant hadith reading (by vowel change) and evaluate the response using the three Maliki arguments.

Core Idea
A single vowel change can shift a ruling, but the Prophet's own reasoning overrides mere textual variants.

In this lesson, we examine how a Hanafi jurist objects to a Maliki argument by citing a different reading of a hadith — a classic technique in jadal. The dispute centers on whether a slave girl's child is free (attributed to the master) or remains a slave. The Hanafi claims the Prophet said 'He is for you' (implying ownership), not 'He is for you, O Abd' (implying brotherhood). We will walk through the objection and the Maliki's rebuttal, learning how context and the Prophet's own explanation (ta'lil) can defeat a mere textual variant.

Guided Path
1
Identify the positions
The Maliki argues that if a master has intercourse with his slave girl, she becomes a bed (firash) and her child is free and attributed to the master without acknowledgment. The Hanafi objects by citing a variant reading of the hadith.
2
Locate the variant
The Hanafi says the narration is 'huwa laka Abd ibn Zam'a' (with a possessive pronoun indicating 'he is for you' i.e., a slave) rather than 'huwa laka ya Abd' (with a vocative 'O Abd', implying brotherhood). This changes the meaning from 'he is your brother' to 'he is your slave'.
The vowel change is in the absence of the vocative particle 'ya'.
3
Analyze the objection
The Hanafi argues that if the Prophet ruled the child is for Abd as a slave, then the Maliki inference of freedom fails. The variant suggests the Prophet did not attribute the child to the master as a free son.
This is a classic objection based on a different textual reading.
4
Examine the response
The Maliki responds with three points: (1) It is impossible that Abd would claim his brother and the Prophet would give him as a slave. (2) The Prophet's own explanation 'al-walad lil-firash' shows the child is free, not a slave. (3) There is another narration with the vocative 'ya Abd' which supports the Maliki.
The response uses context, ta'lil (Prophet's reasoning), and an alternative narration.
5
Assess the outcome
The objection fails because the variant does not override the clear context and the Prophet's explicit principle. The Maliki's evidence is stronger.
In jadal, the response must address the objection directly and provide stronger evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومثاله : أيضًا؛ أن يحتج المالكي في أن الأمة تصير فراشًا بالوطء، وأن ولدها يُلْحَقُ بالسيد من غير أن يستلحقه، بما روي أن سعد بن أبي وقاص وعبد بن زمعة تحاكما إلى رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ، فقال عبد بن زمعة: أخي وابن وليدة أبي وُلد على فراشه فقال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم : (هو لك عبد بن زمعة، الولد للفراش وللعاهر الحجر) . فيقول الحنفي معترضا : الرواية : (هو لك عبد بن زمعة)، وهذا لا يدل على ما تقوله لأنه قضى به عبدا له ولهذا قال لسودة احتجبي منه، والجواب : أن هذا غير صحيح، لأنه يستحيل أن يدعيه أخا، فيقضي النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم له به عبدًا، وأيضا فإن تعليل النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم ينفي ما ادعيتم لأنه قال : (الولد للفراش وللعاهر الحجر)، والولد لا يكون عبدا للفراش، ثم إنه قد روي هو لك يا عبد بن زمعة)(5)، والأخذ به أولى
Another example: also, when the Maliki argues that the slave girl becomes a bed (firash) by intercourse, and that her child is attributed to the master without him acknowledging it, based on what was narrated that Sa'd ibn Abi Waqqas and Abd ibn Zam'a submitted a dispute to the Messenger of Allah ﷺ, and Abd ibn Zam'a said: 'My brother, son of my father's slave girl, was born on his bed.' The Messenger of Allah ﷺ said: 'He is for you, Abd ibn Zam'a; the child belongs to the bed, and for the adulterer is the stone.' Then the Hanafi objects: 'The narration is "He is for you, Abd ibn Zam'a," and this does not indicate what you say because he ruled that he is a slave for him, and that is why he said to Sawda: "Veil from him."' The answer: 'This is incorrect, because it is impossible that he claimed him as a brother and the Prophet ﷺ ruled him to be a slave for him. Also, the Prophet's explanation negates what you claimed because he said: "The child belongs to the bed, and for the adulterer is the stone," and the child cannot be a slave for the bed. Moreover, it has been narrated "He is for you, O Abd ibn Zam'a" and adopting it is more appropriate.'
Source pages p. 385

Commentary
This passage is a second example of objection by variant narration with a change in vowel (حركة). The Maliki argues that if a master has intercourse with his slave girl, she becomes a bed (firash) and her child is attributed to the master without his acknowledgment, citing the hadith of Sa'd and Abd. The Hanafi objects that the narration says 'He is for you, Abd ibn Zam'a' (with a possessive pronoun indicating ownership), not 'He is for you, O Abd ibn Zam'a' (with a vocative). This, he claims, shows the Prophet ruled the child as a slave, not a free brother. The answer refutes this: it is impossible that Abd would claim his brother and the Prophet would give him as a slave. Furthermore, the Prophet's own explanation—'The child belongs to the bed'—shows the child is free, not a slave. Additionally, another version with the vocative ('Ya Abd') supports the Malikis. Thus, the objection fails.

Key Terms
الاعتراض
الفراش
الحركة

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi objection regarding the child of the slave girl, what is the alleged variant narration?
A) The Prophet said 'He is your brother' instead of 'He is yours.'
B) The Hanafi claims the narration is 'He is for you, Abd ibn Zam'a' implying slavery.
C) The hadith had a different vowel in 'al-firash', making it 'al-fursh'.
D) The Hanafi claimed the hadith was abrogated.

Key Takeaway
In jadal, an objection based on a variant reading can be refuted by the impossibility of the claimant's own stance, the Prophet's own reasoning (ta'lil), and the existence of a supporting narration.

Objection by Differing Narration: The Fetus Example
Author gives first example of objection by vowel replacement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct an objection by varying the vowel of a key word in a hadith to create doubt in a legal proof.

Core Idea
A single vowel shift can turn a definitive proof into a doubtful one, forcing the debate to tarjīḥ.

In this lesson, we examine the first example from the text: how a Hanafi jurist objects to a Shafi‘i/Hanbali proof using a variant vowel reading of the hadith about fetal slaughter. Understanding this technique is essential for tracking how debates unfold in uṣūl al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mudda'i's proof
The Shafi‘i/Hanbali uses the hadith 'ذكاه الجنين ذكاة أمه' to argue that slaughtering the mother makes the fetus permissible. The key term is 'ذكاة' with dammah, meaning 'is the slaughter'.
2
Find the mu'tarid's objection
The Hanafi objects by citing a variant reading: 'ذكاةً' with fatha (indefinite accusative), which changes the meaning to 'like the slaughter', creating uncertainty.
This is an objection by differing narration through vowel replacement.
3
Understand the consequence
Because of the doubt (taraddud), the proof is suspended until tarjīḥ (preponderance) is established. The author notes that Abu Hanifa's view is favored since the default is prohibition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ب - الاعتراض باختلاف الرواية بإبدال حركة مكان حركة :
مثاله أن يستدل الشافعي أو الحنبلي في إباحة الجنين بذكاة أمه، بقول النبي : (ذكاه الجنين ذكاة أمه) (2) ، أي أن ذكاته هي ذكاة أمه، فيقول الحنفي معترضا : قد روي (ذكاةً) بالفتح ، فيكون المعنى أن ذكاته مثل ذكاة أمه، وإذا تردد أن تكون ذكاته نفس ذكاة أمه ، بین وبين أن تكون مثل ذكاة أمه، وقف الدليل على الترجيح، وكان ما ذهب إليه أبو حنيفة أشبه لأن الباب باب حظر في الأصل إلى أن يحصل يقين الذكاة(3) ، ومثاله : أيضًا؛ أن يحتج المالكي في أن الأمة تصير فراشًا بالوطء، وأن ولدها يُلْحَقُ بالسيد من غير أن يستلحقه، بما روي أن سعد بن أبي وقاص وعبد بن زمعة تحاكما إلى رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ، فقال عبد بن زمعة: أخي وابن وليدة أبي وُلد على فراشه فقال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم : (هو لك عبد بن زمعة، الولد للفراش وللعاهر الحجر) (4) .
فيقول الحنفي معترضا : الرواية : (هو لك عبد بن زمعة)، وهذا لا يدل على ما تقوله لأنه قضى به عبدا له ولهذا قال لسودة احتجبي منه، والجواب : أن هذا غير صحيح، لأنه يستحيل أن يدعيه أخا، فيقضي النبي فيقضي النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم له به عبدًا، وأيضا فإن تعليل النبي الا الله ينفي ما ادعيتم لأنه قال : (الولد للفراش وللعاهر الحجر)، والولد لا يكون عبدا للفراش، ، ثم إنه قد روي هو لك يا عبد بن زمعة)(5)، والأخذ بالزائد أولى وهذه الرواية مفسرة لروايتكم، لأن حرف النداء يجوز إسقاطه، وأما قوله لسودة احتجبي منه ، فإنما كان ذلك لأن زمعة لم يستلحقه وإنما أقر به ابنه عبد، فثبت ذلك حقه في وهو ألا يملكه ويعطيه حصته من ميراث أبيه على ما أقر به، ولا يثبت بذلك نسبه من ،زمعة فلذلك لم يجز أن يرى سودة بنت زمعة ولا يكون بذلك من ذوي ،محارمها والله أعلم
(1)
B - Objection by differing narration through replacing a vowel with a vowel: Example: the Shafi‘i or Hanbali argues for the permissibility of the fetus by the slaughter of its mother, by the Prophet’s saying: 'Its slaughter is the slaughter of its mother' (2), meaning that its slaughter is the slaughter of its mother. The Hanafi objects: It has been narrated as (ذكاةً) with fatha, so the meaning is that its slaughter is like the slaughter of its mother. When it is uncertain whether its slaughter is the same as its mother’s or similar to it, the proof stops pending preponderance, and what Abu Hanifa held is more appropriate because the matter is one of prohibition in origin until certainty of slaughter is attained (3). Another example: the Maliki argues that the slave-woman becomes a bed (firāsh) by intercourse, and her child is attributed to the master without the master claiming it, by what is narrated that Sa‘d ibn Abi Waqqas and ‘Abd ibn Zam‘a referred a dispute to the Messenger of Allah, and ‘Abd ibn Zam‘a said: 'My brother, son of my father’s slave-woman, born on his bed.' The Messenger said: 'He is yours, ‘Abd ibn Zam‘a; the child belongs to the bed, and for the fornicator is the stone' (4). The Hanafi objects: The narration is 'He is yours, ‘Abd ibn Zam‘a,' and this does not indicate what you claim because he judged him as his slave, and that is why he said to Sawda: 'Veil yourself from him.' The answer: This is not correct, because it is impossible that he claimed him as a brother, then the Prophet judged him as a slave for him. Also, the Prophet’s reasoning negates what you claim because he said: 'The child belongs to the bed, and for the fornicator is the stone,' and the child is not a slave of the bed. Moreover, it has been narrated: 'He is yours, O ‘Abd ibn Zam‘a' (5), and accepting the additional is preferable, and this narration explains your narration because the vocative particle may be dropped. As for his saying to Sawda: 'Veil yourself from him,' that was because Zam‘a did not claim him as his son, but rather his son ‘Abd acknowledged him, so his right was established in that he does not own him and gives him his share of his father’s inheritance according to what he acknowledged, and his lineage from Zam‘a is not established thereby. Therefore it was not permissible for him to see Sawda bint Zam‘a, and he is not among her mahrams. And Allah knows best. (1)
Source pages p. 385

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exposition of a specific type of objection (i‘tirād) in jadal: objecting by differing narration of a hadith through changing a vowel (ḥaraka). The author presents two examples from fiqh. The first example: a Shafi‘i or Hanbali uses the hadith 'ذكاه الجنين ذكاة أمه' (the slaughter of the fetus is the slaughter of its mother) to argue that the fetus is made permissible by the mother's slaughter. The Hanafi objects by noting that the narration can be read with a fatha on the tā’ of 'ذكاةً', making it indefinite and meaning 'like the slaughter of its mother'. This creates uncertainty whether it is the same or similar, so the proof is suspended until preponderance (tarjīḥ) is established. Abu Hanifa’s view is preferred because the default is prohibition until certainty of slaughter is achieved. The second example involves a Maliki argument that a slave-woman becomes a bed (firāsh) by intercourse, so her child is attributed to the master, citing the hadith of Sa‘d and ‘Abd ibn Zam‘a. The Hanafi objects that the narration says 'هو لك عبد بن زمعة' (he is yours), implying ownership, not paternity. The author then gives the answer (jawāb): the Hanafi objection fails because it is impossible that the claimant called him brother yet the Prophet gave him as a slave; also the Prophet's reasoning ('the child belongs to the bed') shows the child is not a slave. Moreover, the variant 'هو لك يا عبد بن زمعة' (he is yours, O ‘Abd ibn Zam‘a) explains the first version, as the vocative can be dropped. The instruction to Sawda to veil was because Zam‘a had not claimed him; thus only ‘Abd’s acknowledgment established a right to inheritance, not lineage. The passage illustrates how variant readings can block certainty in legal argumentation.

Key Terms
الاعتراض باختلاف الرواية
إبدال حركة مكان حركة
الترجيح

Checkpoint
In the first example of objection by differing narration, what is the effect of the Hanafi’s claim that the hadith can be read as 'ذكاةً' with fatha?
A) It proves that the fetus is not permissible.
B) It creates uncertainty, so the proof is suspended until preponderance is established.
C) It shows that the hadith is weak and cannot be used.
D) It indicates that the mother’s slaughter is sufficient.

Key Takeaway
Variant vowel readings can introduce uncertainty into a legal proof, requiring the debater to resort to tarjīḥ based on other principles.

Objection by Differing Narration: The Slave-Woman Example and Its Refutation
Author gives second example of objection and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the strength of a jadal objection by examining the response (jawāb) and identifying how variant readings are resolved.

Core Idea
Not every variant narration destroys a proof; sometimes the variant is explained by a preferred reading that preserves the original meaning.

In the second example, the author presents a more complex exchange where the Hanafi objects using a variant reading, but the author provides a detailed jawāb that defends the Maliki position. This lesson walks through the objection and its refutation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Maliki mudda'i's proof
The Maliki argues that a slave-woman becomes a bed (firāsh) by intercourse, so her child is attributed to the master, citing the hadith of Sa‘d and ‘Abd ibn Zam‘a where the Prophet said: 'الولد للفراش'.
2
Identify the Hanafi mu'tarid's objection
The Hanafi objects that the narration reads 'هو لك عبد بن زمعة' without the vocative 'يا', implying the child is given as a slave, not as a free son.
This is an objection by differing narration through a word change (dropping of the vocative).
3
Analyse the author's jawāb
The author gives two arguments: (1) It is impossible that the claimant called him brother yet the Prophet gave him as a slave; (2) The Prophet's reasoning 'الولد للفراش' shows the child is not a slave. Additionally, the variant 'هو لك يا عبد بن زمعة' with vocative explains the first version.
The author uses the principle of 'al-akhdh bil-zā'id' (accepting the additional) to prefer the fuller reading.
4
Assess the outcome
The Hanafi objection is refuted; the Maliki proof remains valid because the jawāb resolves the ambiguity.

Objection by Explicit Abrogation (Naskh) in Jadal
Author defines a type of jadal objection by claiming that a hadith has been abrogated, with one fiqh example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify an objection by claimed explicit abrogation in a jadal exchange and evaluate its validity based on the transmission of an abrogating evidence.

Core Idea
Claiming abrogation is a valid dialectical move only when supported by explicit transmission.

In dialectical debate (jadal), one powerful way to invalidate an opponent's proof from a hadith is to claim that the hadith has been abrogated (naskh). But not every claim of abrogation is accepted – the objector must provide clear evidence that the ruling was later superseded. This lesson breaks down how an explicit naskh objection works, using the classical dispute between a Zahiri and a Maliki over the obligation of ghusl.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original argument (mudda'i)
The Zahiri argues that ghusl is not required after sexual contact without emission, citing the hadith 'إنما الماء الماء' (water is only by water). This hadith limits ghusl to cases where water (semen) is emitted.
This is the point being challenged by the objector.
2
Identify the objector's claim (mu'tarid)
The Maliki objects by claiming that this hadith is abrogated (mansukh). He does not attack the authenticity or interpretation; he attacks its legal force by stating it has been superseded.
The objection is not against the text itself, but against its current applicability.
3
Examine the evidence for abrogation
The Maliki provides an explicit transmission: Ubayy ibn Ka'b reported that the ruling was a concession (rukhsa) in early Islam, later abrogated by the command to perform ghusl unconditionally. This meets the condition that the abrogation be explicit (ṣarīḥ).
Without such evidence, the objection would be weak.
4
Evaluate the objection's validity in jadal
Since the objector provided a clear transmitted report of abrogation, the objection is valid. The debate would now shift to accepting or rejecting that abrogation evidence. If accepted, the Zahiri's proof collapses.
This shows how naskh objections function as a tool to nullify a proof by showing it is no longer operative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المطلب الخامس الاعتراض على الاستدلال بمتن السنة بدعوى النسخ
وهو من عدة وجوه :
أ - أن ينقل المعترض النسخ صريحًا :
مثاله : أن يستدل الظاهري في ترك الغسل من التقاء الختانين، بما روي عن النبي أنه قال : (إنما الماء الماء) (2) من
فيعارضه المالكي قائلا : إن هذا منسوخ والدليل على ذلك ما روي عن أبي بن كعب أنه قال : أن الفتيا التي كانوا يفتون أن الماء من الماء كان رخصة رخصها رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم في بدء الإسلام ثم أمر بالاغتسال بعد (3)
The fifth demand: Objection to argument from the text of the Sunna by claiming abrogation. It is of several aspects: A – That the objector transmits abrogation explicitly. Example: The Zahiri argues for omitting ghusl from the meeting of the two circumcised parts, by what is narrated from the Prophet that he said: 'Water is only [required] by water' (2). The Maliki opposes him saying: This is abrogated, and the evidence for that is what is narrated from Ubayy ibn Ka‘b that he said: 'The fatwa they used to give that water is by water was a concession permitted by the Messenger of Allah at the beginning of Islam, then he commanded ghusl afterwards' (3).
Source pages p. 386

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical classification of another type of objection in jadal: objecting to an argument from a hadith by claiming that the hadith has been abrogated (naskh). The author states that this objection has several aspects; the first is when the objector explicitly transmits an abrogating evidence. The example given: a Zahiri argues that ghusl (ritual bath) is not required after sexual intercourse with only contact of the circumcised parts (without emission), based on the hadith 'إنما الماء الماء' (water is only by water), meaning ghusl is only required when water (semen) is emitted. The Maliki objector responds by claiming this hadith is abrogated, citing the report from Ubayy ibn Ka‘b that the original fatwa was a concession in early Islam, but later the Prophet commanded ghusl regardless of emission. Thus, the Zahiri’s proof is invalidated by the abrogation. The passage illustrates how naskh, when supported by explicit transmission, serves as a valid i‘tirāḍ in jadal.

Objecting to Sunnah Arguments by Claiming Abrogation
Author explains abrogation objection with two types and examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and reconstruct the two dialectical moves for objecting to a hadith-based argument by claiming abrogation, and analyze the responses available to the arguer.

Core Idea
Claiming abrogation requires evidence; the arguer can counter by redefinition or criticizing the narration.

When an opponent uses a hadith to support a legal ruling, a skilled mutakallim may object by claiming that the ruling has been abrogated by a later revelation or prophetic action. This passage from a classical jadal work classifies this objection into two types: an explicit claim of abrogation with supporting evidence, and an implicit claim by bringing a later contradictory report that cannot be reconciled. Through concrete examples from fiqh debates, you will learn how this dialectical tool is wielded and how it can be countered.

Guided Path
1
Identify the arguer and objector
In each example, find who is the mustadill (arguer presenting a hadith) and who is the mu'tarid (objector claiming abrogation). Note their madhhab or legal school, as it influences the dialectic.
For instance, in the first example the Zahiri argues for not requiring ghusl, and the Maliki objects.
2
Extract the claim of abrogation
What exactly is claimed to be abrogated? What evidence does the objector provide? Distinguish between explicit abrogation (type A) where the objector states 'this is abrogated' and gives proof, and implicit abrogation (type B) where he brings a later report that contradicts without explicitly saying 'abrogated'.
In type A, the objector often cites a later narration or event that abrogates the earlier ruling.
3
Analyze the response
How does the mustadill counter the objection? Look for strategies: redefining the subject (e.g., 'after tanning it is called leather, not skin'), or challenging the narration (e.g., questioning its authenticity or interpretation).
In the Shafi'i vs Hanbali example, the response redefines the term 'ihab' (skin) to exclude tanned leather.
4
Compare the two types
Notice the difference in the objector's approach: explicit claim requires direct proof of abrogation; implicit claim relies on the principle that the later report abrogates the earlier if reconciliation is impossible. Consider which type is stronger in debate.
The author notes that for implicit claims, the arguer may respond by bringing a counterexample or attacking the narration.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المطلب الخامس الاعتراض على الاستدلال بمتن السنة بدعوى النسخ
وهو من عدة وجوه :
أ - أن ينقل المعترض النسخ صريحًا :
مثاله : أن يستدل الظاهري في ترك الغسل من التقاء الختانين، بما روي عن النبي أنه قال : (إنما الماء الماء) من
فيعارضه المالكي قائلا : إن هذا منسوخ والدليل على ذلك ما روي عن أبي بن كعب أنه قال : أن الفتيا التي كانوا يفتون أن الماء من الماء كان رخصة رخصها رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم في بدء الإسلام ثم أمر بالاغتسال بعد
أمثلته كذلك أن يستدل الشافعي في طهارة جلود الميتة بالدبغ، بقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام: (أيما إهاب دبغ فقد طهر) فيقول الحنبلي معترضا : هذا منسوخ بما روي عن عبد الله بن عكيم أنه قال : أتانا كتاب رسول الله أن لا تنتفعوا من الميتة بإهاب ولا عصب
وهذا صريح في نسخ كل خبر ورد في طهارة الجلود بالدباغ.
فيقول الشافعي مجيبا : هذا عاد إلى تحريم الإهاب، وبعد الدباغ لا يسمى إهابا، لكن يسمى جلدًا أو أديما أو أفيقا.
ب - أن لا يدعي النسخ صريحًا ولكن ينقل ما يخالفه متأخرا عنه ولا يمكن الجمع بينهما : مثاله : أن يستدل الزهري بما روى أبو موسى الأشعري عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أنه قال : (توضؤوا مما مست النار) ، فيعارضه المالكي قائلا : هذا منسوخ، والدليل على ذلك ما روى محمد بن المنكدر عن جابر أنه قال : كان آخر الأمرين من رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ترك الوضوء مما مست النار . والطريق في الجواب عن مثل هذا أن ينقل المستدل معارضًا لما اعترض به على استدلاله، أو يقدح في الرواية إن وجد إلى ذلك سبيلا . أمثلته كذلك : أن يستدل الظاهري أو الحنبلي في جَلْد الثيب مع الرجم، بقوله : (خذوا عني خذوا عني قد جعل الله لهن سبيلا، البكر بالبكر جلد مائة ونفي سنة، والثيب بالثيب جلد مائة والرجم)
The fifth section: Objecting to argumentation using the text of the Sunnah by claiming abrogation. It has several aspects: A - That the objector conveys the abrogation explicitly. Example: The Zahiri argues for not performing ghusl from the meeting of the two circumcisions based on what was narrated from the Prophet (ﷺ) that he said: 'Water is only for water.' The Maliki objects saying: This is abrogated, and the evidence for that is what was narrated from Ubayy ibn Ka'b that he said: The fatwa they used to give that 'water is from water' was a concession granted by the Messenger of Allah (ﷺ) at the beginning of Islam, then he ordered ghusl afterwards. Another example: The Shafi'i argues for the purity of the skins of dead animals by tanning, based on his saying (ﷺ): 'Any skin that is tanned has become purified.' The Hanbali objects saying: This is abrogated by what was narrated from Abdullah ibn Ukaym that he said: The letter of the Messenger of Allah came to us that you should not benefit from a dead animal's skin or sinew. And this is explicit in abrogating every report that came regarding the purity of skins by tanning. The Shafi'i responds saying: This reverts to the prohibition of the skin, and after tanning it is not called a skin but rather it is called leather, hide, or tanned skin. B - That he does not claim abrogation explicitly but narrates what contradicts it, later in time, and reconciliation between them is impossible. Example: Al-Zuhri argues with what Abu Musa al-Ash'ari narrated from the Prophet (ﷺ) that he said: 'Perform wudu from what fire has touched.' The Maliki objects saying: This is abrogated, and the evidence for that is what Muhammad ibn al-Munkadir narrated from Jabir that he said: The last of the two matters from the Messenger of Allah (ﷺ) was abandoning wudu from what fire has touched. The method of answering such is that the arguer brings a counterexample to what he objected to his argument, or he criticizes the narration if he finds a way to do so. Another example: The Zahiri or Hanbali argues for flogging the previously married person along with stoning, based on his saying: 'Take from me, take from me, Allah has made a way for them: the virgin with the virgin: one hundred lashes and exile for a year; the previously married with the previously married: one hundred lashes and stoning.'
Source pages p. 386

Commentary
This passage from a work on jadal (dialectical argumentation) explains a specific dialectical move: objecting to an argument that relies on a Prophetic hadith by claiming that the hadith has been abrogated (naskh). The author classifies this objection into two types. The first type (أ) is when the objector explicitly claims abrogation and provides evidence for it. Two examples are given: the first involves the hadith 'Water is only for water' (meaning no ghusl required except after ejaculation) and the Maliki objector cites Ubayy ibn Ka'b's report that this was a temporary concession later abrogated. The second example involves the hadith about tanning purifying skins; the Hanbali objector cites a letter from the Prophet prohibiting use of dead animal skins, which is explicit in abrogating earlier reports. The response from the Shafi'i is to argue that after tanning, the item is no longer called a skin (ihab) but leather, so the prohibition does not apply. The second type (ب) is when the objector does not explicitly claim abrogation but brings a later report that contradicts the evidence and cannot be reconciled. The example given is the hadith about performing wudu after eating food touched by fire, and the Maliki objector cites Jabir's report that the last practice of the Prophet was to abandon wudu for that. The author then explains the method of responding: the arguer can either bring a counter-example to the objector's argument or criticize the narration if possible. The passage ends with another example: the hadith about flogging the previously married person along with stoning. This passage is a typical expansion of a dialectical rule, providing concrete fiqh examples to illustrate how the move is applied in practice. It emphasizes that claiming abrogation must be supported by evidence and that the respondent has strategies to counter the objection.

المعترض
المستدل

Key Takeaway

Refuting Naskh by Terminological Distinction
Author gives an example of answering a naskh claim by terminological distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and apply the jadal method of rejecting a naskh claim by clarifying the subject matter's terminology.

Core Idea
Abrogation can be refuted by showing the subject has changed its definition.

In jadal, one common way to challenge an opponent's claim of abrogation is to argue that the term in question does not apply to the same thing. Here, an opponent asserts that a report on the purity of hides via tanning has been abrogated. Imam al-Shafi'i responds not by denying the possibility of naskh, but by clarifying that 'ihab' (hide) only refers to the untanned skin; after tanning, it is called 'jild' (leather). Thus, the alleged abrogated report does not apply to tanned leather. This lesson walks through that logical move.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opponent's claim
The opponent claims that all reports on the purity of hides by tanning are abrogated. That is the mudda'a (assertion) being challenged.
2
Locate Shafi'i's jawab (response)
Shafi'i responds by redefining the subject. He does not deny the abrogation itself, but argues that the abrogated report concerns 'ihab', not what becomes after tanning.
3
Spot the terminological distinction
The key is that 'ihab' (hide) is the untanned skin. After tanning, it takes different names: jild, adim, afiq. So the abrogated report does not cover the tanned product.
4
Assess how the distinction refutes the naskh claim
The opponent's naskh claim fails because it assumes the subject remains the same. By showing the change of name/status after tanning, the report's applicability is preserved without negation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
صريح في نسخ كل خبر ورد في طهارة الجلود بالدباغ. فيقول الشافعي مجيبا : هذا عاد إلى تحريم الإهاب، وبعد الدباغ لا إهابا، لكن يسمى جلدًا أو أديما أو أفيقا (1).
Explicit in abrogating every report that came regarding the purity of hides by tanning. So al-Shafi'i responds: This returns to the prohibition of the hide, and after tanning it is not a hide, but rather it is called leather, or leather, or leather (1).
Source pages p. 387

Commentary
This passage presents a dialectical example where an opponent claims that a report about the purity of hides through tanning is abrogated. Shafi'i's response does not deny the possibility of abrogation but rather refutes it by pointing out a terminological shift: the term 'ihab' (hide) applies only to the untanned skin; after tanning, it is called 'jild' (leather), 'adim', or 'afiq'. Thus, the abrogated report (if it existed) would concern the hide, not the tanned leather. This is a typical method in jadal of resolving apparent conflict by clarifying the subject matter.

Key Takeaway
A naskh claim can be answered without denying abrogation itself, by showing that the subject has changed its meaning.
