Responding to Implied Naskh in Jadal
Author explains a type of naskh objection and how to respond with mu'arada or riwaya critique.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify an objection based on a later conflicting report without explicit naskh and apply the two response strategies: mu'arada (counter-evidence) and qadh fi al-riwaya (impugning the narration).

Core Idea
A later report does not automatically abrogate; it can be countered or its reliability questioned.

In juristic debate, an opponent may cite a later report that contradicts your evidence without explicitly claiming abrogation. This is a common tactic that requires a precise response. This lesson teaches you to recognize such objections and deploy the two approved methods from usul al-fiqh: either bring a later counter-report (mu'arada) or criticize the narration itself (qadh fi al-riwaya).

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection type
Look for an opponent who cites a report later in time than your evidence, claims the two cannot be reconciled, but does not use the word 'naskh' explicitly. This is an implied abrogation objection.
In the first example, al-Zuhri's evidence is a command to do wudu; the Maliki objects with a later report that the Prophet left wudu.
2
Assess reconciliation possibility
Check whether the two reports can be harmonized (jam'). If yes, the objection fails. If not, you must respond using one of the two methods.
In both examples, the objector claims reconciliation is impossible, setting the stage for a response.
3
Apply mu'arada (counter-evidence)
Bring a report that is also later and contradicts the opponent's report, thereby neutralizing their claim of abrogation. Your report must be at least as strong as theirs.
The response in the wudu example: the proponent can bring another report that shows wudu was not abandoned.
4
Apply qadh fi al-riwaya (impugning the narration)
If the opponent's report can be criticized for incompleteness or error, argue that the negation ('he did not flog') is a negative statement that the narrator cannot fully verify. Positive evidence (action) outweighs negative reports.
In the thayyib example, the response argues the narrator only witnessed stoning and missed the flogging due to forgetfulness, supported by Ali's practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ب - أن لا يدعي النسخ صريحًا ولكن ينقل ما يخالفه متأخرا عنه ولا يمكن الجمع بينهما : مثاله : أن يستدل الزهري بما روى أبو موسى الأشعري عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أنه قال : (توضؤوا مما مست النار ) (2) ، فيعارضه المالكي قائلا : هذا منسوخ، والدليل على ذلك ما روى محمد بن المنكدر عن جابر أنه قال : كان آخر الأمرين من رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ترك الوضوء مما مست النار (3) . والطريق في الجواب عن مثل هذا أن ينقل المستدل معارضًا لما اعترض به على استدلاله، أو يقدح في الرواية إن وجد إلى ذلك سبيلا (4) . أمثله كذلك : أن يستدل الظاهري أو الحنبلي في جَلْد الثيب مع الرجم، بقوله : (خذوا عني خذوا عني قد جعل الله لهن سبيلا، البكر بالبكر جلد مائة ونفي سنة، والثيب بالثيب جلد مائة والرجم) (5) . فيعارضه الحنفي أو الشافعي قائلا : هذا منسوخ بما روي أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم رجم ماعزا ولم يجلده (1)، وهذا كان متأخرا عن الأول (2) والجواب : أن قوله في خبركم ولم يجلده نفي لا يحيط به الراوي، وعساه شاهد الرجم خاصة فروى ما شاهد وعساهم سبقوا إلى رجمه ذهولا عن الجلد، فسقط بالسهو عنه، يوضح هذا ما روي أن عليا كرم وجهه جلد شراحة الهمذانية يوم الخميس، ورجمها يوم الجمعة ثم قال: «جلدتها بكتاب الله، ورجمتها بسنة نبي الله» (3) ، فكان هذا من علي رضي الله عنه رواية وعملا، وهو إثبات معه رواية، وخبركم نفي مع احتمال فبقي لفظ رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم في الجمع الجلد والرجم ،بحاله فلا ينسخ بمحتمل متردد
B - That he does not claim abrogation explicitly, but cites something that contradicts it, later than it, and reconciliation between them is not possible. Its example: That al-Zuhri argues with what Abu Musa al-Ash'ari narrated from the Prophet (peace be upon him) that he said: 'Perform wudu from what fire has touched' (2), so the Maliki opposes him saying: This is abrogated, and the evidence for that is what Muhammad ibn al-Munkadir narrated from Jabir that he said: 'The last of the two matters from the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) was abandoning wudu from what fire has touched' (3). And the way to answer such a thing is that the proponent brings a counter-evidence to what he objected to with his argument, or impugns the narration if he finds a way to that (4). And likewise examples: That the Zahiri or Hanbali argues concerning flogging the thayyib along with stoning, with his statement: 'Take from me, take from me; Allah has made a way for them: the virgin with the virgin: one hundred lashes and exile for a year, and the thayyib with the thayyib: one hundred lashes and stoning' (5). The Hanafi or Shafi'i opposes him saying: This is abrogated by what is narrated that the Prophet (peace be upon him) stoned Ma'iz and did not flog him (1), and this was later than the first (2). And the answer: That his statement in your report 'and he did not flog him' is a negation that the narrator cannot encompass; perhaps he witnessed the stoning only and narrated what he witnessed, and perhaps they preceded to his stoning forgetful of the flogging, so it fell due to forgetfulness. This is clarified by what is narrated that Ali (may Allah honor his face) flogged Sharaha al-Hamadhaniyya on Thursday and stoned her on Friday, then said: 'I flogged her by the Book of Allah and stoned her by the Sunnah of the Prophet of Allah' (3). So this from Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) is both narration and action, and it is an affirmation with narration, while your report is a negation with possibility. So the wording of the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) regarding combining flogging and stoning remains as it is, so it is not abrogated by a probable, uncertain (report).
Source pages p. 387

Commentary
This passage presents a type of objection in jadal where the opponent does not explicitly claim abrogation (naskh) but cites a later report that contradicts the proponent's evidence, and the two cannot be reconciled. Two examples are given: the first concerns wudu from fire-touched food; al-Zuhri cites a command to do wudu, while the Maliki objects with a later report from Jabir that the Prophet left off wudu. The proper response is either to bring a counter-evidence (mu'arada) that is also later, or to impugn the narration (qadh fi al-riwaya) if possible. The second example concerns the punishment for a thayyib (previously married adulterer): the Zahiri/Hanbali argues that the Prophet prescribed both flogging and stoning; the Hanafi/Shafi'i objects that the later case of Ma'iz (stoning only) abrogates that. The detailed answer shows that the narrator's report of 'did not flog' is a negation that may be due to incomplete observation or forgetfulness, as supported by the action of 'Ali who combined both punishments. Thus, the report of negation cannot abrogate the explicit command that includes both.

Key Terms

Dialectic -- Jadal

Jadal: Answering an Objection of Abrogation with Epistemic Weakening
Author presents an objection claiming abrogation of a hadith and gives a detailed answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyse a dialectical answer (jawab) that weakens an objection by pointing out the epistemic limits of a negative report.

Core Idea
A negative report cannot overturn a positive one if the narrator might have missed details.

In this passage, the author defends a hadith that prescribes both flogging and stoning for adultery against an objection that it was abrogated by a later report of stoning without flogging. The answer uses epistemological reasoning: the narrator's silence about flogging does not prove its absence. This is a classic jadal move where the responder 'weakens' the evidential force of the objection. By the end, you will be able to identify such a stratagem.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection (mu'tarid)
Find the exact claim: the Hanafi or Shafi'i opponent says the hadith is abrogated by a later report that the Prophet stoned Ma'iz without flogging.
The objection relies on chronological priority (later action abrogates earlier ruling).
2
Extract the answer's epistemic critique
The answer argues that the negative report ('he did not flog him') is epistemically weak because the narrator may not have witnessed the flogging, or the Companions may have been forgetful.
The term 'nafy' (negation) is used; the answer claims it cannot encompass all details.
3
Examine the supporting evidence from Ali
The answer cites Ali's action: flogging on Thursday and stoning on Friday, confirming that both punishments are intended. This is a positive report (ithbat) with practice.
Ali's statement 'I flogged by the Book and stoned by the Sunnah' reinforces the combined ruling.
4
Determine the outcome by jadal rules
The objector's evidence is probable (zanni) and uncertain, while the original hadith is explicit (nas). Therefore the objection fails; the ruling remains.
This illustrates the principle 'what is established with certainty is not removed by doubt'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فيعارضه الحنفي أو الشافعي قائلا : هذا منسوخ بما روي أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم رجم ماعزا ولم يجلده (1)، وهذا كان متأخرا عن الأول (2) والجواب : أن قوله في خبركم ولم يجلده نفي لا يحيط به الراوي، وعساه شاهد الرجم خاصة فروى ما شاهد وعساهم سبقوا إلى رجمه ذهولا عن الجلد، فسقط بالسهو عنه، يوضح هذا ما روي أن عليا كرم وجهه جلد شراحة الهمذانية يوم الخميس، ورجمها يوم الجمعة ثم قال: «جلدتها بكتاب الله، ورجمتها بسنة نبي الله» (3) ، فكان هذا من علي رضي الله عنه رواية وعملا، وهو إثبات معه رواية، وخبركم نفي مع احتمال فبقي لفظ رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم في الجمع الجلد والرجم ،بحاله فلا ينسخ بمحتمل متردد
The Hanafi or Shafi'i might object saying: This is abrogated by what was narrated that the Prophet stoned Ma'iz and did not flog him, and this was later than the first. And the answer: That his statement in your report 'and he did not flog him' is a negation that the narrator cannot comprehensively know; perhaps he only witnessed the stoning and narrated what he witnessed, and perhaps they hastened to stone him forgetful of the flogging, so it was omitted due to forgetfulness. This is clarified by what was narrated that Ali flogged Sharaha al-Hamadhaniyya on Thursday and stoned her on Friday and then said: 'I flogged her by the Book of Allah and stoned her by the Sunnah of the Prophet', so this from Ali was a narration and practice, and it is an affirmation with a narration, while your report is a negation with probability, so the words of the Prophet regarding combining flogging and stoning remain as they are and cannot be abrogated by a probable uncertain [report].
Source pages p. 388

Commentary
This passage presents a dialectical exchange typical in jadal. The Hanafi or Shafi'i opponent objects that a hadith prescribing both flogging and stoning for adultery is abrogated by a later hadith that reports the Prophet stoned Ma'iz without flogging. The answer (jawab) uses several arguments: first, a negative report (لا يحيط به الراوي) is epistemologically weak because the narrator may not have witnessed the flogging. Second, the narrator may have only seen part of the event. Third, the Companions might have forgotten to flog due to preoccupation. To support this, the answer cites Ali's action: he flogged first then stoned, showing that both are intended. Thus the positive report of combining both is retained, and a probable negative cannot abrogate it. This demonstrates the principle that a explicit (نص) is not superseded by a speculative (ظني) report.

Key Terms
نسخ
جواب
نفي

Checkpoint

B) Because a negative report cannot cover all details and is speculative
C) Because stoning was never practiced
D) Because the earlier hadith is from the Quran

Key Takeaway
In jadal, a negative report (nafy) is inherently weaker than a positive one (ithbat) when the narrator's comprehensiveness is uncertain.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Consider a scenario: Someone objects to the hadith of hand amputation for theft by citing a report that the Prophet did not amputate a particular thief. Using the jadal technique from this lesson, write a brief answer that weakens the objection by pointing out the epistemic limits of the negative report.

Objection by Claiming Abrogation through Companions' Practice
Author illustrates an objection that a hadith is abrogated because Companions acted contrary to it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze an objection that claims abrogation based on Companions' practice and identify the role of inferred consensus in such a dialectical argument.

Core Idea
Companions' practice when known and uncontested can ground a claim of abrogation in jadal.

In dialectical debates over hadith, one powerful objection is to claim abrogation by the Companions' practice. This lesson walks through a concrete example from zakat on camels, showing how the Hanafi's proof from a hadith is met by the opponent's argument that the hadith is abrogated because Abu Bakr and Umar acted differently, and the rest of the Companions remained silent. We will dissect the logical structure of this objection and see how inferred consensus becomes the linchpin.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original claim (mudda'i)
Find the Hanafi's argument in the passage. What hadith does he use, and what ruling does he derive? The Hanafi uses the hadith that after 120 camels, the obligation restarts with one sheep per five camels.
The Hanafi is the one making the initial case; the objection comes from the opponent.
2
Identify the objection (mu'tarid)
Locate the opponent's claim: the first hadith is abrogated. On what basis? The opponent cites another hadith with different rates and argues that Abu Bakr and Umar acted on that second hadith, not the first.
The objection is that the first hadith is no longer operative.
3
Analyze the evidence for abrogation
The opponent uses two pieces of evidence: (1) the practice of Abu Bakr and Umar acting on the second hadith, and (2) the silence of other Companions (sukut) which is taken as approval, forming consensus (ijma'). Trace how the author presents this reasoning.
Silence in the face of a known action is a classic way to infer consensus in usul al-fiqh.
4
Evaluate the objection's strength
Consider if the evidence really establishes abrogation. The opponent assumes that the Companions' practice shows the second hadith was the operative one. But does silence necessarily mean consensus? This is where the dialectic could continue.
In jadal, the strength of this objection can be challenged by questioning whether the consensus is certain or whether the first hadith might have been abrogated by a specific ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ج - أن يعترض عليه بدعوى النسخ لعمل الصحابة بخلافه : وذلك مثل استدلال الحنفي في مسألة استئناف الفريضة في زكاة الإبل بقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام : (فإذا زادت الإبل على عشرين ومائة، استؤنفت الفريضة في كل خمس شاة)(5)، فيقول المالكي أو الشافعي أو الحنبلي: إن ثبت هذا الحديث، فهو منسوخ بما روي أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قال : (إذا زادت الإبل على مائة وعشرين، ففي كل أربعين بنت لبون، وفي كل خمسين حقة) (6)، والدليل على أنه منسوخ ما ثبت أن أبا بكر وعمر رضي الله عنهما عملا بالخبر الثاني، ولم يعملا بالخبر الأول الدال على الاستئناف، وهذا مما لا يخفى على الصحابة كلهم العمل به ولم ينكره منهم منكر، فثبت أنه إجماع منهم على العمل بالخبر الثاني باعتباره ناسخا للأول(1)
J - That one objects to it by claiming abrogation due to the Companions' practice contrary to it. For example the Hanafi's argument in the issue of restarting the obligation in zakat of camels by his saying: 'When camels exceed one hundred and twenty, the obligation is restarted with every five a sheep', so the Maliki or Shafi'i or Hanbali says: If this hadith is established, then it is abrogated by what was narrated that the Prophet said: 'When camels exceed one hundred and twenty, then for every forty a bint labun, and for every fifty a hiqqah', and the evidence that it is abrogated is that Abu Bakr and Umar acted by the second report and did not act by the first report indicating restarting, and this action was not hidden from all the Companions and no one denied it, so it is established that it is a consensus on acting by the second report considering it as abrogating the first.
Source pages p. 388

Commentary
This passage presents a second type of objection in jadal: claiming abrogation based on the Companions' practice. The example is from zakat on camels. The Hanafi uses a hadith that sets a rate of one sheep for every five camels after 120. The opponent (Maliki, Shafi'i, or Hanbali) argues that this hadith is abrogated by another hadith specifying rates of bint labun and hiqqah. The proof of abrogation is that Abu Bakr and Umar practiced the second hadith and not the first, and this was known to all Companions without objection, thus constituting consensus (ijma') on the second as abrogating the first. This illustrates how jadal uses the concept of consensus as evidence for abrogation.

Key Terms
عمل الصحابة
إجماع
زكاة الإبل
نسخ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The first hadith contradicts the Qur'an
B) Abu Bakr and Umar acted on the second hadith, and no Companion objected
C) The second hadith is more authentic than the first
D) The Prophet himself explicitly abrogated the first hadith

Key Takeaway
In jadal, a claim of abrogation can be supported by showing that the Companions practiced a contrary ruling and that their silence indicates consensus, but the strength of this argument depends on the certainty of the consensus.

Debating the Binding Force of Previous Revelations
Author presents an objection that a hadith is based on previous scripture and abrogated, with answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the dialectical exchange regarding shar' man qablana in usul al-fiqh and evaluate the strength of the response.

Core Idea
The status of previous revelations is settled by prophetic action, not by textual preservation.

This passage presents a classic jadal exchange in usul al-fiqh. A Shafi'i or Hanbali jurist uses a hadith of the Prophet stoning two Jews to argue for stoning a married dhimmi. The Hanafi objector claims the Prophet was acting based on the Torah, which is abrogated by Islamic law. The author provides a two-part answer that defends the hadith's evidential value. Understanding this exchange sharpens your ability to analyse how objections are raised and answered in Islamic legal dialectic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claimant's position and evidence
The Shafi'i or Hanbali argues that a married dhimmi should be stoned for zina, citing the hadith that the Prophet stoned two Jews. Locate this claim in the passage and note the type of evidence used (prophetic action).
2
Extract the objector's challenge
The Hanafi objects that the Prophet's action was based on the Torah, which is abrogated by Islamic law. The objection thus denies the hadith's authority for Muslims. Identify the grounds of the objection: reliance on previous scripture that is no longer valid.
This is a common objection when a ruling seems to derive from pre-Islamic revelation.
3
Analyse the first part of the answer: the principle of shar' man qablana
The author states 'شرع من قبلنا شرع لنا ما لم نعلم نسخه' — previous revelations remain binding for Muslims unless abrogation is known. This principle is a key tool in usul al-fiqh. The Prophet's action confirms the ruling's applicability, not its repeal.
The three positions on shar' man qablana: (1) it is our sharia, (2) it is not, (3) it is if affirmed by our sharia. The author adopts the first.
4
Analyse the second part: corruption of the Torah prevents reliance
The Torah available in the Prophet's time had been altered (tabdil), so the Prophet would not have acted based on it as a general source. His specific stoning action therefore could not be imitation of a corrupted text; it was a new divine command for the Muslim community. This strengthens the argument that the hadith is valid evidence.
The passage cuts off at 'لا', but the logic is clear: corruption of earlier scripture means prophetic actions cannot be attributed to following that scripture.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
د - الاعتراض على الاستدلال بالحديث بدعوى أنه من شرع من قبلنا وأنه منسوخ بشرعنا : وذلك مثل أن يستدل الشافعي أو الحنبلي في رجم الذمي المحصن، وعدم اعتبار إسلامه، بما روي أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم رجم يهوديين زنيا بعد إحصانهما(2) . فيقول الحنفي معترضا : إنما رجمهما بحكم التوراة فإنه أمر بإحضار التوراة، ثم عمل بذلك، وشرعنا قد نسخ حكم التوراة (3) . والجواب عن هذا الاعتراض، أن شرع من قبلنا شرع لنا ما لم نعلم نسخه، ولأن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم عمل به، فدل على أنه شرع لنا (4) ثم إن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم لا يثق بنقل هذه التوراة لما تطرق إليها من التبديل والتغيير فيها، فهذا يمنع النبي أن يعمل بحكمها في الجملة، لا
D - Objecting to using the hadith as evidence by claiming it is from the preceding revelation and abrogated by our Sharia: For example, the Shafi'i or Hanbali argues for stoning the protected dhimmi who is married, and not considering his Islam, by what was narrated that the Prophet stoned two Jews who committed adultery after being married. So the Hanafi objects: He stoned them only by the judgment of the Torah, for he ordered to bring the Torah, then acted accordingly, and our Sharia has abrogated the judgment of the Torah. And the answer to this objection is that the Sharia of those before us is a Sharia for us as long as we do not know its abrogation, and because the Prophet acted upon it, this indicates it is a Sharia for us. Moreover, the Prophet did not trust the transmission of this Torah due to the alteration and change that had occurred in it, and this prevents the Prophet from acting by its judgment in general, no...
Source pages p. 389

Commentary
This passage presents a third objection: that a hadith is actually from previous scriptures and therefore abrogated by Islamic law. The example is stoning a married dhimmi. The Shafi'i or Hanbali uses the hadith that the Prophet stoned two Jews. The Hanafi objects that the Prophet acted based on the Torah, which is abrogated. The answer gives two points: first, the principle that previous revelations remain valid for us unless abrogated; second, the Prophet's action itself indicates its validity. Additionally, the Torah at the Prophet's time was corrupted, so he would not have relied on it as a general source, but his specific action shows it was sanctioned for that case. The answer is cut off, but the main refutation is clear.

Key Terms
شرع من قبلنا (shar' man qablana): The revealed laws before Islam; debated whether they are binding upon Muslims if not abrogated. [Note: In usul al-fiqh, there are three positions: it is our sharia, it is not, or it is if affirmed by our sharia.]
تبديل (tabdil): Alteration of scripture; here, the Torah was corrupted, so it cannot be a source.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the first part of the answer to the objection that the stoning of Jews was abrogated?
A) The Torah is completely false and cannot be used at all
B) Previous revelations are valid for us unless abrogated
C) The Prophet never used the Torah, so the objection is invalid
D) The hadith is weak and cannot be used as evidence

Key Takeaway
The authority of previous revelations for Muslims is determined by prophetic endorsement, not by textual integrity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Using the two-part answer from this lesson, construct a response that defends the prohibition's validity.

Abrogation via Consensus of Companions
Author argues that practice of Abu Bakr and Umar constitutes consensus on abrogation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the structure of the dialectical argument from consensus (ijma') for abrogation (naskh) used in usul al-fiqh.

Core Idea
Silent practice of the Companions with universal knowledge and no objection is binding consensus.

This lesson examines a classic argument in usul al-fiqh: how the practice of Abu Bakr and Umar, known to all Companions and met with no objection, establishes the principle of abrogation between conflicting reports. The passage demonstrates the logical move from observed behavior to consensus (ijma'), a key source of Islamic law. Understanding this argument helps you see how early jurists derived legal principles from community practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim
What is the author trying to prove? The conclusion is that the later report abrogates the earlier one. Look for the term 'naskh' and the final sentence.
2
Extract the evidence
What specific practice is cited? Abu Bakr and Umar acted upon the second report and did not act upon the first report indicating postponement. Note that the first report is described as indicating postponement (al-istinaf).
3
Analyze the consensus mechanism
How does the author move from their practice to general consensus? The practice was known to all Companions, and none objected. This silence implies tacit approval. Identify the phrase 'lam yunkirhu minhum munkir' (no denier denied it).
The logic: universal knowledge + absence of objection = tacit consensus.
4
Evaluate the conclusion
Is this a valid argument for abrogation? What conditions are necessary for such consensus to be binding? Consider whether the silence truly indicates agreement, and whether the practice of two Companions is sufficient for consensus.
This step requires understanding of usul al-fiqh conditions for ijma'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
منسوخ ما ثبت أن أبا بكر وعمر رضي الله عنهما عملا بالخبر الثاني، ولم يعملا بالخبر الأول الدال على الاستئناف، وهذا مما لا يخفى على الصحابة كلهم العمل به ولم ينكره منهم منكر، فثبت أنه إجماع منهم على العمل بالخبر الثاني باعتباره ناسخا للأول(1).
Abrogated: It is established that Abu Bakr and Umar, may Allah be pleased with them, acted upon the second report and did not act upon the first report, which indicated postponement. This is something whose application is not hidden from all the Companions, and no denier among them denied it. So it is established that it is a consensus from them to act upon the second report as abrogating the first.
Source pages p. 389

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical argument establishing the principle of abrogation (naskh) through the consensus (ijma') of the Companions. The author presents a proof: Abu Bakr and Umar practiced according to a later report (al-khabar al-thani) and ignored an earlier report that indicated postponement (al-istinaf). This practice was known to all Companions, and none objected. Their silence is taken as tacit approval, forming a consensus. The conclusion is that the later report abrogates the earlier one. This is a common argument in usul al-fiqh for recognizing abrogation through the behavior of the early community.

Key Terms
إجماع
نسخ
خبر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the practice of Abu Bakr and Umar considered evidence for abrogation?
A) Because they explicitly stated the earlier report was abrogated.
B) Because all the Companions knew of their practice and no one objected.
C) Because the first report was weak in transmission.
D) Because the second report was from a more reliable narrator.

Key Takeaway
The practice of notable Companions, when universally known and not objected to, constitutes a tacit consensus that establishes the abrogation of an earlier report.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Identify the premises and conclusion, and determine if the consensus claim is valid based on the same conditions (universal knowledge, absence of objection).

Responding to the Objection of Abrogated Prior Law
Dialectical refutation of an objection based on prior revelation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the dialectical exchange regarding shar' man qablana and evaluate the strength of the Hanafi objection and the response.

Core Idea
The law of previous prophets remains valid unless abrogated, and the Prophet's action proves it was not abrogated.

This passage from a usul al-fiqh work presents a typical jadal exchange between a Hanafi and a Shafi'i/Hanbali over the validity of using a hadith that seems to rely on the Torah. The Hanafi objects that the Prophet's stoning was based on the Torah, which is abrogated. The response defends the hadith by invoking the principle of shar' man qablana and the Prophet's intent to expose a lie.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
The Shafi'i/Hanbali uses a hadith about stoning Jewish adulterers as evidence for stoning a dhimmi muhsan. The Hanafi objects that the hadith relies on the abrogated Torah.
2
Extract the objection's core claim
The objection claims the Torah's ruling is abrogated by Islamic law, so the hadith cannot be used as evidence.
3
Analyze the response's first part
The response states that shar' man qablana is valid unless abrogated, and the Prophet's action shows it was not abrogated.
This is a general principle in usul al-fiqh.
4
Analyze the response's second part
The Prophet did not trust the Torah due to alterations; he used it to expose the Jews' lie about the punishment, not to derive law.
This shows the hadith is independent of the Torah's authority.
5
Assess who wins
By the rules of jadal, the responder successfully defends the hadith by showing the objection fails on two grounds.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
د - الاعتراض على الاستدلال بالحديث بدعوى أنه من شرع من قبلنا وأنه منسوخ بشرعنا: وذلك مثل أن يستدل الشافعي أو الحنبلي في رجم الذمي المحصن، وعدم اعتبار إسلامه، بما روي أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم رجم يهوديين زنيا بعد إحصانهما(2). فيقول الحنفي معترضا: إنما رجمهما بحكم التوراة فإنه أمر بإحضار التوراة، ثم عمل بذلك، وشرعنا قد نسخ حكم التوراة (3). والجواب عن هذا الاعتراض، أن شرع من قبلنا شرع لنا ما لم نعلم نسخه، ولأن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم عمل به، فدل على أنه شرع لنا (4). ثم إن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم لا يثق بنقل هذه التوراة لما تطرق إليها من التبديل والتغيير فيها، فهذا يمنع النبي أن يعمل بحكمها في الجملة، لا باعتبارها شرعا لموسى ولا شرعا له هو موافق لموسى، وإنما استدعى التوراة وسايرهم في كذبهم إذ قالوا إن عقوبة الزنا التحميم ليبين كذبهم فيما أخبروا به وفيما كتموه من ذكره في التوراة (1).
D - Objection to reliance on the hadith by claiming that it is from the law of those before us and that it is abrogated by our law: For example, the Shafi'i or Hanbali may argue for stoning the protected dhimmi who is muhsan, and not considering his Islam [as preventing stoning], based on the narration that the Prophet stoned two Jews who committed adultery after their ihsan. The Hanafi objects saying: He only stoned them by the ruling of the Torah, for he ordered the Torah to be brought, then he acted upon that, and our law has abrogated the ruling of the Torah. The answer to this objection: that the law of those before us is law for us as long as we do not know its abrogation, and because the Prophet acted upon it, so that indicates it is law for us. Then the Prophet does not trust the transmission of this Torah due to the alteration and change that has affected it. This prevents the Prophet from acting by its ruling in general, not considering it as law for Moses nor as law for him that agrees with Moses. Rather, he requested the Torah and went along with their lie when they said the punishment for adultery is disfigurement, to demonstrate their lie in what they reported and in what they concealed of its mention in the Torah.
Source pages p. 389

Commentary
This passage presents a dialectical exchange (jadal) regarding the use of a hadith that seems to rely on the Torah (the law of previous prophets). The first part describes the objection: a Hanafi argues that the Prophet's stoning of the Jewish adulterers was based on the Torah, which has been abrogated by Islamic law, so the hadith cannot be used as evidence. The answer (jawab) has two parts. First, a general principle: the law of previous prophets (shar' man qablana) remains valid for us unless we know it has been abrogated. The Prophet's own action confirms it is still in effect. Second, the Prophet did not actually trust the Torah due to alterations; rather, he called for the Torah to expose the Jews' lie about the punishment (they falsely claimed it was disfigurement, when it was actually stoning). Thus, the hadith is evidence in itself, not dependent on the Torah. The response defends the validity of using the hadith without accepting the objection of abrogation.

Key Terms
شرع من قبلنا
نسخ
تبديل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The Torah is abrogated so the hadith is invalid.
B) Shar' man qablana is law unless abrogated, and the Prophet's action shows it wasn't abrogated.
C) The Prophet never used the Torah.
D) The Torah was not revealed by God.

Key Takeaway
The law of previous prophets remains a valid source unless we know it has been abrogated, and the Prophet's actions can confirm its continued validity.

Objection by Ta'wil al-Zahir: Interpreting Commands as Recommendation
Explanation of ta'wil al-zahir objection with two fiqh examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the structure of a ta'wil objection and construct a basic jadal exchange using ta'wil in a fiqh dispute.

Core Idea
Ta'wil al-zahir shifts a command from obligation to recommendation — and the holder of apparent meaning must rebut.

This passage from a classical usul text introduces a specific type of objection in dialectic: ta'wil al-zahir. When one scholar argues that a command implies obligation, another may object by interpreting it as recommendation. The original claimant must then defend the apparent meaning by refuting the objector's proofs. Let's see how this works in two real fiqh examples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claimant's argument
In each example, one scholar (the mudda'i) uses a hadith command to argue for obligation. Find the hadith and the claimed legal ruling.
The Maliki uses the command 'Order him to take her back' to argue that taking back a wife after divorce during menstruation is obligatory.
2
Locate the objection and its ta'wil
The opponent (mu'tarid) does not deny the hadith but interprets the command as recommendation. Identify the alternative interpretation and the evidence given.
The Hanafi objects that the command is for recommendation (nadb) and provides proofs.
3
Determine the claimant's response
The original claimant must refute the objector's evidence to preserve the apparent meaning of obligation. What is that response?
The Maliki answers by refuting the Hanafi's proofs (naqd adillat al-mu'tarid).
4
Assess the dialectical status
Who bears the burden of proof? The claimant initially relies on apparent meaning; once the opponent provides a valid interpretation, the claimant must defend. Is the objection valid? The text does not settle, but the structure is clear.
The outcome depends on the strength of the refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المطلب السادس : الاعتراض على الاستدلال بالسنة من جهة التأويل وهو على نوعين : أ - تأويل الظاهر : وذلك مثل أن يستدل المالكي على وجوب الرجعة على من طلق حائضًا، بما روي عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أنه قال لعمر حين طلق ابنه عبد الله زوجته وهي حائض: (مره فليراجعها حتى تطهر ) (2)، فقد أمره بالرجعة، والأمر يقتضي الوجوب. فيعترض الحنفي قائلا : ينبغي أن يحمل على الندب والاستحباب.. ويذكر أدلة تثبت هذا التأويل، وموقف المالكي أن يجيب بنقض أدلة المعترض، ليسلم له حمل الأمر على ظاهره من الوجوب ومن ذلك أيضًا استدلال الحنفي في إيجاب غسل الثوب من المني بقوله : (إن كان رطبا فاغسليه وإن كان يابسًا فحكيه) (4)، وقد اعترض الشافعية والحنابلة على هذا الاستدلال بحمله على الندب.
Sixth Section: Objection to reliance on the Sunnah from the angle of interpretation. It is of two types: A - Interpretation of the apparent meaning: For example, the Maliki may argue for the obligation of taking back (raj'a) for one who divorces his wife while she is menstruating, based on what is narrated from the Prophet that he said to Umar when his son Abdullah divorced his wife while she was menstruating: 'Order him to take her back until she becomes pure.' So he commanded the taking back, and a command implies obligation. The Hanafi objects saying: It should be interpreted as recommendation (nadb) and desirability. And he mentions proofs establishing this interpretation. The Maliki's position is to answer by refuting the proofs of the objector, so that his interpretation of the command on its apparent meaning of obligation is accepted. Also from that is the Hanafi's argument for the obligation of washing a garment from semen by his saying: 'If it is wet, wash it; and if it is dry, scratch it off.' The Shafi'is and Hanbalis objected to this argument by interpreting it as recommendation.
Source pages p. 390

Commentary
This passage introduces a new type of dialectical objection: ta'wil al-zahir, interpreting an apparent meaning away from its default. The author gives two examples. First, the Maliki argues that the Prophet's command to take back a wife after a divorce during menstruation indicates obligation (wujub). The Hanafi objects that the command should be interpreted as recommendation (nadb), providing evidence for his interpretation. The Maliki responds by refuting the Hanafi's evidence to preserve the apparent obligatory meaning. Second, the Hanafi argues that the Prophet's command to wash a garment (if wet) and scratch (if dry) indicates obligation to wash off semen. The Shafi'is and Hanbalis object by interpreting the command as recommendation. This shows how ta'wil is used to shift the legal consequence from obligation to recommendation. The passage illustrates the dialectical process: one party presents a claim based on apparent meaning, the opponent offers an alternative interpretation, and the claimant must defend the apparent meaning.

Key Terms
تأويل الظاهر
أمر
ندب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The hadith is weak in transmission.
B) The command should be interpreted as recommendation, not obligation.
C) The divorce was valid and no taking back is needed.
D) The hadith was abrogated.

Key Takeaway
Ta'wil al-zahir is a legitimate dialectical move that shifts the apparent meaning of a command from obligation to recommendation, requiring the original claimant to rebut the objector's evidence.

Objection by Interpreting the Apparent Meaning (Ta'wil al-Zahir)
Defines and illustrates objection by interpreting the apparent meaning of a hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and reconstruct an objection by ta'wil al-zahir in a fiqh debate.

Core Idea
Ta'wil al-zahir does not reject the text but challenges its apparent implication with alternative evidence.

When a jurist infers a ruling from a prophetic command, an opponent may object not by denying the hadith, but by arguing that the apparent meaning should be interpreted differently. This is the objection by ta'wil al-zahir. The passage below illustrates this with three classic examples. By studying them, you will learn how such objections are structured and how they are answered.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proponent's claim
In each example, the proponent cites a hadith and argues that its apparent command indicates a specific ruling (e.g., obligation). Find the ruling and the hadith.
For the first example, the Maliki claims obligation of raj'a based on the Prophet's order.
2
Locate the objector's challenge
The objector accepts the hadith but offers a non-literal interpretation (e.g., recommendation) supported by other evidence. Identify the alternative meaning and its evidence.
The Hanafi objects that the command is recommendation and provides proofs.
3
Determine the proponent's response
The proponent refutes the objector's evidence to restore the literal meaning. How does the author present this response?
The Maliki's response is to rebut the Hanafi's proofs to preserve the obligation claim.
4
Apply the pattern to the remaining examples
Analyze the garment-washing and shuf'a examples following the same structure: claim, objection, response.
In the shuf'a example, the Hanbali interprets the hadith as recommendation, citing the criterion of partnership.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المطلب السادس : الاعتراض على الاستدلال بالسنة من جهة التأويل وهو على نوعين : أ - تأويل الظاهر : وذلك مثل أن يستدل المالكي على وجوب الرجعة على من طلق حائضًا، بما روي عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أنه قال لعمر حين طلق ابنه عبد الله زوجته وهي حائض: (مره فليراجعها حتى تطهر ) ، فقد أمره بالرجعة، والأمر يقتضي الوجوب. فيعترض الحنفي قائلا : ينبغي أن يحمل على الندب والاستحباب.. ويذكر أدلة تثبت هذا التأويل، وموقف المالكي أن يجيب بنقض أدلة المعترض، ليسلم له حمل الأمر على ظاهره من الوجوب ومن ذلك أيضًا استدلال الحنفي في إيجاب غسل الثوب من المني بقوله : (إن كان رطبا فاغسليه وإن كان يابسًا فحكيه) ، وقد اعترض الشافعية والحنابلة على هذا الاستدلال بحمله على الندب أمثلة هذا النوع من الاعتراض أيضا ، استدلال الحنفي في استحقاق الشفعة بالجوار بقول النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام: (الجار أحق بصقبه) . فيقول الحنبلي معترضا : هو محمول على العرض عليه استحبابا، والإحسان لجواره، بدليل أن الشفعة لا تستحق إلا بالشركة، فيجمع بين هذا ما وبين أنه قضى بالشفعة فيما لم يقسم، فإذا وقعت الحدود وصرفت الطرق فلا شفعة»
The sixth section: Objection to inference from the Sunnah by way of interpretation (ta'wil). It is of two types: A - Interpretation of the apparent meaning: Such as when the Maliki argues for the obligation of taking back (رجعة) one who divorced his wife while she was menstruating, based on what was reported from the Prophet, peace be upon him, that he said to ‘Umar when his son ‘Abdullah divorced his wife while she was menstruating: 'Order him to take her back until she becomes pure.' So he commanded the taking back, and a command implies obligation. Then the Hanafi objects saying: It should be interpreted as recommendation (nadb) and desirability... and he mentions proofs that support this interpretation. The position of the Maliki is to respond by refuting the evidence of the objector, so that his interpretation of the command on its apparent meaning of obligation remains valid. Also from that is the Hanafi's inference that washing the garment from semen is obligatory by his statement: 'If it is wet, wash it; if it is dry, scrape it.' The Shafi‘is and Hanbalis objected to this inference by interpreting it as recommendation. Also examples of this type of objection: the Hanafi's inference that pre-emption is due by neighborly proximity by the Prophet's statement, peace be upon him: 'The neighbor is more entitled to his adjacent property.' Then the Hanbali objects saying: It is interpreted as offering it to him by way of recommendation and kindness to his neighbor, evidenced by the fact that pre-emption is not due except by partnership, so the reconciliation between this and the fact that he judged pre-emption in undivided property is that when boundaries are set and roads are drawn, there is no pre-emption.
Source pages p. 390

Commentary
This passage discusses the first type of objection to inference from the Sunnah, namely interpretation of the apparent meaning (ta'wil al-zahir). The author defines it and provides three examples from fiqh debates. In each example, the objector does not reject the prophetic report but argues that the apparent command should be interpreted as recommendation (nadb) rather than obligation (wujub). For instance, the Maliki argues that the Prophet's command to take back a divorced wife during menstruation indicates obligation, but the Hanafi objects by interpreting it as recommended, citing other evidence. Similarly, the Hanafi's inference that semen requires washing the garment is met by Shafi‘i and Hanbali objection interpreting the command as recommended. The third example involves pre-emption by neighbors; the Hanbali objector argues that the hadith is interpreted as a recommendation to offer the property to the neighbor, because pre-emption is only established through partnership. The author presents each objection and notes that the proponent's response is to refute the evidence for the alternative interpretation. This shows that objections based on ta'wil require both parties to present evidence for their preferred interpretation.

Key Terms
تأويل الظاهر
الوجوب
الندب
الشفعة
الرجعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The general principle that commands indicate obligation.
B) The explicit wording of the hadith 'Order him to take her back'.
C) The consensus of the Companions.
D) Analogy with other obligations.

Key Takeaway
Objection by ta'wil does not reject the hadith but offers an alternative interpretation; the debate then shifts to evidence supporting the literal vs. non-literal reading.

Objection by Specifying the General (Takhsis al-‘Umum)
Explains and exemplifies objection by specifying a general ruling to exclude certain cases.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when an objection is using takhsis al-‘umum and formulate the appropriate response to preserve the general proof.

Core Idea
Specifying the general does not reject the proof but restricts its scope based on counter-evidence.

In dialectical argumentation, one common objection is to claim that a general textual proof should be narrowed by another evidence. This objection does not deny the general principle but argues for its limitation. Understanding how to handle such objections is crucial for defending inferences from general texts in Islamic legal debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general statement
Find the general (ʿāmm) in the proof. In the example, it is the hadith 'Whoever changes his religion, kill him' which covers all apostates, male and female.
2
Recognize the objector's specification evidence
The objector (Hanafi) provides evidence to restrict the general: analogy with original disbelief and a hadith linking killing to fighting. Note that the objection does not reject the general hadith but claims it should be specified to exclude women.
3
Determine the objector's basis for specification
Examine the type of evidence: qiyas (analogy) and another text (another hadith). The key is that the objector must bring a valid dalil for takhsis.
4
Formulate the proponent's response
The proponent must refute the objector's evidence to uphold the generality. This can be done by showing the analogy is weak or the second hadith is not applicable. The passage says 'refute the objector's evidence so that his inference by the general remains valid.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ب - تخصيص العموم : والاعتراض بهذا ليس فيه أكثر من تبيين الموضع الذي يعمل فيه التخصيص العموم، ويذكر دليل التخصيص. وذلك مثل أن يستدل المالكي أو الشافعي أو الحنبلي في وجوب قتل المرتدة، بقول النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم : من بدل دينه فاقتلوه، وهو عام في الرجال والنساء. فيقول الحنفي معترضا : أخصه بالدليل وهو أن هذه الردة كفر لكن لا تقتل به المرأة، تماما كالكفر الأصلي، وقد ثبت قوله عليه الصلاة والسلام: (ما بالها قتلت وهي لا تقاتل)، فكأن علة القتل المقاتلة لا مجرد الكفر . والطريق للمستدل في الجواب عن هذا الاعتراض أن يرد دليل المعترض ليسلم له الاستدلال بالعموم
B - Specification of the general (takhsis al-‘umum): Objection by this is nothing more than clarifying the locus where specification of the general applies, and mentioning the evidence of specification. An example is when the Maliki, Shafi'i, or Hanbali argues for the obligation of killing an apostate woman by the Prophet's statement, peace be upon him: 'Whoever changes his religion, kill him.' This is general including men and women. Then the Hanafi objects saying: I specify it by evidence, which is that this apostasy is disbelief, but the woman is not killed for it, just like original disbelief. And it has been established by his statement, peace be upon him: 'What is wrong with her? She was killed while she was not fighting.' So as if the cause of killing is fighting, not mere disbelief. The method for the proponent in answering this objection is to refute the objector's evidence so that his inference by the general remains valid.
Source pages p. 391

Commentary
This passage presents the second type of objection to Sunnah inference: specification of the general (takhsis al-‘umum). The author explains that this objection does not challenge the general meaning itself but points out that it should be restricted by another evidence. The example given is the hadith 'Whoever changes his religion, kill him,' which is general for all apostates, including women. The Hanafi objects by specifying it to exclude women, based on two pieces of evidence: first, apostasy is disbelief but original disbelief does not warrant killing women, and second, a hadith indicating that the cause of killing is fighting, not mere disbelief. Thus, the general command to kill apostates is specified to only those who fight (men). The proponent's response is to refute the objector's evidence for specification, thereby preserving the general ruling. This demonstrates that objections by takhsis require engaging with the evidence for specification rather than simply rejecting the general proof.

Key Terms
تخصيص العموم
العموم
المرتدة
دليل التخصيص
القتل

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Consensus of the companions.
D) The fact that women are not legally responsible.

Key Takeaway
An objection by takhsis does not reject the general proof but demands its restriction, and the response must refute the evidence for that restriction.

Counter-argument by Text (Mu'arada bil-Nutq) - Classification and Example
Classifies counter-arguments by text into known and probable, then into more general, more specific, or equal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a counter-argument by text (mu'arada bil-nutq) as more general, more specific, or equal to the original proof, and apply the appropriate resolution.

Core Idea
In mu'arada, specific texts override general ones; both can be reconciled by application.

In Islamic dialectical debates (jadal), one powerful technique is to counter an opponent's textual proof with another text that seems to contradict it. But not all counter-texts are equal. This lesson shows how scholars classify counter-arguments by text based on their scope relative to the original proof, and how they resolve apparent conflicts by applying the specific over the general.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original proof and the counter-text
Locate the two texts in the exchange. In the shighar example, the Maliki's original proof is the hadith forbidding shighar; the Hanafi's counter-text is the Qur'anic verse permitting marriage.
2
Determine the logical relationship of scope
Compare the scope: Does the counter-text cover more cases (a'amm), fewer cases (akhass), or the same range (mithl)? The verse is general (covers all marriages), while the hadith is specific to shighar.
3
Apply the rule of specificity
If the counter-text is more general, the specific original proof remains operative in its limited scope; the general text applies elsewhere. If more specific, the specific counter-text restricts the general proof. Here, the Maliki argues his specific hadith governs shighar, and the general verse applies to other marriages.
4
Evaluate the strength of the response
Check whether the respondent's rebuttal harmonises both texts without discarding one. The Maliki's response is strong because it uses both texts, preserving their authority.

المطلب السابع : الاعتراض على الاستدلال بالسنة من جهة المعارضة وهو على نوعين : المعارضة بالنطق والمعارضة بالعلة : أ - المعارضة بالنطق : وهو إما أن يكون معلوما من جهة القرآن أو خبر التواتر، أو مظنونا كخبر الأحاد: -1 - المعارضة بالمعلوم: وهو لا يخلو أن يكون أعم من الدليل أو أخص منه أو مثله في العموم والخصوص. 1 - فالذي هو أعم منه، فمثل أن يستدل المالكي على تحريم نكاح الشغار بما روي عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أنه نهى عن نكاح الشغار والنهي يقتضي التحريم، فوجب أن يكون حراما . فيعارضه الحنفي بقوله تعالى : ﴿فَانكِحُوا مَا طَابَ لَكُم مِّنَ النِّسَاءِ مَثْنَى وَثُلَاثَ وَرُبَاعَ﴾، وهذا أعم، فيحمل على عمومه. وهنا يجيب المالكي مدافعا عن استدلاله : استدلالنا أولى، لأن خبرنا خاص وآيتكم عامة، فتحمل الآية في النكاح على غير وجه الشغار، ويحمل الخبر على الشغار، فتستعمل الآية والخبر جميعا، وهو أولى من اطراح الخبر لأنهما دليلان. 2 - والذي هو أخص منه، مثاله : أن يستدل الظاهري على أن الصغيرة المتوفى عنها زوجها لا عدة عليها بما روي عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أنه قال : (إنما الأعمال بالنيات وإنما لكل امرئ ما نوى) ، والصغيرة ليست ممن تصح منها
Seventh Section: Objection to inference from the Sunnah by way of counter-argument (mu'arada). It is of two types: counter-argument by text and counter-argument by cause. A - Counter-argument by text: It is either known from the Qur'an or mutawatir report, or probable like ahad reports. 1 - Counter-argument with known text: It is either more general than the (original) proof, or more specific, or equal in generality and specificity. (a) That which is more general: For example, the Maliki argues for prohibition of shighar marriage (exchange marriage) by the hadith that the Prophet forbade shighar, and prohibition implies prohibition, so it must be forbidden. The Hanafi counters with the verse: 'Marry what is good for you of women, two, three, or four.' This is more general, so it is carried upon its generality. Here the Maliki responds in defense of his proof: 'Our proof is worthier, because our report is specific and your verse is general, so the verse is carried upon marriage other than shighar, and the report is carried upon shighar. Thus both the verse and the report are used, and this is better than discarding the report because both are evidences.' (b) That which is more specific: For example, the Zahiri argues that a minor girl whose husband dies has no waiting period ('idda) based on the hadith: 'Actions are only by intentions, and every man gets what he intends.' And the minor is not among those from whom (intention is valid?) [Text cuts off]
Source pages p. 392

Commentary
This passage introduces a new section on objections to inference from the Sunnah by way of mu'arada (counter-argument). It divides mu'arada into two kinds: by text and by cause (illah). Here it focuses on mu'arada by text. The text used for counter-argument can be known (from Qur'an or mutawatir) or probable (ahad reports). Within the known type, the counter-text can be more general (a'amm), more specific (akhass), or equal (mithl) in scope compared to the original proof. The passage provides an example for the more general case: a Maliki argues that shighar marriage (exchange marriage) is forbidden because the Prophet forbade it. The Hanafi counters with the Qur'anic verse 'Marry what is good for you...' which is general and permits all marriages. The Maliki responds that his proof is specific to shighar and thus takes precedence over the general verse; both should be applied harmoniously. The passage then starts the example for the more specific case (a Zahiri using a hadith about intentions to argue that a minor girl has no 'idda) but cuts off. This illustrates how counter-arguments by text are handled: one must consider the relative generality and specificity.

المعتراضة بالنطق
أعم
أخص

Checkpoint
In the example of shighar marriage, how does the Maliki respond to the Hanafi's counter-argument using the general verse?
A) He accepts the verse as abrogating the hadith.
B) He argues that the specific hadith should prevail and both texts can be applied harmoniously.
C) He denies the authenticity of the verse.
D) He changes his proof.

Key Takeaway
In mu'arada bil-nutq, when the counter-text is more general, the specific original proof retains its force in its limited scope, and both texts are applied harmoniously.

Types of Mu'aradah: Opposition by Explicit Text in Jadal
Categorisation of opposition by explicit text into definite and probable with subcases

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between mu'aradah bi al-nutq and mu'aradah bi al-'illah, and classify mu'aradah bi al-nutq into ma'lum and maznun with their subcategories.

Core Idea
Opposition in jadal is structured by textual certainty and scope, not just contradiction.

In this passage, the author introduces the concept of mu'aradah as a method of objecting to an inference from the Sunnah. He divides it into two main types: opposition by explicit text and opposition by cause. Here we focus on the first type, which is further subdivided based on the certainty of the text and its relationship to the original proof. This classification is essential for understanding how scholars from different madhhabs engage in dialectical exchanges.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main types of mu'aradah
Look for the author's division at the start. What are the two categories?
The passage begins with 'المطلب السابع : الاعتراض على الاستدلال بالسنة من جهة المعارضة وهو على نوعين : المعارضة بالنطق والمعارضة بالعلة'.
2
Examine the subdivision of mu'aradah bi al-nutq
Notice that it splits based on certainty: ma'lum (Quran/mutawatir) and maznun (khabar al-ahad).
The author says 'وهو إما أن يكون معلوما من جهة القرآن أو خبر التواتر، أو مظنونا كخبر الأحاد'.
3
Understand the three subcategories for each certainty level
For each, the opposing text can be more general (a'amm), more specific (akhass), or equivalent.
The structure is repeated for both ma'lum and maznun.
4
Preview the examples for ma'lum
Read through the three examples to see how these subcategories operate in practice.
The examples cover the Maliki-Hanafi shighar, Zahiri-Maliki minor widow, and Hanafi/Shafi'i-Maliki predator meat disputes.

كخبر الأحاد: -1- المعارضة بالمعلوم: وهو لا يخلو أن يكون أعم من الدليل أو أخص منه أو مثله في العموم والخصوص. -1- فالذي هو أعم منه، فمثل أن يستدل المالكي على تحريم نكاح الشغار بما روي عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أنه نهى عن نكاح الشغار والنهي يقتضي التحريم، فوجب أن يكون حراما. فيعارضه الحنفي بقوله تعالى: ﴿فَانكِحُوا مَا طَابَ لَكُم مِّنَ النِّسَاءِ مَثْنَى وَثُلَاثَ وَرُبَاعَ﴾، وهذا أعم، فيحمل على عمومه. وهنا يجيب المالكي مدافعا عن استدلاله: استدلالنا أولى، لأن خبرنا خاص وآيتكم عامة، فتحمل الآية في النكاح على غير وجه الشغار، ويحمل الخبر على الشغار، فتستعمل الآية والخبر جميعا، وهو أولى من اطراح الخبر لأنهما دليلان. -2- والذي هو أخص منه، مثاله: أن يستدل الظاهري على أن الصغيرة المتوفى عنها زوجها لا عدة عليها بما روي عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أنه قال: (إنما الأعمال بالنيات وإنما لكل امرئ ما نوى)، والصغيرة ليست ممن تصح منها النية، فلم يكن لها عمل من عدة ولا غيرها. فيعارضه المالكي بقوله تعالى: ﴿وَالَّذِينَ يُتَوَفَّوْنَ مِنكُمْ وَيَذَرُونَ أَزْوَاجًا يَتَرَبَّصْنَ بِأَنفُسِهِنَّ أَرْبَعَةَ أَشْهُرٍ وَعَشْرًا﴾ وهذا خاص في موضع الخلاف، فيجب أن يقدم على عموم الخبر الذي احتججتم به، ولأنه لو تعارض الخبر والآية على وجه لا يمكن الجمع بينهما لكان الأخذ بالآية أولى لأنها معلومة، فإذا كان في الأخذ بالآية استعمال لهما فيحمل الخبر على غير العدة الأولى. -3- والذي مثله في العموم والخصوص مثاله: أن يستدل الحنفي والشافعي على تحريم لحوم السباع بقوله: (كل ذي ناب من السباع فأكله حرام) فيعارضه المالكي بقوله تعالى: ﴿قُل لَّا أَجِدُ فِي مَا أُوحِيَ إِلَى مُحَرَّمًا عَلَى طَاعِمٍ يَطْعَمُهُ إِلا أَن يَكُونَ مَيْتَةً أَوْ دَمًا مَّسْفُوحًا أَوْ لَحْمَ خِيَزِيرٍ﴾ فكل من المستدل والمعترض يدعي أن المطعومات الميتة، فيحمل خبركم على تحريم أكل ميتة السباع. أ-2- المعارضة بالمظنون: وهو خبر الآحاد ولا يخلو أيضًا أن يكون أعم من الدليل، أو أخص منه، أو مثله في الخصوص والعموم. -2-1- فالذي أعم منه، فمثاله أن يستدل المالكي على تحريم نكاح المرأة ...
Seventh section: Objection to inference by the Sunnah from the aspect of opposition, and it is of two types: opposition by explicit text and opposition by cause. A - Opposition by explicit text: It is either known from the Quran or mutawatir report, or probable like khabar al-ahad. 1 - Opposition by the known: It is inevitably either more general than the proof, or more specific than it, or equivalent in generality and specificity. 1 - What is more general than it: for example, the Maliki infers the prohibition of shighar marriage by what was narrated from the Prophet that he forbade shighar marriage, and prohibition necessitates prohibition, so it must be forbidden. The Hanafi opposes it with His saying: 'Marry what is pleasant to you of women, two and three and four' [Quran 4:3], and this is more general, so it is applied according to its generality. Here the Maliki responds defending his inference: Our inference is more worthy, because our report is specific and your verse is general, so the verse is interpreted for marriage in other than the manner of shighar, and the report is interpreted for shighar, so both the verse and the report are used, and that is better than discarding the report because both are proofs. 2 - What is more specific than it: for example, the Zahiri infers that the minor wife whose husband dies has no waiting period by what was narrated from the Prophet that he said: 'Actions are but by intentions, and every man gets what he intended', and the minor is not one who can have a valid intention, so she has no action of waiting period or otherwise. The Maliki opposes him with His saying: 'And those who are taken from among you and leave wives behind, let them wait for themselves four months and ten days' [Quran 2:234], and this is specific to the point of dispute, so it must be given priority over the generality of the report you argued with, and because if the report and the verse contradict in a way that cannot be reconciled, then taking the verse is more worthy because it is known. So if in taking the verse there is a use of both, then the report is interpreted for other than the first waiting period. 3 - What is equivalent in generality and specificity: for example, the Hanafi and Shafi'i infer the prohibition of eating predatory beast meat by his saying: 'Every fanged beast, eating it is forbidden.' The Maliki opposes him with His saying: 'Say: I do not find in what was revealed to me anything forbidden for a consumer to consume except that it be dead carcass or spilled blood or pig meat' [Quran 6:145]. So each of the proponent and opponent claims that what he adduced covers the point of dispute without what his opponent adduced. The Hanafi says: The report is more worthy because it is specific to predators and the verse is general to everything, so the general is based on the specific. And the Maliki says: The verse is more worthy because it is specific to lawful slaughter, from among the edible foods, so your report is interpreted as prohibiting eating the carcass of predators. A-2 - Opposition by the probable: it is khabar al-ahad, and it also is inevitably either more general than the proof, or more specific than it, or equivalent in specificity and generality. -2-1- What is more general than it: for example, the Maliki infers the prohibition of marrying a woman...
Source pages p. 392

Commentary
This passage from a work on jadal (dialectics) and usul al-fiqh explains the method of opposing an inference based on the Sunnah by way of mu'aradah (counter-evidence). The author first divides mu'aradah into two main types: opposition by explicit text (mu'aradah bi al-nutq) and opposition by cause (mu'aradah bi al-'illah). Then he focuses on the first type and further subdivides it based on the certainty of the text: definite (ma'lum) from Quran or mutawatir, and probable (maznun) like khabar al-ahad. For the definite type, the opposing text can be more general (a'amm), more specific (akhass), or equivalent in generality and specificity. He provides three examples from fiqh: the Maliki's use of the hadith prohibiting shighar (a type of marriage) countered by the Hanafi with the general verse about marrying women (Quran 4:3). The Maliki responds that his specific hadith should be preferred over the general verse. The second example: the Zahiri uses the hadith 'innama al-a'mal bi al-niyat' to argue that a minor widow has no 'iddah, because she cannot have intention. The Maliki counters with the specific verse about 'iddah (Quran 2:234) which applies to all widows, including minors. The third example: the Hanafi/Shafi'i use the hadith forbidding fanged beasts (predators), while the Maliki counters with the verse limiting forbidden foods (Quran 6:145) which does not include predators. The Maliki argues that the verse is specific to lawful slaughter and thus more appropriate. Then the author begins the second subtype: opposition by probable texts (khabar al-ahad), noting that it also divides into three similar categories, and gives the beginning of an example. The passage illustrates the practical application of jadal in usul al-fiqh, showing how scholars from different madhhabs use textual opposition to defend their positions.

Key Terms
المعارضة
المعلوم
المظنون
أعم
أخص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the classification of mu'aradah bi al-nutq, what does 'ma'lum' refer to?

B) Quran or mutawatir hadith
C) Khabar al-ahad
D) Consensus

Key Takeaway
Mu'aradah bi al-nutq is systematically classified by certainty and relative scope, enabling structured dialectical engagement.

Mu'aradah with a More General Text: The Shighar Marriage Example
Maliki vs. Hanafi on shighar marriage using general verse to oppose specific hadith

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze a jadal exchange where a general text is used to oppose a specific text, and evaluate the response that both can be reconciled.

Core Idea
A specific text can be preserved alongside a general text by restricting the general's application.

This example illustrates the first subcategory of mu'aradah bi al-ma'lum: when the opposing text is more general. The Maliki argues that shighar marriage is forbidden based on a specific hadith. The Hanafi opposes with a general Quranic verse permitting marriage. The Maliki responds by interpreting the verse to exclude shighar, thus using both texts. This exchange demonstrates the principle of reconciliation over outright rejection.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original proof (daliil)
The Maliki uses the hadith 'nahā 'an nikāḥ al-shighār'.
The hadith specifically mentions shighar.
2
Identify the opposing text (mu'aradah)
The Hanafi cites Quran 4:3: 'Fankihu ma tāba lakum min al-nisā'i'.
This verse is general about marriage.
3
Determine the relationship
The verse is a'amm (more general) than the hadith.
The author explicitly states 'وهذا أعم'.
4
Assess the response
The Maliki argues that the specific hadith restricts the general verse, so both are applied.
He says 'تحمل الآية على غير وجه الشغار، ويحمل الخبر على الشغار'.
5
Evaluate who has the stronger position
Consider the rules of jadal: is the Maliki's response valid?
Both texts are used, which is better than discarding one.

Seventh section: Objection to inference by the Sunnah from the aspect of opposition, and it is of two types: opposition by explicit text and opposition by cause. A - Opposition by explicit text: It is either known from the Quran or mutawatir report, or probable like khabar al-ahad. 1 - Opposition by the known: It is inevitably either more general than the proof, or more specific than it, or equivalent in generality and specificity. 1 - What is more general than it: for example, the Maliki infers the prohibition of shighar marriage by what was narrated from the Prophet that he forbade shighar marriage, and prohibition necessitates prohibition, so it must be forbidden. The Hanafi opposes it with His saying: 'Marry what is pleasant to you of women, two and three and four' [Quran 4:3], and this is more general, so it is applied according to its generality. Here the Maliki responds defending his inference: Our inference is more worthy, because our report is specific and your verse is general, so the verse is interpreted for marriage in other than the manner of shighar, and the report is interpreted for shighar, so both the verse and the report are used, and that is better than discarding the report because both are proofs. 2 - What is more specific than it: for example, the Zahiri infers that the minor wife whose husband dies has no waiting period by what was narrated from the Prophet that he said: 'Actions are but by intentions, and every man gets what he intended', and the minor is not one who can have a valid intention, so she has no action of waiting period or otherwise. The Maliki opposes him with His saying: 'And those who are taken from among you and leave wives behind, let them wait for themselves four months and ten days' [Quran 2:234], and this is specific to the point of dispute, so it must be given priority over the generality of the report you argued with, and because if the report and the verse contradict in a way that cannot be reconciled, then taking the verse is more worthy because it is known. So if in taking the verse there is a use of both, then the report is interpreted for other than the first waiting period. 3 - What is equivalent in generality and specificity: for example, the Hanafi and Shafi'i infer the prohibition of eating predatory beast meat by his saying: 'Every fanged beast, eating it is forbidden.' The Maliki opposes him with His saying: 'Say: I do not find in what was revealed to me anything forbidden for a consumer to consume except that it be dead carcass or spilled blood or pig meat' [Quran 6:145]. So each of the proponent and opponent claims that what he adduced covers the point of dispute without what his opponent adduced. The Hanafi says: The report is more worthy because it is specific to predators and the verse is general to everything, so the general is based on the specific. And the Maliki says: The verse is more worthy because it is specific to lawful slaughter, from among the edible foods, so your report is interpreted as prohibiting eating the carcass of predators. A-2 - Opposition by the probable: it is khabar al-ahad, and it also is inevitably either more general than the proof, or more specific than it, or equivalent in specificity and generality. -2-1- What is more general than it: for example, the Maliki infers the prohibition of marrying a woman...
Source pages p. 392

Commentary
This passage from a work on jadal (dialectics) and usul al-fiqh explains the method of opposing an inference based on the Sunnah by way of mu'aradah (counter-evidence). The author first divides mu'aradah into two main types: opposition by explicit text (mu'aradah bi al-nutq) and opposition by cause (mu'aradah bi al-'illah). Then he focuses on the first type and further subdivides it based on the certainty of the text: definite (ma'lum) from Quran or mutawatir, and probable (maznun) like khabar al-ahad. For the definite type, the opposing text can be more general (a'amm), more specific (akhass), or equivalent in generality and specificity. He provides three examples from fiqh: the Maliki's use of the hadith prohibiting shighar (a type of marriage) countered by the Hanafi with the general verse about marrying women (Quran 4:3). The Maliki responds that his specific hadith should be preferred over the general verse. The second example: the Zahiri uses the hadith 'innama al-a'mal bi al-niyat' to argue that a minor widow has no 'iddah, because she cannot have intention. The Maliki counters with the specific verse about 'iddah (Quran 2:234) which applies to all widows, including minors. The third example: the Hanafi/Shafi'i use the hadith forbidding fanged beasts (predators), while the Maliki counters with the verse limiting forbidden foods (Quran 6:145) which does not include predators. The Maliki argues that the verse is specific to lawful slaughter and thus more appropriate. Then the author begins the second subtype: opposition by probable texts (khabar al-ahad), noting that it also divides into three similar categories, and gives the beginning of an example. The passage illustrates the practical application of jadal in usul al-fiqh, showing how scholars from different madhhabs use textual opposition to defend their positions.

Key Terms
المعارضة
المعلوم
أعم
أخص

In the shighar example, what is the relationship between the hadith and the Quranic verse?

B) The hadith is more general
C) The verse is more general
D) They are equivalent

Key Takeaway
A specific text can be reconciled with a general one by restricting the general's application, preserving both proofs.

Mu'aradah with a More Specific Text: The Minor Widow's Iddah
Zahiri vs. Maliki on minor widow's iddah using specific verse to oppose general hadith

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to argue that a specific text overrides a general text when they are incompatible, and identify cases where reconciliation is impossible.

Core Idea
A specific text that directly addresses a case prevails over a general text that only indirectly applies.

Here we see the second subcategory: the opposing text is more specific (akhass). The Zahiri argues that a minor widow has no iddah based on the general principle that actions depend on intentions. The Maliki opposes with the specific verse about iddah, which applies to all widows. The Maliki argues that this specific verse must take precedence. This example shows that when a text is directly on point, it overrides a general principle.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original proof
The Zahiri uses the hadith 'innamā al-a'mālu bi al-niyyāt'.
This hadith is general about all actions requiring intention.
2
Identify the opposing text
The Maliki uses Quran 2:234 about iddah.
This verse specifically addresses the waiting period for widows.
3
Determine the relationship
The verse is akhass (more specific) than the hadith.
The author says 'وهذا خاص في موضع الخلاف'.
4
Analyze the priority claim
The Maliki says the specific verse should be taken because it is known (ma'lum) and directly addresses the case.
He also argues that if reconciliation is impossible, the verse is preferred due to certainty.
5
Evaluate the outcome
The Zahiri's argument is weakened because his general hadith does not specifically address the widow's iddah.
The specific verse overrides the general principle here.

المطلب السابع : الاعتراض على الاستدلال بالسنة من جهة المعارضة وهو على نوعين : المعارضة بالنطق والمعارضة بالعلة : أ - المعارضة بالنطق : وهو إما أن يكون معلوما من جهة القرآن أو خبر التواتر، أو مظنونا كخبر الأحاد: -1- المعارضة بالمعلوم: وهو لا يخلو أن يكون أعم من الدليل أو أخص منه أو مثله في العموم والخصوص. -1- فالذي هو أعم منه، فمثل أن يستدل المالكي على تحريم نكاح الشغار بما روي عن النبي صلى الله Mu'aradah with Equivalent Generality: The Predator Meat Dispute
Hanafi/Shafi'i vs. Maliki on predator meat with equivalently general texts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when two texts are equivalent in generality and scope, and analyze the arguments each side uses to claim specificity.

Core Idea
When texts are equally general, the debate shifts to which text is more specific in context, requiring deeper analysis.

The third subcategory involves texts that are equivalent in generality and specificity. In this example, the Hanafi and Shafi'i cite a hadith prohibiting fanged beasts, while the Maliki cites a verse limiting forbidden foods to three types. Both sides claim their text is more specific. The Hanafi argues the hadith is specific to predators; the Maliki argues the verse is specific to lawful slaughter. This exchange illustrates the complexity of determining textual priority when neither is clearly more general.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contrary texts
Hadith: 'Kullu dhī nābin min al-sibā‘i fa-akluhu harām'. Verse: 'Qul lā ajidu...' (6:145).
Both texts address what is forbidden to eat.
2
Assess their generality
Both appear to be general/specific in different ways.
The hadith is about predators; the verse lists three specific forbidden categories.
3
Compare each party's claim
Hanafi: hadith is specific to predators; Maliki: verse is specific to lawfully slaughtered meat.
Each claims their text is more specific to the dispute.
4
Analyze the Hanafi's argument
The hadith is about predators (specific category), the verse is about all foods (general).
Hanafi says 'الخبر أولى لأنه خاص في السباع والآية عامة'.
5
Analyze the Maliki's argument
The verse is about what is explicitly forbidden, excluding predators by omission, so it is specific in listing only three.
Maliki says 'الآية أولى لأنها خاصة في المذكى'.
