Seventh section: Objection to inference by the Sunnah from the aspect of opposition, and it is of two types: opposition by explicit text and opposition by cause. A - Opposition by explicit text: It is either known from the Quran or mutawatir report, or probable like khabar al-ahad. 1 - Opposition by the known: It is inevitably either more general than the proof, or more specific than it, or equivalent in generality and specificity. 1 - What is more general than it: for example, the Maliki infers the prohibition of shighar marriage by what was narrated from the Prophet that he forbade shighar marriage, and prohibition necessitates prohibition, so it must be forbidden. The Hanafi opposes it with His saying: 'Marry what is pleasant to you of women, two and three and four' [Quran 4:3], and this is more general, so it is applied according to its generality. Here the Maliki responds defending his inference: Our inference is more worthy, because our report is specific and your verse is general, so the verse is interpreted for marriage in other than the manner of shighar, and the report is interpreted for shighar, so both the verse and the report are used, and that is better than discarding the report because both are proofs. 2 - What is more specific than it: for example, the Zahiri infers that the minor wife whose husband dies has no waiting period by what was narrated from the Prophet that he said: 'Actions are but by intentions, and every man gets what he intended', and the minor is not one who can have a valid intention, so she has no action of waiting period or otherwise. The Maliki opposes him with His saying: 'And those who are taken from among you and leave wives behind, let them wait for themselves four months and ten days' [Quran 2:234], and this is specific to the point of dispute, so it must be given priority over the generality of the report you argued with, and because if the report and the verse contradict in a way that cannot be reconciled, then taking the verse is more worthy because it is known. So if in taking the verse there is a use of both, then the report is interpreted for other than the first waiting period. 3 - What is equivalent in generality and specificity: for example, the Hanafi and Shafi'i infer the prohibition of eating predatory beast meat by his saying: 'Every fanged beast, eating it is forbidden.' The Maliki opposes him with His saying: 'Say: I do not find in what was revealed to me anything forbidden for a consumer to consume except that it be dead carcass or spilled blood or pig meat' [Quran 6:145]. So each of the proponent and opponent claims that what he adduced covers the point of dispute without what his opponent adduced. The Hanafi says: The report is more worthy because it is specific to predators and the verse is general to everything, so the general is based on the specific. And the Maliki says: The verse is more worthy because it is specific to lawful slaughter, from among the edible foods, so your report is interpreted as prohibiting eating the carcass of predators. A-2 - Opposition by the probable: it is khabar al-ahad, and it also is inevitably either more general than the proof, or more specific than it, or equivalent in specificity and generality. -2-1- What is more general than it: for example, the Maliki infers the prohibition of marrying a woman...
Source pages p. 392

Commentary
This passage from a work on jadal (dialectics) and usul al-fiqh explains the method of opposing an inference based on the Sunnah by way of mu'aradah (counter-evidence). The author first divides mu'aradah into two main types: opposition by explicit text (mu'aradah bi al-nutq) and opposition by cause (mu'aradah bi al-'illah). Then he focuses on the first type and further subdivides it based on the certainty of the text: definite (ma'lum) from Quran or mutawatir, and probable (maznun) like khabar al-ahad. For the definite type, the opposing text can be more general (a'amm), more specific (akhass), or equivalent in generality and specificity. He provides three examples from fiqh: the Maliki's use of the hadith prohibiting shighar (a type of marriage) countered by the Hanafi with the general verse about marrying women (Quran 4:3). The Maliki responds that his specific hadith should be preferred over the general verse. The second example: the Zahiri uses the hadith 'innama al-a'mal bi al-niyat' to argue that a minor widow has no 'iddah, because she cannot have intention. The Maliki counters with the specific verse about 'iddah (Quran 2:234) which applies to all widows, including minors. The third example: the Hanafi/Shafi'i use the hadith forbidding fanged beasts (predators), while the Maliki counters with the verse limiting forbidden foods (Quran 6:145) which does not include predators. The Maliki argues that the verse is specific to lawful slaughter and thus more appropriate. Then the author begins the second subtype: opposition by probable texts (khabar al-ahad), noting that it also divides into three similar categories, and gives the beginning of an example. The passage illustrates the practical application of jadal in usul al-fiqh, showing how scholars from different madhhabs use textual opposition to defend their positions.

Key Terms
المعارضة
المعلوم
أعم
أخص

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Whether the hadith is authentic

Key Takeaway
When texts are equivalent in generality, the debate focuses on which text is more specific in context, requiring careful analysis of each text's application.

Mu'arada with Equal Generality: Hanafi vs Maliki on Predator Meat
Author gives an example of mu'arada where both proofs are equal in generality/specificity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyse a mu'arada exchange where both proofs claim specificity and determine how each side argues for priority.

Core Idea
When two proofs are equally specific, each side claims their proof is more particular.

This lesson examines a classic jadal example from usul al-fiqh. The Hanafi/Shafi'i school cites a hadith prohibiting predator meat; the Maliki counters with a Qur'anic verse. Each argues their proof is more specific to the case. You will learn to dissect such dialectical moves.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conflicting proofs
Find the hadith (specific to predators) and the verse (appears general) that the two parties use.
The hadith says 'every predator with a canine tooth' and the verse enumerates only three forbidden items.
2
Understand each side's claim to specificity
The Hanafi says the hadith is specific to predators. The Maliki says the verse is specific to slaughtered meat.
Both are claiming their proof is 'khass' to the case at hand.
3
Analyse the reasoning
The Hanafi applies the usuli principle: general is built on specific. The Maliki argues the verse is actually more specific because it restricts prohibition to dead meat.
Each uses the same principle of preferring the specific, but applies it differently.
4
Evaluate the strength
Consider which argument appears stronger and why. Note that the author does not declare a winner.
This is a dialectical tie; each side has a plausible claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
3 - والذي مثله في العموم والخصوص مثاله : أن يستدل الحنفي والشافعي على تحريم لحوم السباع بقوله : (كل) ذي ناب من السباع فأكله حرام) فيعارضه المالكي بقوله تعالى: ﴿قُل لَّا أَجِدُ فِي مَا أُوحِيَ إِلَى مُحَرَّمَا عَلَى طَاعِمٍ يَطْعَمُهُ إِلا أَن يَكُونَ مَيْتَةً أَوْ دَمًا مَّسْفُوحًا أَوْ لَحْمَ خِيزِيرِ ... فكل من المستدل والمعترض يدعي أن ما احتج به متناول لموضع الخلاف دون ما احتج به خصمه. فيقول الحنفي الخبر أولى لأنه خاص في السباع والآية عامة في كل شيء، فيُبنى العام على الخاص والمالكي يقول : الآية أولى لأنها خاصة في المذكى، من جملة المطعومات الميتة، فيحمل خبركم على تحريم أكل ميتة لأنه قد أخرج السباع
3 - And that which is similar in generality and specificity: its example is that the Hanafi and Shafi'i argue for the prohibition of the meat of predators by his saying: 'Every one with a canine tooth from among the predators, its eating is forbidden.' So the Maliki opposes him by His saying the Exalted: 'Say: I do not find in what has been revealed to me anything forbidden for an eater to eat except that it be dead meat or poured-out blood or flesh of swine...' So each of the arguer and the objector claims that what he has adduced encompasses the point of dispute and not what his opponent has adduced. So the Hanafi says: The hadith is more worthy because it is specific to predators, and the verse is general concerning everything, so the general is built upon the specific. And the Maliki says: The verse is more worthy because it is specific to what is slaughtered, from among the category of dead meats, so your hadith is interpreted as concerning the prohibition of eating dead meat because it has excluded the predators.
Source pages p. 393

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical example illustrating the type of mu'arada (objection) where the two conflicting proofs are equal in terms of generality and specificity. The author, following al-Baji's Minhaj, presents a concrete dispute between the Hanafi/Shafi'i school and the Maliki school regarding the prohibition of eating predator animals. The Hanafi/Shafi'i rely on a hadith stating that every predator with a canine tooth is forbidden. The Maliki objects by citing the Qur'anic verse from Surat al-An'am (6:145) which enumerates only carrion, blood, and pork as forbidden, implying that other things like predator meat are permissible. Each party claims that their proof is more specific to the case: the Hanafi argues that the hadith is specific to predators, while the verse is general (since it appears to permit everything else), so the general should be interpreted in light of the specific. The Maliki counters that the verse is specific to slaughtered animals (since it only mentions dead meat, not meat from lawful slaughter), and therefore the hadith should be understood as referring to dead predators (i.e., carrion), not those that are slaughtered. This exchange demonstrates how each side attempts to privilege its own proof by arguing that it is more particular to the issue at hand, a common move in legal dialectic.

الخاص
العام
المستدل
المعترض

The Principle of Specific vs. General Evidence in Dialectical Objections
Author presents first type of objection with Maliki-Zahiri example on marriage over aunt.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyse a dialectical objection (mu'arada) by identifying the specific and general evidence and evaluating which takes precedence in a jadal exchange.

Core Idea
A specific text that directly addresses the dispute overrides a general text that does not.

In Islamic legal dialectic (jadal), a common objection is to present a more general evidence that seems to contradict a specific proof. The key is to understand that specificity and direct applicability give priority. In this lesson, we examine a classic exchange between a Maliki and a Zahiri over the permissibility of marrying a woman over her aunt, and how the Maliki defends his specific hadith against a general encouragement to marry.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputants and their positions
The Maliki argues that marriage over a paternal or maternal aunt is prohibited. The Zahiri objects using a general hadith encouraging marriage. Note the conflict: specific prohibition vs. general permission.
2
Locate the specific evidence (nass)
The Maliki's evidence is the hadith 'Do not marry a woman over her paternal aunt nor over her maternal aunt.' This is a nass because it directly addresses the contested issue without probability of reinterpretation.
In usul, nass is a text with a clear, unambiguous meaning.
3
Identify the general evidence used as an objection
The Zahiri objects with the hadith 'O young men, whoever among you can afford marriage, let him marry...' This is general and does not specifically address the aunt situation.
General evidence applies broadly but may not override specific rulings.
4
Analyse the response: why does the specific evidence take precedence?
The Maliki argues that his evidence is superior because it is a nass directly covering the dispute. Moreover, his approach allows combining both hadiths: the general one is interpreted as applying to others (not aunts). The Zahiri's objection would require dropping the Maliki's hadith entirely, which is impermissible.
The principle is that specific evidence (khas) prevails over general evidence ('amm) when they conflict.
5
Understand the principle of jam' (reconciliation)
The Maliki reconciles the two evidences by restricting the general hadith to cases other than aunts. This preserves both texts. In jadal, the ability to reconcile strengthens one's argument.
Jam' is preferred over tarjih (preference) when possible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الجدل على عمتها بقوله : (لا تنكح المرأة على عمتها ولا على خالتها)(1)، فيعارضه الظاهري بقوله : (يا معشر الشباب من استطاع الباءة فليتروج فإنه أغض للبصر وأحصن للفرج)(2) فيقول المالكي مجيبا : ما قلناه أولى لأنه نص يتناول موضع الخلاف على وجه غير محتمل، ولأن فيما قلناه الجمع بين الخبرين لأنا نحمل خبركم على غير العمات (3) والخالات وما قلتم يؤدي إلى إسقاط ما رويناه عنه ، وذلك غير جائز )
Jadal: [The Maliki argues] with his saying: 'Do not marry a woman over her paternal aunt nor over her maternal aunt' (1). Then the Zahiri objects with his saying: 'O young men, whoever among you can afford marriage, let him marry, for it is more lowering of the gaze and more protective of the private parts' (2). Then the Maliki responds saying: What we said is more appropriate because it is a text that addresses the point of dispute in a non-probable manner, and because in what we said is the combination of the two reports, for we interpret your report as referring to other than aunts (3) and maternal aunts, and what you said leads to dropping what we have narrated from him, and that is not permissible.
Source pages p. 394

Commentary
This passage presents an example of a dialectical objection (mu'arada) where one piece of evidence is more general than the other. The Maliki uses a specific hadith prohibiting marriage to a woman over her paternal or maternal aunt. The Zahiri objects with a general hadith encouraging marriage. The Maliki responds that his evidence is superior because it is a clear text (nass) that directly addresses the contested issue without possibility of reinterpretation. He further argues that his approach allows reconciling both hadiths by interpreting the general encouragement as applying to cases other than aunts, whereas the Zahiri's objection would require abandoning the Maliki's hadith entirely, which is impermissible. The central idea is that a specific text that directly covers the case takes precedence over a general text that does not.

Key Terms
المعارضة
الخبر
النص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) His evidence is more general.
B) His evidence is more specific and directly addresses the dispute.
C) His evidence is agreed upon by all scholars.
D) He argues that the Zahiri's hadith is weak.

Key Takeaway
In jadal, specific evidence (nass) that directly addresses the dispute takes precedence over general evidence, especially when reconciliation (jam') is possible.

Objection with Specific Evidence in Jadal
Author presents second type with two fiqh examples: zakat and prayer times.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and evaluate an objection using more specific evidence (أخص) in a jadal exchange, and understand how the strength of evidence (consensus, authenticity) can influence the outcome.

Core Idea
Specific evidence normally overrides general, but consensus on authenticity can tilt the balance.

In dialectical debate (jadal), one common move is to counter a general proof with a more specific one that restricts or contradicts it. This lesson examines the second type of 'objection with specific evidence' as presented by the author, using two real fiqh debates: one on zakat thresholds and another on prayer times. By analyzing these examples, you will learn how jurists navigate the conflict between general and specific evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original general proof
In the first example, the Hanafi cites a Quranic verse that appears to mandate zakat on all agricultural produce ('what the sky waters, a tenth...'). This is a general ('amm) proof.
General proofs cover multiple cases without restriction.
2
Identify the objector's specific proof
The Maliki objects with a specific hadith that restricts zakat to amounts above five wasq. This is more specific (akhass) than the verse.
The objector uses a specific proof to negate the general application.
3
Analyze the proponent's response
The Hanafi responds by arguing that his general proof is agreed upon (muttafaq 'alayh) while the specific proof is disputed (mukhtalaf fih). He claims the agreed-upon general should take precedence over the disputed specific.
Consensus on authenticity can override the default preference for specific evidence.
4
Apply the same pattern to the second example
The Shafi'i uses a general hadith about making up missed prayers at any time. The Hanafi/Hanbali objects with specific prohibitions on prayer after Fajr and Asr. The Shafi'i then offers two responses: nullify the objection or seek preponderance (tarjih).
Notice how the principle of 'specific overrides general' is itself subject to counter-arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أ - 2 - 2 - والذي هو أخص منه : فمثاله أن يستدل الحنفي في إيجاب العشر من القليل والكثير بقوله الله : فيما سقت السماء العشر وفيما سقي بنضح أو غرب نصف العشر ) (4) ، فيقول المالكي معترضا : هذا يعارضه قوله : (ليس : في ما دون خمسة أوسق من التمر صدقة) (5) ، وهذا أخص منه، وطريق المستدل الحنفي في الجواب عن هذا الاعتراض أن يقول : إن خبرنا عام متفق عليه، وخبركم - وإن كان خاصا (1) مختلف فيه - مختلف فيه والعام المتفق عليه أولى من الخاص . ومثاله أيضًا : أن يستدل الشافعي في جواز صلاة لها سبب في أوقات النهي بقوله : (من نام عن صلاة أو نسيها فليصلها إذا ذكرها) (2) فيعارضه الحنفي أو الحنبلي بنهي النبي الله عن الصلاة في هذه الأوقات، وهو قوله عليه الصلاة والسلام: (لا صلاة بعد الصبح حتى ترتفع الشمس، ولا صلاة بعد العصر حتى تغيب الشمس) (3) ؛ فهذا خاص يقدم على العموم، لكونه نهيا مخصصا لأوقات، والخاص يقضي على العام. وجواب الشافعي عن هذا الاعتراض من وجهين أحدهما أن يسقط المعارضة، والثاني أن يرجح دليله بما يجد من وجوه الترجيحات
A - 2 - 2 - And that which is more specific than it: an example is that the Hanafi argues for the obligation of tithe on little and much with the saying of Allah: 'What the sky waters, a tenth, and what is watered by a bucket or a pulley, half a tenth' (4). Then the Maliki objects saying: This is contradicted by his saying: 'There is no charity on less than five wasq of dates' (5), and this is more specific than it. The way of the Hanafi in answering this objection is to say: Our report is general and agreed upon, and your report - even if it is specific (1) and disputed - is disputed, and the general agreed-upon is more appropriate than the specific. And another example of it: the Shafi'i argues for the permissibility of a prayer that has a cause at times of prohibition with his saying: 'Whoever sleeps through a prayer or forgets it, let him pray it when he remembers it' (2). Then the Hanafi or Hanbali objects with the Prophet's prohibition of prayer at these times, which is his saying, peace be upon him: 'No prayer after Fajr until the sun rises, and no prayer after Asr until the sun sets' (3); this is specific, to be given precedence over the general, because it is a prohibition specifying times, and the specific overrides the general. The Shafi'i's answer to this objection is from two aspects: one is to nullify the objection, and the second is to give preponderance to his evidence by the aspects of preference he finds.
Source pages p. 394

Commentary
This passage presents the second type of objection, where the objector's evidence is more specific (أخص) than the original proof. Two examples are given. First, the Hanafi uses a general Quranic verse about tithe (العشر) to argue that zakat is due on all agricultural produce, little or much. The Maliki objects with a specific hadith stating there is no zakat on less than five wasq. The Hanafi responds by arguing that his own hadith is general and agreed upon (متفق عليه), while the specific hadith is disputed, so the agreed-upon general evidence is stronger. Second, the Shafi'i uses a general hadith about making up missed prayers at any time. The Hanafi or Hanbali objects with specific prohibitions of prayer at certain times (after Fajr and Asr). They argue that the specific prohibition should take precedence. The Shafi'i offers two responses: either to undercut the objection (perhaps by showing the prohibitions are not absolute) or to give preponderance to his own evidence through various aspects of preference (tarjih). The key principle is that a specific evidence normally overrides a general one, but the strength of the evidence (agreement, authenticity) can affect the outcome.

Key Terms
الخاص (al-khass): Specific evidence that applies to a subset of cases. [Note: In logic, khass can be part of the five universals; here it's about legal evidence.]
العام (al-'amm): General evidence that covers many cases.
الترجيح (al-tarjih): Giving preponderance to one evidence over another. [Note: In jadal, tarjih is used to resolve ta'arud.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the zakat example, what is the Hanafi's main argument for preferring his general evidence over the Maliki's specific hadith?
A) The specific hadith is weak in transmission.
B) The general verse is more specific than the hadith.
C) The general evidence is agreed upon while the specific is disputed.
D) The specific hadith is abrogated by another verse.

Key Takeaway
Specific evidence normally overrides general, but the consensus on authenticity (ittifaq) can tilt the balance in favor of the general.

Objection with Equally General Evidence
Author presents third type with Maliki-Hanbali example on cupping breaking fast.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when two contradictory evidences are equivalent in generality and describe the dialectical challenge that arises.

Core Idea
When two general proofs clash equally, the conflict demands resolution beyond mere preference.

In the dialectical tradition of usul al-fiqh, not all objections are resolved by simply preferring one evidence over another. Sometimes both sides bring proofs that are equal in generality or specificity, creating a direct contradiction. This lesson examines that third type of objection, using a real debate between Maliki and Hanbali jurists over whether cupping breaks the fast. You will learn to recognize this scenario and understand why it requires further investigation rather than a quick ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of equivalence
The author says each evidence is 'like the other' (مثل الآخر). Determine whether both are general, both specific, or each general in one way and specific in another. In the example, both hadiths are general.
Generality here means the ruling applies to many cases, not just one.
2
Recognize the two contradictory proofs
One proof says cupping does not break the fast; the other says it does. Both are attributed to the Prophet (PBUH). Note that each side uses a general hadith to support its position.
The Maliki relies on a hadith listing three things that do not break the fast. The Hanbali relies on a hadith stating the cupper and the one cupped break the fast.
3
Analyze the contradiction
Since both hadiths are general and contradictory, they cannot both be applied literally. This creates a direct conflict that the dialectician must address.
The author does not provide a resolution here; he only presents the problem.
4
Consider possible resolution methods
In usul al-fiqh, such contradictions are resolved by examining authenticity, abrogation, specificity, or interpretation. Think about what tools a mujtahid might use.
This step goes beyond the passage but connects to broader methodology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
3 - وإذا كان كل واحد منهما مثل الآخر : فلا يخلو إما أن يكونا عامين، أو خاصين أو كل واحد منهما عام من وجه خاص من وجه . مثال الأول : أن يستدل المالكي في أن الحجامة لا تفطر بما روي عن النبي (ثلاث لا يفطرن الصائم الحجامة والقيء والاحتلام) (5). فيعارضه الحنبلي بما روي عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أنه قال : أفطر الحاجم والمحجوم)(6).
3 - And if each one of them is like the other: then it is either that both are general, or both are specific, or each one is general in one respect and specific in another. Example of the first: that the Maliki argues that cupping does not break the fast with what is narrated from the Prophet (three things do not break the fast of the fasting person: cupping, vomiting, and nocturnal emission) (5). Then the Hanbali objects with what is narrated from the Prophet, peace be upon him, that he said: 'The cupper and the one cupped have broken the fast' (6).
Source pages p. 395

Commentary
This passage presents the third type of objection, where the two contradictory evidences are equivalent (مثل الآخر) in their degree of generality or specificity. The author classifies three sub-cases: both are general, both are specific, or each is general in one way and specific in another. He gives an example of the first sub-case (both general): the Maliki uses a hadith that lists three things that do not break the fast, including cupping. This hadith is general in that it exempts cupping from breaking the fast. The Hanbali objects with another hadith that says the cupper and the one cupped have broken the fast, which is also general in its ruling. Since both are general and contradictory, there is a direct conflict that must be resolved through other means (e.g., examining authenticity, abrogation, or interpretation). The author does not provide a resolution here; he only presents the problem as an example of when evidences are equivalent.

Key Terms
المثل
الحجامة
العام

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) One must always prefer the specific over the general.

D) Both evidences are automatically invalid.

Key Takeaway
When two contradictory general proofs are equivalent in scope, a direct conflict emerges that cannot be resolved by simple preference; it demands deeper methodological investigation.

Preferring the General Agreed-Upon Report over the Specific Disputed One in Jadal
Rule and example of preferring general agreed-upon over specific disputed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a general agreed-upon report should be preferred over a specific disputed one, and apply this rule in a jadal exchange using an example from Islamic prayer times.

Core Idea
In jadal, the general agreed-upon outranks the specific disputed, but a specific prohibition can override a general permission.

In this passage, the author presents a key rule of mu'arada (dialectical objection) regarding the relative strength of general vs. specific reports. The general report that is agreed upon (mutafaq 'alayh) is given priority over a specific report that is disputed (mukhtalaf fih). However, there is nuance: when the specific report is a prohibition that specifies times, it can overrule a general permission. The example from prayer times illustrates this interplay.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conflicting proofs
Find the general agreed-upon proof (the hadith about praying a missed prayer whenever remembered) and the specific disputed proof (the prohibition of prayer after Fajr and Asr). Note which is 'amm and which is khass.
The general hadith is agreed upon (mutafaq 'alayh), the specific is disputed (mukhtalaf fih).
2
Apply the rule of preference
The rule states that the general agreed-upon is more worthy (awla) than the specific disputed. Why? Because agreement gives it higher epistemological weight.
Agreement implies consensus on authenticity, making it stronger.
3
Notice the exception for specific prohibitions
In the example, the specific hadith is a prohibition (nahy) that specifies times, so it overrules the general permission. This is because 'the specific overrules the general' (al-khas yaqdi 'ala al-'amm) when it specifies an exception.
The specific prohibition is not just any specific, but one that delineates a restricted time.
4
Analyze Shafi'i's two responses
Shafi'i counters the objection in two ways: (1) nullifying the opposition (isqat al-mu'arada) by arguing the prohibition does not apply or his hadith is equally specific, or (2) seeking other aspects of preponderance (wujuh al-tarjih) to favor his evidence.
These responses are typical in jadal: either dissolve the conflict or outweigh it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحنفي في الجواب عن هذا الاعتراض أن يقول : إن خبرنا عام متفق عليه، وخبركم وإن كان خاصا مختلف فيه والعام المتفق عليه أولى من الخاص. ومثاله أيضا أن يستدل الشافعي في جواز صلاة لها سبب في أوقات النهي بقوله من نام عن صلاة أو نسيها فليصلها إذا ذكرها فيعارضه الحنفي أو الحنبلي بنهي النبي عن الصلاة في هذه الأوقات وهو قوله عليه الصلاة والسلام لا صلاة بعد الصبح حتى ترتفع الشمس ولا صلاة بعد العصر حتى تغيب الشمس فهذا خاص يقدم على العموم لكونه نهيا مخصصا لأوقات والخاص يقضي على العام. وجواب الشافعي عن هذا الاعتراض من وجهين أحدهما أن يسقط المعارضة والثاني أن يرجح دليله بما يجد من وجوه الترجيحات.
The Hanafi in answering this objection says: Indeed our report is general and agreed upon, and your report, although specific, is disputed; the general agreed-upon is more worthy than the specific. And an example of this: that al-Shafi'i argues for the permissibility of a prayer with a reason during forbidden times by his saying: 'Whoever sleeps through a prayer or forgets it, let him pray it when he remembers it.' So the Hanafi or Hanbali opposes him with the Prophet's prohibition of prayer in these times, which is his saying, peace be upon him: 'No prayer after Fajr until the sun rises, and no prayer after Asr until the sun sets.' So this [Hadith] is specific and is given precedence over the general because it is a prohibition specifying times, and the specific overrules the general. And al-Shafi'i's answer to this objection is in two ways: one is to nullify the opposition, and the second is to give preponderance to his evidence by whatever aspects of preponderance he finds.
Source pages p. 395

Commentary
This passage discusses a type of mu'arada (objection) in jadal where one party's proof is general and agreed upon, while the other's is specific and disputed. The author first states the rule: the general agreed-upon report is given priority over the specific disputed one. He then provides an example: al-Shafi'i argued for the permissibility of performing a prayer that has a reason (such as a missed prayer) during the times when prayer is normally forbidden, using the hadith 'Whoever sleeps through a prayer or forgets it, let him pray it when he remembers it.' The Hanafi or Hanbali objects by citing the Prophet's prohibition of prayer after Fajr and after Asr. This prohibition is specific because it targets specific times, and the specific overrules the general. Al-Shafi'i responds in two ways: either by nullifying the objection (perhaps by arguing that the prohibition is not general, or that the hadith about missed prayer overrides it due to its specificity in another sense), or by seeking other aspects of preponderance to strengthen his evidence. This demonstrates the interplay between general and specific texts in usul al-fiqh as applied in jadal.

Resolving Mu'arada When Proofs Are Equivalent
Author classifies three scenarios of equivalent proofs: both general, both specific, or each general from one aspect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify three scenarios of equivalent proofs and apply ta'wil to resolve contradictory evidence in juristic debate.

Core Idea
Equivalent proofs do not defeat each other—they demand interpretation.

In jadal (dialectic debate), when two proofs are of equal strength, neither can simply override the other. The mu'arada (objection) must be resolved through ta'wil (interpretation). This passage from a jadal work classifies three cases: both proofs general, both specific, or each general from one aspect. The examples show how the disputant defends his position by interpreting the opponent's evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three scenarios
The passage lists three possibilities when proofs are equal: both general, both specific, each general from one aspect. Note the logical exhaustiveness of these categories.
The author does not claim these are the only possibilities, but the main ones in practice.
2
Analyze the first example
Maliki argues cupping does not break fast using a general hadith listing three things. Hanbali objects with a specific hadith: 'the cupper and cupped break fast.' The Maliki responds by ta'wil: the Prophet meant the breaking occurred due to something else, and cupping was only a way to identify the person.
The Maliki does not reject the hadith but reinterprets it to remove contradiction.
3
Analyze the third example
Maliki argues for making up missed prayers during forbidden times using the hadith 'pray when you remember.' Hanafi objects with the prohibition of prayer during certain hours. Each side interprets the other's proof: Maliki limits the prohibition to supererogatory prayers; Hanafi limits the permission to outside forbidden times.
Both parties use the specificity of their own proof to restrict the opponent's general proof.
4
Extract the rule
When proofs are equivalent, the response always involves ta'wil: interpreting the opponent's evidence to reconcile it with one's own. The interpretation often appeals to a distinction (e.g., missed prayer vs. supererogatory) or a hidden meaning.
This is a key principle in jadal: equivalent proofs do not lead to preference but to interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا كان كل واحد منهما مثل الآخر : فلا يخلو إما أن يكونا عامين، أو خاصين أو كل واحد منهما عام من وجه خاص من وجه . مثال الأول : أن يستدل المالكي في أن الحجامة لا تفطر بما روي عن النبي (ثلاث لا يفطرن الصائم الحجامة والقيء والاحتلام) فيعارضه الحنبلي بما روي عن النبي أنه قال : أفطر الحاجم والمحجوم. والطريق في الجواب عنه أن يقول المالكي: إنه أراد به أن فطره وقع بغير الحجامة، وإنما جعل الحجامة تعريفا له ليعرفه به من يشاركه. ومثال الثالث : أن يستدل المالكي في قضاء الفوائت في أوقات النهي بما روي عن النبي أنه قال : (من) نام عن صلاة أو نسيها فليصلها إذا ذكرها، فإن ذلك وقتها فيعارضه الحنفي بما روي أن النبي نهى عن الصلاة في هذه الساعات. فيقول المالكي: أحمل خبرك على النوافل بدليل خبرنا فإنه خاص في الفوائت، ويقول الحنفي وأنا أحمل خبرك على من ذكرها في غير وقت النهي، بدليل خبرنا فإنه خاص في ساعات النهي.
And if each of them is like the other, then they are either both general, or both specific, or each is general from one aspect and specific from another. Example of the first: that the Maliki argues that cupping does not break the fast based on what was narrated from the Prophet: 'Three things do not break the fast of the one fasting: cupping, vomiting, and wet dream.' So the Hanbali opposes him with what was narrated from the Prophet that he said: 'The cupper and the cupped break the fast.' And the way to answer this is for the Maliki to say: He meant by that that his breaking occurred by something other than cupping, and he only made cupping a way to identify it so that those who share with him would know it. Example of the third: that the Maliki argues for making up missed prayers during forbidden times based on what was narrated from the Prophet that he said: 'Whoever sleeps through a prayer or forgets it, let him pray it when he remembers it, for that is its time.' So the Hanafi opposes him with what was narrated that the Prophet forbade prayer during these hours. So the Maliki says: I interpret your report as referring to supererogatory prayers, due to the evidence of our report which is specific to missed prayers. And the Hanafi says: And I interpret your report as referring to one who remembers it outside the forbidden time, due to the evidence of our report which is specific to the forbidden hours.
Source pages p. 395

Commentary
This passage deals with the case when two proofs are equivalent in strength. The author enumerates three possibilities: both reports are general, both are specific, or each is general from one aspect and specific from another. He then gives examples. The first example: the Maliki argues that cupping does not break the fast using a general hadith listing three things that do not break the fast. The Hanbali objects with a specific hadith stating that the cupper and the cupped break the fast. The Maliki responds by interpreting that the Prophet meant that their breaking occurred due to something else, not cupping itself, and he only mentioned cupping as a way to identify the person. The third example: the Maliki argues for making up missed prayers during forbidden times using the hadith about remembering a missed prayer; the Hanafi objects with the general prohibition of prayer during specific times. The Maliki interprets the prohibition as applying to supererogatory prayers, while the Hanafi interprets the permission as applying only when the prayer is remembered outside the forbidden time. This shows how each party uses the specificity of their own proof to restrict the other's.

The Rule: A Clear Text Cannot Be Opposed by Analogy
Author demonstrates the rule that a nass cannot be overridden by qiyas.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule that a clear text (nass) takes precedence over analogy (qiyas) in a dialectical exchange, using the Maliki-Hanafi example.

Core Idea
A clear text from revelation overrides any human analogy.

In Islamic legal theory, not all texts are equal. Some are so clear that they cannot be interpreted away. When an opponent tries to use analogy to oppose such a clear text, the correct response is to assert the primacy of the text. This lesson walks through a classic exchange between a Maliki and a Hanafi over the sale of fresh dates for dried dates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the positions
In the example, the Maliki prohibits the sale based on a hadith. The Hanafi uses qiyas to argue it is allowed. Who is the mudda'i? Who is the mu'tarid? (The Maliki is the one with the proof, so he is the mudda'i; the Hanafi objects using qiyas.)
2
Analyze the Maliki's evidence
What type of text does the Maliki rely on? The passage says it is a nass (clear text). Why is it considered nass? Because the Prophet's question and explicit prohibition leave no room for interpretation.
3
Analyze the Hanafi's objection
What is the Hanafi's 'illa? He argues that equality in weight exists at contract time, so the sale should be valid like dried dates for dried dates. What legal principle is he invoking? (The prohibition in riba is due to inequality.)
4
Apply the rule of precedence
The Maliki's response is that a nass cannot be opposed by qiyas. Why does this rule exist? Because revelation is a stronger source of knowledge than human reasoning.
5
Check the dialectical outcome
By the rules of munazara, who wins? The Maliki, because he has a text that is stronger than analogy. The Hanafi's objection is not accepted because it attempts to override a clear text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المعارضة بعلة قياس : ومتن السنة هنا لا يخلو أن يكون نصا لا يحتمل التأويل، أو ظاهرًا يحتمل التأويل، أوعموما يحتمل التخصيص. ب - 1 - معارضة الاستدلال بالنص الذي لا يحتمل التأويل بالعلة : ومثاله : أن يستدل المالكي في تحريم بيع الرطب بالتمر بما روي أن النبي سئل عن بيع الرطب بالتمر فقال : (أينقص الرطب إذا جف؟)، قالوا: نعم، قال: (فلا إذا) . فيعارضه الحنفي بأنه قد وُجد فيه التساوي حين العقد فيما يحرم فيه التفاضل، فوجب أن يصح كالتمر بالتمر . وطريق المالكي في الجواب عن هذا الاعتراض أن يقول : إن ما استدللنا به نص، والنص لا يجوز معارضته بالقياس.
Opposition with the cause of analogy. The text of the Sunnah here is either a clear text that does not allow interpretation, or apparent allowing interpretation, or general allowing specification. Section B-1: Opposition of a proof by a text that does not allow interpretation with a cause. Its example: that the Maliki argues for the prohibition of selling fresh dates for dried dates based on what was narrated that the Prophet was asked about selling fresh dates for dried dates and said: 'Do fresh dates decrease when they dry?' They said: Yes. He said: 'Then do not.' So the Hanafi opposes it with the fact that equality exists at the time of contract in what is prohibited to have a difference, so it must be valid like the sale of dry dates for dry dates. And the Maliki's way to answer this objection is to say: What we used as evidence is a clear text, and a clear text cannot be opposed by analogy.
Source pages p. 396

Commentary
This passage addresses a specific type of mu'arada where one party uses a text that is a clear nass (unambiguous and not open to interpretation) and the other party uses analogy (qiyas). The author first outlines the three possible statuses of the Sunnah text: nass (clear, not subject to ta'wil), zahir (apparent, subject to ta'wil), and 'amm (general, subject to specification). Then he focuses on opposing a nass with a 'illa (cause) of analogy. The example: The Maliki prohibits selling fresh dates for dried dates because the Prophet asked if fresh dates decrease when dry, and when told yes, he prohibited it. The Hanafi objects by using analogy: since the two items are equal at the time of contract in weight, and the prohibition in riba is due to inequality, here there is equality, so it should be valid like selling dried dates for dried dates. The Maliki's answer is that their evidence is a clear nass, and a nass cannot be overridden by qiyas. This illustrates the principle that revealed texts take precedence over human reasoning.

Key Terms
نص (nass): A clear text that does not admit interpretation (ta'wil); here it is the hadith quoting the Prophet's explicit prohibition.
ظاهر (zahir): An apparent text that allows interpretation (ta'wil); one of the categories of Sunnah texts.
عام ('amm): A general text that allows specification (takhsis); third category of Sunnah texts.
علة ('illa): The effective cause or ratio legis in analogy; here the Hanafi argues that equality of weight is the 'illa for allowing the sale.
قياس (qiyas): Analogical reasoning; a method of deriving legal rulings by comparing an issue to a similar one with a known ruling based on a common 'illa.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the hadith is more authentic
B) Because the text (nass) cannot be opposed by qiyas
C) Because the Hanafi's analogy is flawed
D) Because the Maliki has consensus (ijma')

Key Takeaway
A clear text (nass) from revelation is a stronger evidence than analogy (qiyas), and cannot be overridden by it.

The Hanafi objects using qiyas: since the risk (gharar) is absent in some cases, it should be allowed. Formulate the Maliki-style response to this objection, stating why the nass takes precedence.

Mu'arada and Jawab: Reconciling Conflicting Hadiths
A complete dialectical exchange with objection and answer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct and analyze a dialectical exchange involving mu'arada and jawab in Islamic legal debate, identifying how conflicting texts are reconciled through interpretative specification.

Core Idea
Conflicting hadiths are reconciled by interpreting each in its specific context, not by rejecting one.

In Islamic legal debate (jadal), it is common for opponents to cite conflicting textual evidence. The art of mu'arada (objection) and jawab (answer) requires each side to engage with the other's evidence, often through ta'wil (interpretation). This passage shows how a Maliki and a Hanafi handle a conflict between a command to pray missed prayers and a prohibition on prayer at certain times.

Guided Path
1
Identify the positions
The Maliki argues for making up missed prayers even during prohibited times, citing a hadith. The Hanafi objects with a hadith prohibiting prayer during those specific hours.
2
Extract the objector's evidence
The Hanafi's evidence is the general prohibition of prayer during certain hours (e.g., after Fajr and after 'Asr). Notice that this is a general ruling.
The prohibition hadith is general in scope.
3
Analyze the Maliki's response (jawab)
The Maliki restricts (haml) the Hanafi's prohibition to supererogatory prayers only, because his own hadith is specific (khas) to missed obligatory prayers.
This is an example of takhsis (specification) of a general text by a specific one.
4
Analyze the Hanafi's counter-response
The Hanafi restricts the Maliki's command to those who remember the missed prayer outside the prohibited hours, because his hadith is specific to those hours.
Both sides use the same method: interpretation to avoid direct conflict.
5
Evaluate the outcome
Neither side's argument is definitively defeated; each offers a plausible interpretation. The debate illustrates that conflicting texts can be reconciled through ta'wil, and jadal does not necessarily produce a winner.
This demonstrates the principle of al-jam' wa al-tawfiq (reconciliation) in usul al-fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومثال الثالث : أن يستدل المالكي في قضاء الفوائت في أوقات النهي بما روي عن النبي الله أنه قال : (من) نام عن صلاة أو نسيها فليصلها إذا ذكرها، فإن ذلك وقتها) . فيعارضه الحنفي بما روي أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم نهى عن الصلاة في هذه الساعات. فيقول المالكي: أحمل خبرك على النوافل بدليل خبرنا فإنه خاص في الفوائت، ويقول الحنفي وأنا أحمل خبرك على من ذكرها في غير وقت النهي، بدليل خبرنا فإنه خاص في ساعات النهي
And the third example: That the Maliki argues in the matter of making up missed prayers during prohibited times by what is narrated from the Prophet, peace be upon him, that he said: 'Whoever sleeps through a prayer or forgets it, let him pray it when he remembers it, for that is its time.' Then the Hanafi objects with what is narrated that the Prophet, peace be upon him, prohibited prayer during these hours. So the Maliki says: I interpret your hadith as referring to supererogatory prayers, because our hadith is specific to missed prayers. And the Hanafi says: And I interpret your hadith as referring to one who remembers them outside the prohibited time, because our hadith is specific to the prohibited hours.
Source pages p. 396

Commentary
This passage presents a complete dialectical exchange (jadal) between a Maliki and a Hanafi jurist concerning the validity of making up missed prayers during times when prayer is normally prohibited (e.g., after Fajr and after 'Asr). The Maliki first cites a hadith that seems to command prayer whenever one remembers a missed prayer, implying it is always valid. The Hanafi objects by citing another hadith that explicitly prohibits prayer during those specific hours. Each then offers an interpretation (ta'wil) of the other's evidence to resolve the apparent contradiction. The Maliki restricts the prohibition to supererogatory prayers, while the Hanafi restricts the command to remember outside the prohibited hours. The passage illustrates how conflicting texts are reconciled in Islamic legal debate by appealing to specificity and context.

Key Terms
معارضة
جواب
حمل

D) He says the Maliki's hadith is abrogated.

Key Takeaway
In jadal, conflicting textual evidence is reconciled through interpretative specification (takhsis) rather than outright rejection.

Objection by 'Illa Against a Zahir Text
Author gives example of objection by 'illa against zahir and method of answer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a mu'arada by 'illa against a zahir text and explain the correct method of response in a jadal exchange.

Core Idea
When an opponent uses qiyas to oppose a text, refute the qiyas first.

Here we see a classic dialectical move in usul al-fiqh: a Maliki uses a hadith command to argue for najasa of semen, while a Shafi'i objects by analogy to dust. The lesson shows the proper rebuttal: attack the analogy, not the text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claimant's original argument
The Maliki cites the hadith command as a zahir for obligation (wujub) of washing. Note the logical move: from command to wajib.
This is the standard usuli understanding of amr.
2
Identify the objection by 'illa
The Shafi'i introduces a qiyas: semen is like dust because both are origins of man. The 'illa is the origin-creating property.
This is a mu'arada: using an 'illa-based argument to oppose the apparent meaning.
3
Recognize the required response strategy
The Maliki should not abandon the text nor accept the qiyas. Instead, he must refute the qiyas itself by showing a flaw in its 'illa or the analogy.
The passage says: 'فأن يتكلم المستدل على القياس بما يفقه ليسلم له الاستدلال بالظاهر'.
4
Apply to other examples
Generalize: whenever a text is opposed by a qiyas, first examine the validity of the qiyas. If it is invalid, the text stands.
This is a key dialectical principle in jadal al-mujtahidin.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
2 - معارضة الاستدلال بالظاهر الذي يحتمل التأويل بالعلة : ومثاله : أن يستدل المالكي على وجوب غسل الثوب من المني بما روي عنه عليه الصلاة والسلام أنه قال : (إن كان رطبا فاغسليه وإن كان يابسا فحكيه)، والأمر يقتضي الوجوب . فيعارضه الشافعي بأن المني أصل خلقة ابن آدم فوجب أن يكون طاهرا كالتراب . والطريق في الجواب على هذا ؛ أن يتكلم المستدل على القياس بما يفقه ليسلم له الاستدلال بالظاهر
2 - Objection to argumentation with the apparent text that admits interpretation by means of the 'illa: Its example is that the Maliki argues for the obligation of washing the garment from semen by what is narrated from him, peace be upon him, that he said: 'If it is wet, wash it, and if it is dry, scrape it off.' And the command implies obligation. Then the Shafi'i objects by saying that semen is the original substance from which the son of Adam is created, so it must be pure like dust. And the way to answer this is that the arguer should refute the qiyas by (explaining) what invalidates it, so that the argument by the apparent text is accepted.
Source pages p. 397

Commentary
This passage presents the second type of mu'arada by 'illa: opposing a zahir (apparent) text. The Maliki cites a hadith that commands washing or scraping semen from clothes, taking the imperative as indicating obligation (wujub). The Shafi'i objects by analogizing semen to dust (which is pure), arguing that since semen is the origin of man, it should be pure. The answer strategy is to refute the qiyas itself by pointing out a flaw in its reasoning, thus preserving the apparent meaning of the hadith. The passage does not specify how to invalidate the qiyas, but the method is to challenge the 'illa or the analogy.

Key Terms
ظاهر (zahir): An apparent text that admits interpretation (ta'wil) but is prima facie clear. [Note: In usul al-fiqh, zahir is one of the categories of ambiguous text, but here it is specifically the text open to ta'wil.]
أمر (amr): A command form; in usul, it generally indicates obligation unless there is a contrary indication. [Note: No significant shift.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Reject the analogy by showing the difference between semen and dust.
C) Say the hadith is weak.
D) Say the command is not obligatory.

Key Takeaway
When a text is opposed by an 'illa-based objection, the defender must refute the qiyas itself, not the objection directly.

Responding to Objection by 'Illa Against General Text
Author gives example of objection by 'illa against general text and method of answer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to respond to a mu'arada that uses analogy to specify a general text by refuting the analogy itself.

Core Idea
Refute the analogy, not the general text.

In jadal, when one party uses a general text as evidence, the opponent may object by drawing an analogy that would limit the text. The proper response is not to accept the limitation but to invalidate the analogy, preserving the generality of the evidence. This passage illustrates this with the Maliki-Hanafi debate on killing apostate women.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general text ('amm)
Locate the general command being used as evidence. Here, it is the hadith: 'Whoever changes his religion, kill him.' This applies to all apostates, both male and female.
The general text is typically a command or prohibition covering all individuals of a category.
2
Identify the opponent's analogy (qiyas)
Determine the objector's analogical argument. The Hanafi claims apostasy is a type of disbelief, and original disbelief does not entail killing women, so by analogy women apostates should not be killed either.
The analogy relies on the 'illa (effective cause) of 'disbelief'.
3
Determine the potential specification
If the analogy is accepted, the general text would be specified to exclude women. This would change the ruling from general to specific.
Specification (takhsis) limits the scope of the general term.
4
Formulate a refutation of the analogy
Identify a difference between the two cases that breaks the analogy. For example, apostasy is a breach of the social contract (treason), whereas original disbelief is not. Thus the 'illa is not shared, and the qiyas fails.
The refutation must show either a difference in the 'illa or a missing condition for qiyas.
5
Conclude that the general text stands
Since the analogy is invalid, the general text remains applicable to all individuals, including women. The objection is overcome.
The arguer wins by defending the general text without conceding specification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
3 - معارضة الاستدلال بالعام الذي يحتمل التخصيص بالعلة : وذلك مثل أن يستدل المالكي في قتل المرتد بقوله : (من بدل دينه فا قتلوه) فيعارضه الحنفي بأن هذا أحد نوعي الكفر، فلم تقتل به المرأة، كالكفر الأصلي، والطريق في الجواب على هذا ؛ أن يتكلم المستدل على القياس بما يفقه ليسلم له الاستدلال بالعام
3 - Objection to argumentation with the general text that admits specification by means of the 'illa: That is like when the Maliki argues for killing the apostate by his saying: 'Whoever changes his religion, kill him.' Then the Hanafi objects by saying that this is one of the two types of disbelief, so the woman is not killed by it, like original disbelief. And the way to answer this is that the arguer should refute the qiyas by (explaining) what invalidates it, so that the argument by the general text is accepted.
Source pages p. 397

Commentary
This passage covers the third type: mu'arada by 'illa against a general text ('amm). The Maliki uses the general command to kill anyone who changes religion, implying both men and women. The Hanafi objects by analogizing apostasy to original disbelief (kufr asli), where a woman is not killed; thus, the general command should be specified to exclude women. The answer is to refute the qiyas itself, similar to the previous case. The method is to point out the flaw in the analogy, allowing the general text to remain applicable. This shows that general texts can be specified, but the objector must be answered by invalidating the reasoning, not by conceding the specification.

Key Terms
عام ('amm)
تخصيص (takhsis)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
When an opponent uses analogy to specify a general text, the correct response is to refute the analogy itself, not to accept the specification.

Objection by Ziyāda ʿalā al-Sabab
Author defines objection by adding a specific cause that limits the general ruling.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and use the objection type 'al-ziyāda ʿalā al-sabab' to challenge a general ruling by introducing a specific cause from a variant narration.

Core Idea
A specific cause in a variant narration can limit a general ruling.

In dialectical debate over rulings from the Sunna, one party may cite a general hadith. The opponent can object by introducing a variant narration that adds a specific cause (sabab), thereby limiting the ruling's application. This lesson explains that objection and its logical structure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claimant's argument
Find the ruling the claimant (mudda'i) is trying to prove using a general hadith. In the example, the Hanbali argues for cutting the hand of a borrower who denies.
2
Locate the objector's variant narration
The objector brings a different narration that adds a specific cause (sabab) to the story, which was not in the claimant's version.
The exact variant is cut off in the source, but the structure is clear.
3
Determine the effect on the ruling
The addition of a cause specifies the ruling, limiting it to that particular circumstance, thus undermining the general application.
This is the core of the objection.
4
Consider the jadal exchange
How would the claimant respond? The passage does not provide the full answer, but consider possible responses based on jadal rules.
The incomplete example highlights the need for further textual evidence.

First Type of Objection: Transmitting a Wording with an Addition
Defines the first type of objection to occasion-based sunna reasoning with example and answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and analyse the first type of objection (naql ziyadat al-lafdh) in a dialectical exchange on sunna-based arguments.

Core Idea
Adding a wording changes the occasion, forcing the claimant to prefer or reconcile.

In dialectical reasoning on sunna that occurred on a specific occasion, an opponent can counter a claimant's proof by presenting a narration that adds more detail to the occasion, potentially altering the ruling. This lesson dissects that objection type—called 'transmission of an addition of wording' (naql ziyadat al-lafdh)—using a classic fiqh example from the Barira hadith dispute between Hanafis and Malikis. You will learn the structure of the objection and the required response strategies.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claimant's argument
Find the hadith and the narrator the claimant (mudda'i) uses to prove his position. In the example, the Hanafi uses the Barira hadith and Aisha's report that Barira's husband was free to argue that a freed female slave under a free husband has choice (khiyar).
2
Identify the opponent's objection
Look for the additional wording the opponent (sa'il) brings. The Maliki cites Ibn Abbas's narration that the husband was a slave. This addition (ziyada) specifies the occasion differently, challenging the claimant's application of the ruling.
3
Understand the objection type
Recognize that the opponent is not denying the hadith but adding a detail that changes the apparent sabab (occasion). This is the essence of naql ziyadat al-lafdh: the new wording alters the context of the ruling.
The addition must be a genuine extra wording, not a contradiction in the main content.
4
Analyze the response strategies
The passage states the claimant must either prefer (tarjih) his narration over the opponent's by criteria like number of narrators or reliability, or reconcile (jam') the two narrations harmoniously. Both are forms of istidlal (deductive reasoning).
5
Map the dialectical structure
Identify the roles: mudda'i (claimant) uses evidence, sa'il (objector) counters with another narration, and the expected jawab (answer) is a reasoned defense. This structure is standard in jadal on sunna.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المطلب الأول : نقل زيادة اللفظ
وذلك أن يستدل المدعي بحديث وارد على سبب على صحة دعواه، فيعترض عليه السائل برواية أخرى فيها زيادة بيان للسبب ومعارضة للرواية التي استدل بها المدعي . مثاله : أن يستدل الحنفي على ثبوت الخيار للأمة المعتقة تحت الحر بما روي بريرة أعتقت فخيرها رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم، وقالت عائشة: «كان زوج بريرة حرا» (1) فيقول المالكي معترضا: قد روى ابن عباس كان زوج بريرة عبدا أسودا يقال له مغيث (2) . والطريق في الجواب بالنسبة للحنفي، وهو المستدل أن يرجح رواية عائشة على رواية ابن عباس إن أمكنه أو يجمع بينهما وهو استدلال (3).
The first requirement: transmission of an addition of wording. That is, the claimant argues using a hadith reported on an occasion to support his claim, so the opponent objects with another narration that contains additional clarification of the occasion and contradicts the narration used by the claimant. Its example: The Hanafi argues for proving the choice for a female slave who is freed while under a free husband by what was narrated: 'Barira was freed and the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, gave her choice', and Aisha said: 'Barira's husband was free.' (1) So the Maliki says, objecting: 'Ibn Abbas narrated that Barira's husband was a black slave called Mughith.' (2) And the way to answer from the perspective of the Hanafi, who is the claimant, is to prefer the narration of Aisha over the narration of Ibn Abbas if possible, or to reconcile between them. And it is a deduction (3).
Source pages p. 398

Commentary
This passage defines the first type of objection against reasoning based on a sunna that occurred on a specific occasion. The objection is called 'transmission of an addition of wording' (naql ziyadat al-lafdh). The structure is: a claimant (mustadill) uses a hadith reported on an occasion to prove his position. The opponent (sa'il) then counters by presenting another narration that adds more detail to the occasion, thereby altering the apparent ruling or its applicability. The author gives a concrete example from fiqh: the Hanafi school argues that a female slave who is freed while married to a free man has the choice (khiyar) to stay or leave the marriage. They base this on the hadith of Barira, where the Prophet gave her choice, and Aisha's report that Barira's husband was free. The Maliki objects by citing Ibn Abbas's narration that Barira's husband was actually a black slave named Mughith. This addition—that the husband was a slave—undermines the Hanafi's deduction, because the ruling might be specific to a slave husband. The response (jawab) for the Hanafi claimant is to either prefer Aisha's narration over Ibn Abbas's (tarjih) if possible, or to reconcile the two narrations (jam'). This answer itself is a form of deductive reasoning (istidlal). The passage illustrates how dialectical engagement in usul al-fiqh uses textual criticism and preference to defend a position.

Key Terms
نقل زيادة اللفظ
المدعي
السائل

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) The Maliki argues that the Hanafi's analogy is invalid.
D) The Maliki claims the hadith is weak.

Key Takeaway
The first type of objection in occasion-based sunna reasoning is when the opponent brings an additional wording that clarifies the occasion, requiring the claimant to defend via tarjih or jam'.

The Second Objection: Adding to the Occasion and Claiming Specificity
Defines the second objection where the opponent adds to the occasion and claims the ruling is specific to that addition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when an opponent uses the 'adding to the occasion' objection and articulate the claimant's standard response.

Core Idea
An added detail in a narration can shift the apparent cause of a ruling; the claimant must argue that the ruling attaches to only one of the transmitted causes.

In jadal, opponents often introduce additional textual details to undermine the claimant's inference from a general incident. This lesson examines the second type of objection: the opponent adds an extra element to the reported occasion of a ruling and then claims that the ruling is specific to that addition. Through a classic example from Hanbali-Maliki debate, you will learn how to detect this move and how the claimant can reply.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claimant's original inference
The Hanbali uses the hadith of the woman who borrowed and denied, and the Prophet ordered her hand cut, to argue that amputation is obligatory for a borrower who denies. Note the cause (sabab) he infers: borrowing and denial.
This is the mudda'i's position.
2
Identify the opponent's added detail
The Maliki brings a fuller narration that includes 'she stole' (saraqat) in addition to borrowing and denial. This addition (ziyada) changes the apparent cause.
The opponent claims the ruling is specific to theft, not borrowing and denial.
3
Understand the opponent's claim of specificity
The Maliki argues that because the added element (theft) is a clear cause for amputation, the ruling is specific to that, and borrowing/denial is merely identifying the woman.
This is takhsis al-hukm bi-al-ziyada.
4
Learn the claimant's response
The Hanbali replies that both causes were transmitted together with the ruling, so the apparent (zahir) is that the ruling is attached to one of them (borrowing and denial), not the other.
This is the standard jawab: the addition does not necessarily negate the original inference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المطلب الثاني : الزيادة على السبب وادعاء تخصيص الحكم بالزيادة أن يستدل المدعي لرأيه في مسألة ما بواقعة حكم فيها رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم عموم، فيعترض عليه السائل بتخصيص الاستدلال بسبب معين في تلك الواقعة وذلك بإيراد رواية غير رواية المستدل فيها زيادة توضيح لذلك السبب . وذلك مثل أن يستدل الحنبلي في إيجاب القطع على المستعير إذا جحد العارية، بما روي أن امرأة من بني مخزوم كانت تستعير المتاع أو الحلي فتجحده فأمر رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم بقطع يدها (1) فيقول المالكي معترضا قد روي في هذا الخبر أنها كانت تستعير فتجحده، فسرقت، فقطعها رسول الله ؛ وهذا يدل على أن القطع كان للسرقة، وأما الاستعارة والجحود فليسا بسبب للقطع، وإنما نقل ذلك لتعريف المرأة، وجواب المستدل في مثل هذا أن يقول : قد نقل السببان معا مع الحكم، فالظاهر أنه يتعلق بأحدهما دون الآخر (2).
The second requirement: adding to the occasion and claiming the ruling is specific to the addition. That the claimant argues for his opinion on some issue using an incident in which the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, ruled generally, so the opponent objects by specifying the reasoning to a particular cause in that incident by presenting a narration other than the claimant's narration which contains additional clarification of that cause. An example: The Hanbali argues for obligating amputation on the borrower who denies the borrowed item, based on the report that a woman from Banu Makhzum used to borrow goods or jewelry and deny them, so the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, ordered her hand to be cut off (1). So the Maliki says, objecting: It has been reported in this story that she used to borrow and deny, then she stole, and the Messenger cut off her hand; this indicates that the amputation was for theft, whereas borrowing and denial are not a cause for amputation, and that was mentioned only to identify the woman. The answer of the claimant in such a case is to say: Both causes have been transmitted together with the ruling, so the apparent is that it is attached to one of them not the other (2).
Source pages p. 398

Commentary
This passage defines the second type of objection: 'adding to the occasion and claiming the ruling is specific to that addition' (al-ziyada 'ala al-sabab wa-'da' takhsis al-hukm bi-al-ziyada). The claimant argues using a general incident, but the opponent introduces a narration that adds extra information to the occasion, claiming that the ruling is actually contingent on that added element, not on the general cause the claimant assumed. The example involves a Hanbali using a hadith about a woman from Banu Makhzum who borrowed goods and denied them, leading to amputation. The Hanbali argues that amputation is obligatory for a borrower who denies the borrowed item. The Maliki objects by pointing out that in the fuller report, the woman also stole (saraqat), and the amputation was for theft, not for borrowing and denial. The mention of borrowing and denial was only to identify the woman. This addition (stealing) changes the cause. The response (jawab) for the claimant is to argue that both causes (borrowing-and-denial and stealing) were transmitted together with the ruling, and the apparent (zahir) is that the ruling is attached to one of them (presumably the borrowing-and-denial) not the other. This is a dialectical move to defend the original interpretation. The passage illustrates how opponents use additional textual details to narrow the scope of a ruling, and how claimants respond by arguing that the added element does not negate their original inference.

Key Terms
الزيادة على السبب (al-ziyada 'ala al-sabab): An objection where the opponent brings a narration that adds an extra element to the reported occasion of a ruling.
تخصيص الحكم بالزيادة (takhsis al-hukm bi-al-ziyada): Claiming that the ruling is specific to the added element in the occasion, not to the original cause.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example of the woman from Banu Makhzum, what does the Maliki claim is the actual cause for amputation?
A) Borrowing and denial.
B) The woman's identity.
C) Theft (sariqa).
D) The command of the Prophet.

Key Takeaway
The 'adding to the occasion' objection forces the claimant to defend why the added element does not change the apparent cause of the ruling.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Third Dialectical Objection: Shifting the Cause with Evidence
Defines the third objection where the opponent shifts the cause to an un-transmitted one using evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and analyze the 'shifting from transmitted cause to un-transmitted cause' objection in jadal and formulate a response that defends the apparent causal link.

Core Idea
The opponent accepts the hadith but breaks the causal link using separate evidence.

This passage introduces a sophisticated dialectical move in usul al-fiqh debates: the opponent does not deny the transmitted cause but shifts to an un-transmitted one by providing independent evidence. Understanding this strategy sharpens your ability to follow juristic argumentation and respond effectively. The example involves cupping and fasting, a classic debate between Hanbalis and Malikis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claimant's transmitted cause
In the example, the Hanbali uses the hadith 'The cupper and the one being cupped have broken their fast' to claim that cupping is the cause of breaking the fast. This is the transmitted cause (sabab manqul).
The claimant relies on the apparent meaning (zahir) that the ruling is attached to the description (cupping).
2
Recognize the opponent's shift
The Maliki accepts the hadith but argues that the actual cause of breaking the fast may be something else, not cupping itself. He shifts the cause to an un-transmitted one using a separate evidence: cupping is a wound, and wounds do not break the fast.
The opponent does not reject the hadith; he reinterprets the causal link.
3
Understand the opponent's evidence
The Maliki's evidence is an analogy: cupping is a wound like other wounds, and since other wounds do not break the fast, cupping should not either. This evidence overrides the apparent causal link.
The opponent must produce a valid dalil to justify the shift.
4
Formulate the claimant's response
The claimant must attack the opponent's evidence, showing it does not prevent the original causal link from holding. The response involves discussing the evidence in a way that preserves the apparent causal connection, e.g., by pointing out the difference between cupping and other wounds or strengthening the hadith's implication.
The claimant defends the zahir by undermining the opponent's counter-evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المطلب الثالث : العدول عن السبب المنقول إلى سبب غير منقول بدليل وذلك أن يستدل المدعي بحكم للنبي صلى الله عليه وسلم في واقعة بسبب ما، فيعترض عليه السائل بأن الحكم في الواقعة لا يتعلق بذلك السبب الذي ذكره المدعي، وإنما له آخر، ويمنع تعلقها بالسبب المدعى بدليل .
ومثاله : أن يستدل الحنبلي على أن الحجامة تفطر الصائم بما روي أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم مر برجل يحجم آخر، فقال: (أفطر الحاجم والمحجوم) (3) فيقول المالكي معترضا: هذه قضية عين يحتمل أن يكون فطره بغير الحجامة، وذكره النبي له على وجه التعريف ،له وإن كان الظاهر أن تعليق الحكم على هذه الصفة يقتضي اختصاصه بها وكونه علة له إلا أنا نعدل عن هذا الظاهر بدليل، له، وذلك أن الحجامة جراحة فلم يقع بها الفطر كسائر الجراح. وطريق الجواب عن مثل هذا أن يتكلم على الدليل بما يمنع الاستدلال ليسلم له التعلق بالظاهر (4).
The third requirement: shifting from the transmitted cause to an un-transmitted cause by evidence. That the claimant argues for a ruling of the Prophet, peace be upon him, in an incident with a certain cause, so the opponent objects that the ruling in the incident does not pertain to that cause mentioned by the claimant, but rather has another cause, and he denies its connection to the claimed cause with evidence. Its example: The Hanbali argues that cupping (hijama) breaks the fast of the fasting person based on what was narrated: The Prophet, peace be upon him, passed by a man cupping another and said: 'The cuppee and the cupper have broken their fast.' (3) So the Maliki says, objecting: This is a particular incident; it is possible that his breaking of the fast was due to something other than cupping, and the Prophet mentioned it as a way of identification. Although the apparent is that attaching the ruling to this description implies its exclusivity and that it is a cause for it, we still shift from this apparent meaning due to evidence: that cupping is a wound, and breaking of the fast does not occur through it just like other wounds. The way to answer such an objection is to discuss the evidence in a manner that prevents the deduction (from that evidence), so that the attachment to the apparent is preserved (4).
Source pages p. 398

Commentary
This passage defines the third type of objection: 'shifting from the transmitted cause to an un-transmitted cause by evidence' (al-'udul 'an al-sabab al-manqul ila sabab ghayr manqul bi-dalil). Here, the claimant uses a hadith that explicitly links a ruling to a specific cause (sabab). The opponent does not deny the hadith but argues that the ruling is not actually connected to that cause; rather, there is another cause that is not mentioned in the transmitted report, and the opponent produces a separate evidence (dalil) to support this shift. The example involves a Hanbali claiming that cupping (hijama) nullifies the fast, citing the hadith: 'The cupper and the one being cupped have broken their fast.' The Maliki objects by suggesting that the Prophet's statement might have been merely identifying the individuals (تعريف), and the actual cause of breaking the fast could be something else (like the cupper swallowing blood, or other factors). Although the apparent (zahir) of the wording is that cupping is the cause, the Maliki shifts from this apparent meaning based on another evidence: cupping is a form of wound (jaraha), and wounds do not break the fast by consensus on other wounds. This evidence overrides the apparent. The response (jawab) for the claimant is to attack that evidence (al-dalil) itself, showing that it does not prevent the deduction from the apparent causal link. The claimant must therefore defend the apparent causal connection by refuting the opponent's counter-evidence. This passage illustrates a deeper level of dialectical engagement where the opponent introduces an independent argument to break the causal link, and the claimant responds by undermining that argument.

Key Terms
العدول عن السبب المنقول
الظاهر

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) The opponent questions the chain of transmission.
D) The opponent claims the ruling is abrogated.

Key Takeaway
This objection shows that in jadal, accepting the transmitted text is not enough; the causal link can be challenged by independent evidence, requiring a response that defends the apparent causal connection.
