The Fourth Condition: Transmitted Cause Must Have an Effect in the Ruling
Author states the fourth condition and provides a jadal example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a transmitted cause is incomplete due to an omitted transmitted element (ziyada manqula) in a jadal argument.

Core Idea
A transmitted cause must include its full transmitted effect; omitting part invalidates the argument.

In dialectical reasoning (jadal), a claimant may cite a transmitted cause (sabab manqul) from a prophetic incident. However, if the opponent can point to an additional transmitted element (ziyada manqula) that the claimant omitted, the cause is incomplete. This lesson examines that condition through a classical debate between al-Shafi‘i and a Maliki opponent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Claimant's Argument
Find al-Shafi'i's reasoning: He cites a hadith about a muhrim who died and concludes that all deceased muhrims should not be perfumed or covered. This is his transmitted cause.
The ruling is based on a specific incident, but the claimant uses it generally.
2
Find the Objection (Mu'tarid)
The Maliki objects by pointing out the complete hadith includes 'he will be raised on the Day of Resurrection talbiyah.' This extra part is the ziyada manqula that the claimant omitted.
The objector's move is to show the cause is incomplete.
3
Understand the Logical Move
The Maliki argues that the ruling is attached to the entire cause. Since the added element (being raised talbiyah) is part of the cause, and we cannot know who will be raised talbiyah, the ruling cannot be generalized. The claimant's omission makes his argument invalid.
The key is that the transmitted cause must include all its parts from the source.
4
Evaluate Who Wins
By jadal rules, the objector succeeds because the claimant omitted part of the transmitted cause. The condition that the transmitted cause must have an effect (and be complete) is violated.
The burden falls on the claimant to address the added element.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المطلب الرابع : أن يكون للسبب المنقول تأثير في الحكم يدعي فيه زيادة منقولة فقد يستدل المدعي بحكم النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم في واقعة ما مبينا تخصيصها بذلك الحكم، فيعترض عليه السائل بنقل زيادة في الحكم هي سبب ذلك التخصيص وقد أغفلها المستدل . وذلك مثل أن يستدل الشافعي على أن المحرم إذا مات لا يحنط ولا يخمر رأسه بما روي أن محرما وقصت به ناقته فقال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم: (لا تحنطوه ولا تخمروا رأسه) (1) . فيعترض المالكي على هذا الاستدلال بأن يقول : تمام الحديث فإنه يبعث يوم القيامة ملبيا) ، والحكم يتعلق بجميع هذا السبب فلو علمنا اليوم من يبعث ملبيا من المحرمين لفعلنا به ذلك، ولكن لا طريق لنا إلى معرفته (2)
The fourth requirement: that the transmitted cause has an effect on the ruling, claiming in it an added transmitted element. So the claimant may argue with the ruling of the Prophet ﷺ in a certain incident, specifying it with that ruling, and the questioner objects by transmitting an addition in the ruling that is the reason for that specification, which the arguer overlooked. For example, al-Shafi‘i argues that a muhrim when he dies should not be perfumed nor his head covered, based on what was narrated that a muhrim was thrown by his camel and the Messenger of Allah ﷺ said: 'Do not perfume him and do not cover his head' (1). So the Maliki objects to this reasoning by saying: The complete hadith is that he will be raised on the Day of Resurrection talbiyah. The ruling is attached to all of this cause. If we knew today who among the muhrims will be raised talbiyah, we would do that to him, but we have no way to know it (2).
Source pages p. 400

Commentary
This passage defines the fourth condition for a transmitted cause in jadal: the cause must have an effect in the ruling, and the claimant must not omit part of it. The author illustrates this with a debate between Shafi‘i and Maliki over a muhrim who dies. Shafi‘i cites a hadith in which the Prophet forbids perfuming and covering the head of a deceased muhrim. The Maliki objects by noting that the full hadith includes that the man will be raised talbiyah, implying that the ruling depends on that quality. Since we cannot know who will be raised talbiyah, the ruling cannot be generalized. The Shafi‘i's omission of this added element invalidates his reasoning. This shows that a transmitted cause must be considered in its entirety.

Key Terms
سبب منقول
زيادة منقولة

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) The Shafi'i's reasoning contradicts his own school.
D) The ruling applies only to the specific incident.

Key Takeaway
A transmitted cause in jadal must include its full transmitted effect; omitting a part (ziyada manqula) invalidates the argument.

Objection: The Istidlal Does Not Hold the Position
Objection from inconsistency with madhhab

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify an objection in jadal where the reasoning does not align with the claimant's own madhhab, and explain the dialectical response using the Hanafi-Shafi'i example.

Core Idea
An objection can target inconsistency between the cited evidence and the claimant's own school.

In jadal, not all objections attack the authenticity or relevance of a proof. A subtle and powerful objection is that the arguer's own madhhab does not accept the very case they cite. This passage illustrates that move with a clear example from Hanafi and Shafi'i/Hanbali debate on qisas. Understanding this objection helps you see how dialectical pressure forces consistency within a school's own principles.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claimant's istidlal
Find the evidence the Hanafi argues from: the Prophet killed a Muslim for a kafir and said 'I am most entitled to fulfill his covenant'. What principle is the Hanafi trying to establish?
2
Recognize the objector's challenge
The Shafi'i/Hanbali points out that the kafir was a messenger (musta'min), and the Hanafi school does not permit killing a Muslim for a messenger kafir. Therefore, the cited case does not support the Hanafi's claimed general rule.
The objection is not about the hadith's authenticity but about inconsistency with the Hanafi's own madhhab.
3
Analyse the claimant's response (jawab)
The Hanafi argues that the case shows a general rule (Muslim killed for dhimmi), but it was abrogated for messengers, leaving the dhimmi rule intact. This distinguishes the specific case from the general principle.
The jawab uses abrogation (naskh) to reconcile the apparent inconsistency.
4
Assess the dialectical outcome
The Hanafi's response attempts to show that the evidence is not contradictory to his school when properly interpreted. The objection is addressed by explaining that the case is an exception, but the general rule remains valid for dhimmis.
In jadal, the outcome depends on whether the opponent accepts the abrogation claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المطلب الأول : الاعتراض على الاستدلال بالفعل من جهة أن المستدل لا يقول به ومثاله : أن يستدل الحنفي في قتل المسلم بالكافر بأن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قتل مسلما بكافر، وقال: (أنا أحق من وفى بذمته)(1): فيقول له الشافعي أو الحنبلي معترضًا : هذا لا نقول به، فإن الذي قتله به كان مستأمنا لأنه كان رسولا، وعند أبي حنيفة لا يقتل المسلم بالرسول الكافر، وجواب الحنفي أن يقول : إنه لما قتل المسلم بالرسول دل على أنه بالذمي أولى أن يُقْتَل ثم نُسخ ذلك في الرسول وبقي في الذمي على مقتضاه (2)
The first requirement: objection to reasoning from an action from the angle that the arguer does not say it. Example: the Hanafi argues in the case of killing a Muslim for a kafir that the Prophet ﷺ killed a Muslim for a kafir and said: 'I am the most entitled to fulfill his covenant' (1). So the Shafi‘i or Hanbali objects to him: We do not say this, because the one he killed for was a musta‘min since he was a messenger, and according to Abu Hanifa, a Muslim is not killed for a kafir messenger. The Hanafi's answer is to say: When he killed the Muslim for the messenger, it indicates that the dhimmi is more deserving of being killed. Then that was abrogated regarding the messenger and remained regarding the dhimmi according to its implication (2).
Source pages p. 401

Commentary
This passage presents a type of objection in jadal: the claim that the reasoning (istidlal) does not align with the claimant's own legal school. The author provides an example: the Hanafi argues that the Prophet killed a Muslim for a kafir, implying that a Muslim can be killed for a kafir. The Shafi‘i or Hanbali objects by noting that the kafir in question was a messenger (musta’min), and in the Hanafi school, a Muslim is not killed for a messenger kafir. Thus, the very case cited does not conform to the Hanafi's claimed principle. The Hanafi responds by arguing that the case shows a general rule that a Muslim is killed for a dhimmi, but it was later abrogated for messengers, leaving the dhimmi rule intact. This illustrates the dialectical move where the objection targets inconsistency between the evidence and the claimant's own school.

Key Terms
اعتراض
استدلال

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) The Shafi'i does not accept the hadith.
D) The ruling was abrogated.

Key Takeaway
In jadal, an effective objection may target the coherence between the evidence and the claimant's own legal doctrine, forcing the claimant to resolve the apparent contradiction.

Objection That the Proponent Does Not Act on the Evidence
Author explains the first type of objection to using prophetic actions as evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and respond to the objection that the proponent does not act on the implication of his own evidence in a jadal debate.

Core Idea
Consistency is key: a debater cannot cite a prophetic action while rejecting its implication.

In jadal, when a proponent uses a prophetic action (fi'l) as evidence, the opponent may object that the proponent himself does not uphold the implication of that action. This lesson unpacks this objection through two concrete fiqh examples. You will learn how to recognize this type of objection and how to formulate a valid response by distinguishing between abrogated and remaining implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection's structure
The opponent points out that the proponent's own legal position contradicts the implication of the prophetic action he cites. Find where the opponent says 'you do not say/act on it' in the examples.
The objection attacks the proponent's consistency, not the authenticity of the action.
2
Analyze the proponent's response
The proponent typically admits the contradiction but argues that the prophetic action implies multiple rulings, some of which are abrogated. Trace how the Hanafi and Zahiri each separate the abrogated part from the remaining part.
The response preserves the evidence by isolating the non-abrogated implication.
3
Apply to a new scenario
Imagine a debater uses a hadith that the Prophet prayed two rak'ahs during a journey of one mile, but his own school only allows shortening for journeys over two miles. Formulate the objection and a valid response.
The response should distinguish between distance and occasion, as in the Zahiri example.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المطلب الأول : الاعتراض على الاستدلال بالفعل من جهة أن المستدل لا يقول به
ومثاله : أن يستدل الحنفي في قتل المسلم بالكافر بأن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قتل مسلما بكافر، وقال: (أنا أحق من وفى بذمته)(1): فيقول له الشافعي أو الحنبلي معترضًا : هذا لا نقول به، فإن الذي قتله به كان مستأمنا لأنه كان رسولا، وعند أبي حنيفة لا يقتل المسلم بالرسول الكافر، وجواب الحنفي أن يقول : إنه لما قتل المسلم بالرسول دل على أنه بالذمي أولى أن يُقْتَل ثم نُسخ ذلك في الرسول وبقي في الذمي على مقتضاه (2) ومثاله كذلك : أن يستدل الظاهري على جواز التقصير في ثلاثة أميال بما روي عن النبي ﷺ أنه صلى العصر بذي الحليفة ركعتين (3) فَيُعْتَرضُ عليه بأن يقال له : أنت لا تقول بمقتضى الحديث، لأن عندك لا يجوز القصر إلا في غزو أو حج أو عمرة، وليس في السفر إلى ذي الحليفة شيء ذلك، فكيف يستقيم لك الاستدلال بهذا الحديث؟! وطريق الظاهري في الجواب عن هذا الاعتراض أن يقول : إن هذا الحديث يقتضي أمرين أحدهما جواز القصر في مثل هذه المسافة، والثاني جواز القصر في غير حج أو عمرة، ثم دل الدليل على إبطال الثاني وبقي الأول على أصله في الجواز (1)
First request: Objection against using the action as evidence from the aspect that the proponent does not act on it. Example: The Hanafi argues for killing a Muslim in retaliation for a disbeliever by [saying] that the Prophet killed a Muslim for a disbeliever and said: 'I am most entitled to fulfill his covenant.' So the Shafi'i or Hanbali says to him in objection: 'We do not act on this, for the one he killed for was a musta'min (protected) because he was a messenger, and according to Abu Hanifa, a Muslim is not killed for a disbeliever messenger.' The Hanafi's response is to say: 'When he killed the Muslim for the messenger, it indicates that he is more deserving of being killed for the dhimmi; then this was abrogated regarding the messenger, and it remained regarding the dhimmi according to its implication.' Another example: The Zahiri argues for the permissibility of shortening prayer in three miles based on the report that the Prophet prayed Asr at Dhu al-Hulayfa two rak'ahs. He is objected to by saying: 'You do not act on the implication of the hadith, because according to you shortening is only allowed in battle, Hajj, or Umra, and the journey to Dhu al-Hulayfa is none of these, so how can you argue with this hadith?' The Zahiri's way in answering this objection is to say: 'This hadith implies two things: one is the permissibility of shortening over such a distance, the second is the permissibility of shortening without Hajj or Umra; then evidence indicated the nullification of the second, and the first remains on its original permissibility.'
Source pages p. 401

Commentary
This passage defines and illustrates the first type of objection in dialectical argumentation (jadal) concerning the use of prophetic actions as legal evidence. The objection is that the proponent himself does not consistently uphold the implication of the action he cites. Two examples are given. First, the Hanafi argues that a Muslim may be killed in retaliation for a disbeliever because the Prophet killed a Muslim for a disbeliever messenger. The opponent (Shafi'i or Hanbali) objects that the Hanafi himself does not accept that a Muslim is killed for a messenger (since a messenger is a musta'min, not a dhimmi). The Hanafi responds by distinguishing between the messenger (where the ruling was abrogated) and the dhimmi (where it remains). Second, the Zahiri argues for shortening prayer over three miles using the report that the Prophet shortened at Dhu al-Hulayfa (which is about three miles). The opponent objects that the Zahiri only allows shortening for war, Hajj, or Umra, not for a mere journey. The Zahiri responds by saying the hadith implies two permissibilities: the distance and the occasion; the latter is abrogated by other evidence, but the distance remains. The commentary here follows the author's sequential exposition: first objecting that the proponent does not practice what he preaches, then providing two concrete examples from fiqh debates, and finally showing the method of answering such objections.

المستدل
اعتراض
مقتضى الحديث

Objection by Disputing the Implication of a Prophetic Action
Author explains the second type of objection: disputing the implication of the prophetic action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and analyze the jadal objection type 'disputing the implication' (al-munaza'a fi muqtadahu) by reconstructing the Hanafi-Shafi'i exchange on wudu.

Core Idea
In jadal, you can object to a prophetic action by disputing its linguistic meaning, not just its authenticity.

When a prophetic action is cited as evidence, an opponent may dispute not the action itself but its implication—what it is taken to prove. The classic example involves the Hanafi objection to the Shafi'i use of the wudu hadith. Let's walk through how this objection works and how it is answered according to the rules of jadal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Shafi'i claim
The Shafi'i argues that the Prophet performed wudu three times, implying that wiping the head is repeated three times (as part of wudu). This is based on a hadith where the Prophet says 'whoever performs wudu like this wudu of mine...'.
2
Identify the Hanafi objection
The Hanafi objects: The word 'wudu' in the narration linguistically means only washing (cleanliness). Therefore, the hadith only proves that washing was done three times, not wiping. He disputes the implication that wiping is included.
This is a munaza'a fi muqtadahu: disputing what the action implies.
3
Reconstruct the two possible answers (jawab)
The Shafi'i can answer in two ways: (1) Show that in Shari'a custom ('urf al-shar'), wudu includes both washing and wiping, so the term must be interpreted according to Shari'a usage. (2) Provide contextual evidence (e.g., the phrase 'once once' for the first wudu) that indicates wiping was also performed.
The answer does not deny the action but clarifies its meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المطلب الثاني : الاعتراض على الاستدلال بالفعل من جهة المنازعة في مقتضاه
وهذا النوع من الاعتراض يتوجه على الاستدلال بفعل النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم من طريقين : أن ينازعه في ما فعل: وذلك مثل أن يستدل الشافعي في تكرار مسح الرأس في الوضوء بما روي أن النبي ﷺ توضأ ثلاثا ثلاثا وقال : (من توضأ نحو هذا وضوئي ثم صلى ركعتين لا يحدث بهما نفسه غفر له ما تقدم من ذنبه)(2). فيقول الحنفي معترضا قول الراوي : توضأ ثلاثا»، معناه : غسل، لأن الوضوء في اللغة هو النظافة وما تحصل به الوضاءة وذلك إنما يحصل بالغسل دون المسح. والجواب عن هذا الاعتراض يكون من وجهين : أحدهما : أن يبين المستدل أن الوضوء في عرف الشرع هو الغسل والمسح جميعًا، وفي اللغة عبارة عن الغسل، فوجب حمله على عرف الشرع. والثاني : أن يبين بالدليل من جهة السياق أو غيره أن المراد به الغسل والمسح، مثل أن يقول الشافعي في جوابه لما كان قوله في الأولى : توضأ مرة مرة، رجع
Second request: Objection against using the action as evidence from the aspect of disputing its implication. This type of objection is directed against using the Prophet's action as evidence in two ways: one is that he disputes what was done. For example, the Shafi'i argues for repeating the wiping of the head in wudu based on the report that the Prophet performed wudu three times three times and said: 'Whoever performs wudu like this wudu of mine and then prays two rak'ahs without speaking to himself, his previous sins will be forgiven.' So the Hanafi says in objection: 'The statement of the narrator "he performed wudu three times" means washing, because wudu in language is cleanliness and what brings about brightness, and that is only achieved by washing, not by wiping.' The answer to this objection has two aspects: One: The proponent clarifies that wudu in the custom of Shari'a is both washing and wiping together, while in language it is used for washing, so it must be carried upon the custom of Shari'a. Two: He clarifies by evidence from the context or otherwise that what is meant is washing and wiping, such as when the Shafi'i says in his answer: 'Since his statement in the first: he performed wudu once once, he returned...'
Source pages p. 402

Commentary
This passage introduces the second type of objection in jadal concerning prophetic actions: disputing the implication (muqtada) of the action. It explains that the objection can be raised in two ways, but the text only details one: disputing what was actually done. The example involves the Shafi'i argument for repeating the wiping of the head in wudu based on the hadith that the Prophet performed wudu three times (implying each limb three times, including wiping). The Hanafi objects that the Arabic word 'wudu' in the hadith means only washing, not wiping, because its linguistic root denotes cleanliness through washing. The Shafi'i must answer by either showing that in Shari'a usage 'wudu' includes both washing and wiping (thus the term should be interpreted according to the Shari'a convention), or by providing contextual evidence that wiping was intended (e.g., the phrase 'once once' for the first wudu suggests a different pattern that includes wiping). The author presents this as a typical dialectical exchange: objection (naqd) and answer (jawab).

Key Terms
المنازعة في مقتضاه
الوضوء
عرف الشرع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi objection, why does the phrase 'wudu three times' not support the Shafi'i's view on wiping?
A) Because the hadith is weak.
B) Because 'wudu' in language means washing only.
C) Because the Prophet did not wipe his head.
D) Because the narration says 'once once'.

Key Takeaway
The objection of disputing the implication challenges the meaning of the prophetic action, not its occurrence, and is answered by clarifying the Shari'a usage or contextual evidence.

Contesting the Act: The Hanafi-Shafi'i Debate on Wiping the Head
First example of contesting the act itself: the Hanafi-Shafi'i debate on wiping the head.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and evaluate objections that contest the linguistic interpretation of a prophetic act in a dialectical debate.

Core Idea
Contesting the meaning of a key term can undermine an entire argument from the Prophet's action.

In the first example, the Hanafi objects to the Shafi'i's argument for repeating wiping by contesting the meaning of 'wudu' in the hadith. This lesson walks through the objection and the Shafi'i's response, showing how linguistic and contextual analysis are used in munazara.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Shafi'i's argument from the act
Find the reported prophetic action (wudu three times) and the legal claim (repeating wiping). The Shafi'i uses the hadith to prove that wiping is repeated because the Prophet's wudu included all limbs three times.
2
Locate the Hanafi's objection
The Hanafi accepts the hadith but contests the meaning of 'wudu', arguing that it linguistically means only washing, not wiping. This is an objection to the act itself because it denies that wiping occurred.
The objection is about the factual nature of the act, not its implication.
3
Analyze the basis of the objection
The Hanafi relies on the linguistic definition of 'wudu' as 'cleanliness through washing'. He assumes the hadith uses the linguistic sense, thus excluding wiping.
This is a common move: using language to narrow the scope of a reported act.
4
Examine the Shafi'i's response
The Shafi'i responds with two arguments: (1) the term 'wudu' in Sharia convention includes both washing and wiping, so it must be interpreted according to Sharia, not language; (2) contextual evidence: the first 'wudu once' included wiping, so the later 'wudu twice' also includes it.
The response shows how to defend interpretation by appealing to technical convention and internal coherence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المطلب الثاني : الاعتراض على الاستدلال بالفعل من جهة المنازعة في مقتضاه
وهذا النوع من الاعتراض يتوجه على الاستدلال بفعل النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم من طريقين : أن ينازعه في ما فعل: وذلك مثل أن يستدل الشافعي في تكرار مسح الرأس في الوضوء بما روي أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم توضأ ثلاثا ثلاثا وقال : (من توضأ نحو هذا وضوئي ثم صلى ركعتين لا يحدث بهما نفسه غفر له ما تقدم من ذنبه). فيقول الحنفي معترضا قول الراوي : توضأ ثلاثا»، معناه : غسل، لأن الوضوء في اللغة هو النظافة وما تحصل به الوضاءة وذلك إنما يحصل بالغسل دون المسح. والجواب عن هذا الاعتراض يكون من وجهين : أحدهما : أن يبين المستدل أن الوضوء في عرف الشرع هو الغسل والمسح جميعًا، وفي اللغة عبارة عن الغسل، فوجب حمله على عرف الشرع. والثاني : أن يبين بالدليل من جهة السياق أو غيره أن المراد به الغسل والمسح، مثل أن يقول الشافعي في جوابه لما كان قوله في الأولى : توضأ مرة مرة، رجع إلى مسح الرأس مع غسل الأعضاء، وجب غسل الأعضاء، وجب أن يكون قوله : ثم توضأ مرتين مرتين، راجعا إلى جميع الأعضاء دون أن يخرج الرأس من الثانية والثالثة، وقد انتظمها الحديث (1)

ومثاله كذلك : أن يستدل المالكي على أن الموالاة شرط في صحة الوضوء، بما روي عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أنه توضأ مرة وقال: (هذا وضوء من لا يقبل الله منه صلاة إلا به) ، ولا يجوز أن يكون وضؤوه ذلك متفرقا لإجماع الأمة على أن الموالاة أفضل فلم يبق إلا أنه والى الطهارة. فيعترض المخالف بأن يقول : إنما كان قوله ذلك راجعا إلى عدد الأفعال دون موالاتها وسائر هيئاتها والدليل على ذلك أنه علق الحكم على مرة، ولو أراد به الموالاة لعلق الحكم عليه .
وجواب المالكي عن هذا الاعتراض أن يقول : أن الحكم إنما علقه النبي بالوضوء فقال : (هذا) وضوء لا يقبل الله الصلاة إلا به، وهو يشتمل على عدد الأفعال وصفاتها من الموالاة وغيرها، فيجب أن يحمل على جميعها إلا أن يخص الدليل بعضها (3)
The second topic: Objection to argument from the act concerning disputing its import. This type of objection is directed at argument from the action of the Prophet, peace be upon him, in two ways: disputing what he did. For example, the Shafi'i argues for repeating the wiping of the head in wudu by what was narrated that the Prophet performed wudu three times each and said: 'Whoever performs wudu like this wudu of mine then prays two rak'ahs without speaking to himself about them, his previous sins are forgiven.' The Hanafi objects saying: The narrator's statement 'he performed wudu three times' means washing, because wudu in language is cleanliness and what produces brightness, and that is obtained only by washing, not by wiping. The answer to this objection is from two aspects: First, the arguer shows that wudu in the Sharia convention is both washing and wiping, while in language it expresses washing, so it must be interpreted according to the Sharia convention. Second, he shows by evidence from context or otherwise that what is meant is washing and wiping. For example, the Shafi'i says in his answer: Since his statement in the first: 'he performed wudu once', included wiping of the head along with washing of the limbs, it is necessary that his statement 'then he performed wudu twice' refers to all limbs without excluding the head from the second and third, and the hadith encompassed them. Another example: The Maliki argues that continuity is a condition for the validity of wudu, based on what was narrated that the Prophet performed wudu once and said: 'This is a wudu without which Allah does not accept prayer.' It is not possible that his wudu was discontinuous because of the consensus of the Ummah that continuity is better; so nothing remains except that he observed continuity in purification. The opponent objects by saying: His statement referred only to the number of acts, not their continuity and other forms. The evidence for that is that he tied the ruling to 'once', and if he meant continuity he would have tied the ruling to it. The Maliki's answer to this objection is to say: The Prophet indeed tied the ruling to wudu and said: 'This is a wudu without which Allah does not accept prayer.' It includes the number of acts and their attributes including continuity and others, so it must be interpreted as referring to all of them unless evidence specifies some.
Source pages p. 402

Commentary
This passage introduces the second topic (المطلب الثاني) concerning objections to arguments based on the Prophet's action (الاستدلال بالفعل). The author specifies two methods of objection: first, contesting the act itself (المنازعة في ما فعل), and second, accepting the act but contesting its implication (which appears in the next lesson). The first method is illustrated with two examples from usul al-fiqh debates between madhhabs. The first example involves the Shafi'i arguing for repeating the wiping of the head in wudu based on a hadith that the Prophet performed wudu three times each. The Hanafi objects by claiming that the word 'wudu' in the hadith means only washing, not wiping, because the linguistic meaning of wudu is cleanliness through washing. The Shafi'i responds in two ways: (1) the term 'wudu' in the Sharia convention includes both washing and wiping, so it must be understood according to the Sharia, not language; (2) by contextual evidence, since the first wudu (once) included wiping the head along with washing limbs, the subsequent wudu (twice) must also include the head. The second example is the Maliki arguing that continuity (الموالاة) is a condition for wudu based on a hadith where the Prophet performed wudu once and said this wudu is obligatory. The opponent objects that the wording 'once' only specifies the number of acts, not continuity. The Maliki answers that the Prophet tied the ruling to the entire wudu, which includes all its attributes, so continuity is included unless evidence excludes it. The passage demonstrates the dialectical method of contesting the factual basis of an opponent's evidence.

Key Terms
الاستدلال بالفعل
المنازعة في مقتضاه
الاعتراض
المخالف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the first example, what was the basis of the Hanafi's objection to the Shafi'i's argument for repeating wiping?
A) The hadith is weak in transmission.
B) The word 'wudu' in the hadith means only washing, not wiping, according to language.
C) The Prophet never wiped his head.
D) The Hanafi denied the consensus on the obligation of wiping.

Key Takeaway
Contesting the linguistic meaning of a key term in a prophetic act is a valid dialectical move that must be countered by appealing to technical convention or contextual evidence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Write a short dialectical exchange where a Hanafi objects to a Shafi'i argument using the linguistic interpretation of 'wudu', and the Shafi'i responds using one of the two methods given in the passage.

Contesting the Act: The Maliki Debate on Continuity in Wudu
Second example: the Maliki debate on continuity (muwalat) in wudu.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between contesting the act itself and contesting its implication, and evaluate the strength of objections based on narrow interpretation of wording.

Core Idea
An opponent may accept the act but interpret its scope narrowly; the response requires showing that the act's attributes include the disputed element.

The second example involves the Maliki argument that continuity (al-muwalah) is a condition for wudu based on the Prophet performing wudu once. The opponent argues that the wording only indicates the number of acts, not continuity. This lesson analyzes the exchange and how the Maliki defends the inclusion of all attributes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Maliki's argument from the act
The Maliki uses the hadith that the Prophet performed wudu once and said 'This is a wudu without which Allah does not accept prayer.' He argues that this shows continuity is a condition because the Prophet's wudu must have been continuous, since the Ummah agrees continuity is better.
The argument infers continuity from the fact that the Prophet's wudu was exemplary.
2
Locate the opponent's objection
The opponent agrees the Prophet performed wudu once but contends that the wording 'once' only specifies the number of actions (e.g., washing each limb once), not the attribute of continuity.
This objection contests the scope of what the act entails, not the occurrence of the act itself.
3
Analyze the basis of the objection
The opponent argues that because the ruling is tied to the word 'once' (referring to number), continuity is not indicated. He assumes the Prophet's statement only covers the explicit mention, not implicit attributes.
The objection uses a narrow reading of the text, focusing on the explicit wording.
4
Examine the Maliki's response
The Maliki responds that the Prophet tied the ruling to 'wudu' as a whole, not just the number of acts. The word 'this' points to the entire wudu, which includes all its attributes (number, continuity, etc.). Unless there is evidence to exclude continuity, it must be included.
The response relies on the principle that when the ruling is attached to a concept, it applies to all its inherent attributes unless specified otherwise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Second Type of Objection in Jadal: Contesting the Implication of the Act
Author presents the second type of objection with example and two answers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and formulate the second type of objection in jadal (accepting the act but disputing its implication) and apply the standard responses in a usul al-fiqh debate.

Core Idea
Conceding the act does not end the debate—the implication can still be contested.

In usul al-fiqh debates, one may accept that the Prophet performed an action but dispute what legal ruling it entails. This passage presents exactly that move, with a concrete example from the obligation of being upright in bowing and prostration, along with two rejoinders. Mastering this distinction is essential for constructing and responding to arguments from prophetic action.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection type
Recognize that the opponent accepts the occurrence of the act (al-taslīm bi al-fi‘l) but challenges the ruling derived from it (al-munāza‘a fī al-muqtaḍā).
This is different from denying the act itself.
2
Extract the example
The Shafi'i or Hanbali argues for wujūb (obligation) of being upright in prayer based on the Prophet's action. The opponent objects that the action does not necessitate wujūb—it could indicate recommendation or permissibility.
The example is from fiqh al-‘ibādāt.
3
Analyze the first answer
The arguer asserts that according to his own uṣūl, the Prophet's action does imply wujūb. This is a direct claim based on his methodological principles.
This answer depends on the arguer's school-specific uṣūl.
4
Analyze the second answer
The arguer reinterprets the action as a bayān (explanation) of a mujmal (ambiguous) wājib in the Qur'an. Since explaining an obligatory matter is itself obligatory, the action carries wujūb.
This answer transforms the action from independent proof to contextual explanation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
2 - التسليم بالاستدلال بالفعل مع المنازعة في مقتضاه : وذلك مثل أن يستدل الشافعي أو الحنبلي في وجوب الاعتدال في الركوع والسجود بأن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم فعل ذلك . فيعترض المخالف بأن فعل النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم لا يقتضي الوجوب . والجواب عنه من ثلاثة أوجه: أولها : أن يقول المستدل : فعل النبي الله عندي يقتضي الوجوب . والثاني : أن يقول : هذا بيان لمجمل واجب في القرآن، وبيان الواجب واجب .
2 - Accepting the argument from the act while disputing its implication: That is like when the Shafi'i or Hanbali argues for the obligation of being upright in bowing and prostration by the fact that the Prophet, peace be upon him, did that. The opponent objects that the Prophet's action does not entail obligation. The answer to it is from three aspects: First, the arguer says: The Prophet's action in my view entails obligation. Second, he says: This is an explanation of an obligatory ambiguous expression in the Qur'an, and explaining an obligatory matter is obligatory.
Source pages p. 403

Commentary
This passage presents the second type of objection to arguments based on the Prophet's action: accepting the act (التسليم بالفعل) but contesting its legal implication (المنازعة في مقتضاه). The example given is a debate about the obligation of being upright (الاعتدال) in bowing and prostration. The Shafi'i or Hanbali argues that the Prophet's performance of this action proves it is obligatory. The opponent objects that the Prophet's action does not necessarily indicate obligation; it could indicate recommendation or permissibility. The author provides three possible answers: (1) The arguer can assert that according to his own usul, the Prophet's action does indicate obligation. (2) The action can be considered an explanation (بيان) of a general obligation in the Qur'an, and explaining an obligatory matter is itself obligatory, so the action is obligatory. The third answer is not fully given in the text (it stops abruptly), but the context suggests it would be another argument. This passage illustrates a common dialectical move in usul al-fiqh where the legal value of the Prophet's action is contested.

Key Terms

المنازعة في المقتضى
الوجوب
البيان

Checkpoint
What is the main claim of the opponent when contesting the implication of the Prophet's action in the example given?
A) The Prophet never performed that action.
B) The action is not obligatory but only recommended or permissible.
C) The action was abrogated.
D) The action applies only to the Prophet.

Key Takeaway
Contesting the implication of an act (al-munāza‘a fī al-muqtaḍā) is a standard dialectical move in jadal, and the arguer can respond by either asserting the ruling directly from the act or reinterpreting it as an explanation of a general obligation.

Dialectic -- Jadal
intermediate

Accepting Prophetic Action as Evidence While Disputing Its Implication
Author explains jadal technique of conceding evidence but disputing implication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the jadal technique of taslim bi al-istidlal bi al-fi'l ma'a al-munaza'a fi muqtadah and apply the three standard responses used to defend obligation from a prophetic action.

Core Idea
Accepting evidence does not mean accepting the conclusion—the implication can be challenged.

In jadal, both sides may agree that a prophetic action is valid evidence (taslim bi al-istidlal), but disagree on its legal implication (muqtada). This passage teaches you how to dispute the implication while conceding the evidence. We will explore the three classic responses used by Shafi'i/Hanbali and Maliki jurists.

Guided Path
1
Identify the technique
Recognize that both sides accept the prophetic action as valid evidence, but one side disputes whether it indicates obligation (wujub) or recommendation (istihbab).
This is a dialectical move, not a logical fallacy.
2
Analyze the first example
Shafi'i/Hanbali argue that i'tidal in prayer is obligatory because the Prophet did it. The opponent objects that the action does not entail obligation.
The objection is to the implication, not the evidence.
3
Learn the three responses
The arguer has three options: (1) assert that in his madhhab prophetic acts indicate obligation, (2) claim the act explains an obligatory general (bayan al-mujmal), (3) note that the act was accompanied by an explicit command (amr).
Each response has different jurisprudential grounds.
4
Apply to the second example
Maliki argues wiping the entire head is obligatory based on the Prophet's action. Hanafi objects that it only indicates recommendation. Maliki responds using bayan al-mujmal and the Qur'anic verse 'follow him'.
The same technique is used across madhhabs.
5
Distinguish evidence from implication
Conceding the evidence (taslim) does not mean conceding the conclusion (muqtada). This technique allows precise challenge in debate.
It is a key skill in munazara.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
2 - التسليم بالاستدلال بالفعل مع المنازعة في مقتضاه : وذلك مثل أن يستدل الشافعي أو الحنبلي في وجوب الاعتدال في الركوع والسجود بأن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم فعل ذلك .
فيعترض المخالف بأن فعل النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم لا يقتضي الوجوب .
والجواب عنه من ثلاثة أوجه:
أولها: أن يقول المستدل : فعل النبي الله عندي يقتضي الوجوب .
والثاني : أن يقول : هذا بيان لمجمل واجب في القرآن، وبيان الواجب واجب .
والثالث : أن يقول : قد اقترن به أمر وهو قوله عليه الصلاة والسلام (صلوا كما رأيتموني أصلي)، والأمر يقتضي الوجوب، ومثاله كذلك : استدلال المالكي في وجوب استيعاب الرأس في الوضوء بما روي عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أنه توضأ فمسح رأسه بيديه فأقبل بهما وأدبر ، بدأ بمقدم رأسه حتى ذهب بهما إلى قفاه ثم ردهما إلى المكان الذي بدأ منه، وأفعاله تقتضي الوجوب فيعترضه الحنفي بأن يقول : هذا لا يصح الاحتجاج به، لأن أفعال النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم نسلم أنها تقتضي الوجوب، وإنما تقتضي الاستحباب ومسح كل الرأس عندنا مستحب وليس بواجب.
والجواب أن يقول المالكي : إن أفعال النبي الله عندنا على الوجوب، بدليل : قوله تعالى : ﴿ ... . وَاتَّبِعُوهُ لَعَلَّكُمْ تَهْتَدُونَ ... ﴾، ولا خلاف أن أفعاله إذا كانت بيانا للمجمل الواجب إنما تكون على الوجوب وهذا بيان للمجمل من قوله : (لا تقبل صلاة بغير طهور )، وفي رواية: (لا يقبل الله صلاة بغير طهور) . . وكذلك فإنه إذا دل الدليل على أن بعض أفعاله في الوضوء على
2 - Acknowledging the evidence of the act while disputing its implication: Such as when the Shafi'i or Hanbali argues for the obligation of moderation (i'tidal) in bowing and prostrating because the Prophet, peace be upon him, did that. The opponent objects by saying that the Prophet's act does not entail obligation. The answer to this is from three aspects: First: that the arguer says: The Prophet's act, in my view, entails obligation. Second: that he says: This is an explanation of an obligatory general (mujmal) in the Qur'an, and explaining the obligatory is obligatory. Third: that he says: An command (amr) has accompanied it, namely his saying, peace be upon him, 'Pray as you have seen me pray', and a command entails obligation. And an example of this as well: the Maliki's argument for the obligation of completely covering the head in ablution based on what is narrated that the Prophet, peace be upon him, performed ablution and wiped his head with his hands, bringing them forward and backward, starting from the front of his head until he reached his nape, then returning them to the place he started from. And his acts entail obligation. The Hanafi objects by saying: This cannot be used as evidence, because we acknowledge that the Prophet's acts entail obligation? No, they only entail recommendation, and wiping the entire head is recommended in our view, not obligatory. The answer is that the Maliki says: The Prophet's acts, in our view, are upon obligation, due to His saying, the Exalted: '...and follow him that you may be guided...' And there is no dispute that if his acts are an explanation of an obligatory general (mujmal), they are upon obligation. And this is an explanation of the general from his saying: 'No prayer is accepted without purification' and in a narration: 'Allah does not accept prayer without purification.' And likewise, if evidence indicates that some of his acts in ablution are upon...
Source pages p. 403

Commentary
This passage explains the jadal technique known as 'al-taslim bi al-istidlal bi al-fi'l ma'a al-munaza'a fi muqtadah', which means accepting the evidence of a prophetic action while disputing its legal implication (in this case, whether it indicates obligation or recommendation). The author presents two examples. First, a Shafi'i or Hanbali argues that i'tidal (standing straight) in ruku' and sujud is obligatory because the Prophet did it. The opponent objects that the Prophet's act does not necessarily imply obligation—it could be recommended. The answer is given in three ways: (1) the arguer simply asserts that in his school prophetic acts indicate obligation; (2) the prophetic act is explanatory (bayan) of a general command in the Qur'an that is obligatory, and explaining an obligation is itself obligatory; (3) the act was accompanied by the explicit command 'Pray as you have seen me pray', which is an imperative (amr) indicating obligation. The second example involves the Maliki argument for wiping the entire head in wudu'. The Prophet's action of wiping his head forward and backward is taken as evidence of obligation. The Hanafi opponent concedes the action but denies obligation, claiming it only indicates recommendation. The Maliki responds by citing the Qur'anic verse 'follow him' and the principle that prophetic acts that explain an obligatory general are themselves obligatory. He further argues that the Prophet's statement 'No prayer is accepted without purification' is a general (mujmal) that his action of wiping the entire head explains, thus making it obligatory. The passage illustrates the dialectical moves in usul al-fiqh where the authority of prophetic actions is acknowledged, but the precise legal import is contested.

Key Terms
التسليم بالاستدلال بالفعل
المنازعة في مقتضاه
الوجوب
الاستحباب
بيان المجمل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A Hanafi argues that wiping the head in wudu' is recommended (mustahabb) based on the Prophet's action. The Maliki responds that the Prophet's actions indicate obligation (wujub). Which of the following is NOT one of the three answers given by the Shafi'i/Hanbali in the first example?
A) The prophetic act at 1:00 PM indicates obligation in my madhhab.
B) This act is an explanation of a general command in the Quran, and explaining the obligatory is obligatory.
C) The act is accompanied by an explicit command from the Prophet, which indicates obligation.
D) The act is a recommended (nafl) act that is not binding.

Key Takeaway
In jadal, conceding the evidence does not concede the conclusion; the implication (muqtada) can be independently challenged using three standard responses: madhhab assertion, bayan al-mujmal, and accompanying amr.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Objecting by Claiming Ambiguity (Ijmal) in Jadal
Author explains objection based on ambiguity of prophetic action with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a prophetic action is ambiguous between two interpretations and apply the jadal rule of suspension (tawaqquf) in a dialectical exchange.

Core Idea
Ambiguity suspends inference until a clear indication (qarina) appears.

This lesson introduces the third demand in the adab al-bahth tradition: the opponent's objection that a prophetic action is ambiguous (mujmal) because it could bear two meanings without a contextual clue (qarina). Through examples from funeral prayer and ritual purity, you will see how ijmal forces the inferrer to wait for clarification, preventing hasty conclusions in fiqh debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two possible interpretations
In the Shafi'i's use of the hadith where the Prophet prayed over a grave, what are the two interpretations? The woman may or may not have been prayed over before burial. The ambiguity lies in which of these two scenarios actually occurred.
The ruling differs depending on the interpretation; this is what makes the action ambiguous.
2
Locate the absence of a qarina
Look for any contextual clue in the narration that would favor one interpretation over the other. The passage states there is no qarina. For example, the hadith does not mention whether the woman had a funeral prayer before burial.
Without a qarina, both possibilities remain equally plausible.
3
Apply the rule of suspension (tawaqquf)
When ambiguity persists, the correct jadal procedure is to suspend judgment. The Maliki objects that the hadith cannot be used as proof until the ambiguity is resolved. This prevents the Shafi'i from drawing a definite inference.
Suspension is a temporary measure; the debate may continue if a qarina is produced.
4
Extend the principle to the second example
In the maniy example, Aisha rubs off semen while the Prophet is praying. The opponent might claim ambiguity: does the Prophet's continued prayer indicate that the garment is pure, or did Aisha rub it off after the prayer ended? Without a qarina, the action is ambiguous.
The text cuts off, but the same logic applies: the inferrer must suspend until clarification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المطلب الثالث : الاعتراض على الاستدلال بفعل النبي بدعوى الإجمال وذلك أن يكون الفعل الصادر عن النبي محتملا لوجهين، دون أن يكون هناك دليل يرجح أحدهما على الآخر، وليس في سياق الرواية قرينة تنبئ عن رجحان أحد الوجهين فيدعي المعترض إجماله ليمنع المستدل من الاحتجاج به . ذلك : أن يستدل الشافعي على الصلاة على القبر بعد أن صلى على الميت، بما روي عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أنه صلى على قبر امرأة سوداء كانت تقم المسجد فيعترضه المالكي قائلا : لا يصح الاحتجاج بهذا الخبر لأننا لا نمنع الصلاة على كل قبر، وإنما نمنع الصلاة على قبر من صُلّي عليه قبل دفنه ونجيز الصلاة على قبر من لم يُصَلَّ عليه ولا نعلم أن هذه المرأة صُلِّي عليها قبل الدفن، فيحتمل أن يكون قد صلي عليها ويحتمل ألا يكون صلي عليها، وإذا احتمل الأمرين وجب التوقف حتى يقع البيان. ومثاله أيضًا : أن يستدل الشافعي في طهارة المني بأن عائشة قالت : «كنت أفرك المني من ثوب رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم وهو يصلي عليه»، فلو كان
The third demand: Objecting to inference from the Prophet's action by claiming ambiguity. That is, when the action emanating from the Prophet is susceptible to two interpretations, without there being evidence that favors one over the other, and there is no contextual clue in the narration that indicates the preponderance of one of the two interpretations. So the opponent claims its ambiguity to prevent the inferrer from using it as evidence. For instance: the Shafi'i infers the permission of prayer over the grave after having prayed over the deceased, based on what is narrated from the Prophet, peace be upon him, that he prayed over the grave of a black woman who used to sweep the mosque. The Maliki objects, saying: 'This narration cannot be used as evidence because we do not forbid prayer over every grave; rather we forbid prayer over a grave of someone who has already been prayed over before burial, and we permit prayer over a grave of someone who has not been prayed over. We do not know whether this woman was prayed over before burial. So it possible that she was prayed over and possible that she was not prayed over. When it is possible for both, it is necessary to suspend until clarification occurs.' Another example: the Shafi'i infers the purity of maniy (semen) from the statement of Aisha: 'I used to rub off maniy from the garment of the Messenger of Allah while he was praying in it.' For if...
Source pages p. 405

Commentary
This passage introduces the third demand (طلب) in the Adab al-Bahth/jadal: the objection that a prophetic action is ambiguous (mujmal) when it can bear two interpretations without a clear contextual indication (qarina). The author provides two examples: First, the Shafi'i uses the hadith of the Prophet praying over a grave to argue that prayer over a grave is permissible even after burial. The Maliki objects that the hadith is ambiguous because it is unknown if the woman had already been prayed over before burial; if she had, then the Prophet's action does not support the Shafi'i's intended ruling. Therefore, the hadith cannot be used as proof until the ambiguity is resolved. Second, the Shafi'i argues for the purity of maniy based on Aisha's practice of rubbing it off while the Prophet was still praying in the garment. The implication is that if it were impure, he would not have continued praying. But the text cuts off, likely showing the opponent's objection to this inference. This passage illustrates how ambiguity (ijmal) is used as a dialectical tool to suspend inference.

Key Terms
إجمال
قرينة

Key Takeaway
When a prophetic action can bear two meanings without a contextual clue (qarina), the opponent may claim ijmal and suspend the inference until clarification is provided.
